## פתח דבר

## Introduction

As we approach the tenth day, "in the eleventh month, which is the month of Shevat", may it come upon us for good—the day that will mark forty-six years since the beginning of the Nesius of the Rebbe, Melech HaMoshiach—we are publishing this volume, in continuation of the Likkutei Sichos volumes that have appeared: Likkutei Sichos, Volume 35, on Sefer Bereishis (the first volume of the ninth book of Likkutei Sichos).

* * *

This volume includes:

a) The Likkutei Sichos on the Torah portions (and the Yomim Tovim) of the time of reading Sefer Bereishis, which were printed in individual booklets week by week during the years 5750–5752.

b) The Sicha of 4 Marcheshvan, the night preceding Friday, Parshas Noach, 5737 (printed in Likkutei Sichos for Parshas Noach, 5755).

c) Letters whose content pertains to the weekly Torah portions (and Yomim Tovim) included in this book. It is worth noting that this section for Parshas Chayei Sarah includes a collection of letters regarding the laws of comforting mourners; for 19 Kislev, regarding the study and dissemination of Pnimiyus HaTorah; and for Parshas Vayechi, regarding matters of passing, mourning, etc.

Let it be known: a large portion of the letters in this volume have been printed in the *Igros Kodesh* series from the Rebbe, Melech HaMoshiach, which is currently being published in chronological order (to date, twenty-three volumes have been published). By popular request, we have collected here those letters whose content pertains to the weekly Torah portions (and Yomim Tovim) of Sefer Bereishis.

d) For the benefit of the reader, we have added at the end of the book (as in volumes 30–34) an index of subjects, verses, statements of Chazal, and books.

* * *

May it be Hashem’s will that we soon merit to publish further Sichos and Maamarim of the Rebbe, Melech HaMoshiach. May the merit of studying and printing his holy Torah increase the strengthening of our hiskashrus with the Rebbe, Melech HaMoshiach. And may this month of Shevat fulfill that which is hinted at in the month of Shevat—the true and complete Redemption through Moshiach Tzidkeinu,

[As explained in the Sicha of the Rebbe, Melech HaMoshiach, that "the concept of the Redemption is hinted at in the name of the month—'Shevat'—which is (a) from the expression 'shevtei moshlim' (tribes of rulers), and specifically the rule of the Kingdom of the House of David, as it is written, 'The scepter (shevet) shall not depart from Yehudah (from David onward, these are the heads of the exile in Bavel who rule the people with a scepter)... until Shiloh (Moshiach) comes,' of whom it is said, 'A scepter (shevet—a king who rules and governs) shall rise from Yisrael,' 'this is Melech HaMoshiach'; (b) from the expression for a tree branch, as it is written, 'And a shoot shall come forth from the stem of Yishai, and a branch (netzer) shall grow from his roots', 'a royal scepter (shevet) shall go forth from the root of Yishai, from the seed of David—this is Melech HaMoshiach'."]

May the Rebbe, Melech HaMoshiach, arise and redeem us, and lead us upright to our Holy Land. We shall learn Torah from the mouth of Moshiach Tzidkeinu, "a new Torah shall go forth from Me", and "your eyes shall behold the King in His beauty" in the revelation of His kingdom before the eyes of all, immediately and truly, "and the kingdom shall be Hashem’s". Amen, may it be His will.

In anticipation of the dissemination of the Sichos,
3 Shevat, 5756.
Brooklyn, N.Y.


## תוכן ומפתח

## Table of Contents and Index

### Bereishis

**Sicha 1 (5750)**
The debate among the commentators as to whether Adam HaRishon named the fish as well (Radak and Chizkuni 2:19; Midrash Chaser V'Yatir); the various explanations regarding the content of the act of naming by Adam HaRishon.

**Sicha 2 (5752)**
The difference between Chanoch, son of Kayin, of whom it is said (4:17), "And (Kayin) was building a city and called the name of the city after the name of his son, Chanoch"—representing the settling of the world—and Chanoch, a descendant of Shem, of whom it is said (5:24), "And he was not, for Elokus took him"; the two eras—before the Mabul and after the Mabul—in the avodah of Bnei Yisrael, and the integration between them.

### Noach

**Sicha 1 (5751)**
"Noach was a righteous man, perfect in his generations"—"But in other generations he is not considered anything, such as the generation of Avraham... of Moshe... of David" (Zohar Vol. I 60a)—the significance of the three generations of Avraham, Moshe, and David and their connection to Noach, and an explanation in Likutei Levi Yitzchok on the Zohar there.

**Sicha 2 (5752)**
The difference between a disqualification of a *ba'al mum* (blemished animal), which is not forbidden to a *ben Noach* (Zevachim 115b), and a disqualification of *mechusar eiver* (missing a limb), which is forbidden even to a *ben Noach* (Zevachim ibid.); the view of the Rambam (Hilchos Issurei Mizbe'ach, beginning of Ch. 9) that the disqualification of *mechusar eiver* in a bird is because "one does not offer a deficient animal at all"; the inner reason why the standard example of a *ba'al mum* that is permitted to a *ben Noach* and in a bird is specifically "white spots in the eye."

**Sicha 3 (5750)**
The difference between the view of the Bereishis Rabbah (Ch. 35:2) and Rashi (9:12) regarding the generations in whose days the rainbow did not appear; how is it possible to say that only in a generation of completely righteous people is there no need for the sign of the rainbow?

### Lech Lecha

**Sicha 1 (5751)**
"Five acquisitions did the Ribono shel Olam acquire in His world... Torah... Heaven and Earth... Avraham... Yisrael... the Beis HaMikdash" (Avos Ch. 6:10)—the definition of an "acquisition" that "the Ribono shel Olam acquired in His world," and a resolution of several precision points in the language of the Beraisa.

**Sicha 2 (5750)**
The connection between the promise of the inheritance of the Land and the Bris Milah, based on what is written in Likutei Levi Yitzchok (Likutei Levi Yitzchok on Zohar Vol. II, p. 47) that through the Milah, "they shall inherit the Land, an inheritance that has no interruption"; two definitions in Bris Milah—the *bris* of Avraham Avinu, which is subject to interruption, and the *bris* of the Ribono shel Olam, which has no interruption; and their parallels in the inheritance of the Land.

**Sicha 3 (5752)**
The three sources for the teaching that Milah overrides Shabbos—from the *gezeirah shavah* "sign of the covenant and generations" (in our Parsha 17:11-12); from the verse (Tazria 12:3) "On the eighth day he shall be circumcised"; and from "And Bnei Yisrael shall keep the Shabbos" (Tisa 31:16 and in Mechilta there)—the practical difference between the three teachings in Halacha and according to Chassidus; the reason why the power of *mesiras nefesh* that exists in all of Yisrael is an inheritance from our forefathers (Tanya Ch. 18), meaning even before Matan Torah.

### Vayeira

**Sicha 1 (5751)**
A discussion regarding the fact that the obligation of *chinuch* is only *d'rabbanan*, whereas *min haTorah* there is no obligation to prepare a minor for the time of his adulthood; how can it be said that for Avraham there were "complete days" (Zohar Vol. I 129a, etc.), when he only recognized his Creator at the age of three (Nedarim 32a)?

**Sicha 2 (5752)**
The reason for the difference between Sedom and Amora, which were burned, and the four cities, which were only overturned (19:24-25 and Rashi there) according to the *peshat*; the inner explanation of what is written in the prophecy of Yechezkel (16:53) that in the future, the cities of Sedom will return.

**Sicha 3 (5750)**
Rashi 22:12, s.v. *Ki atah yadati* (the first one): "Rabbi Abba said: Avraham said to Him, 'I will explain my argument before You. Yesterday You said to me... and now You say... The Ribono shel Olam said to him, 'I will not profane My covenant...'"—the necessity in the *peshat* of the text for this dialogue between Avraham and the Ribono shel Olam, and the connection to the view of Rabbi Abba (Chagigah 16a) that "anyone who does not have concern for the honor of his Creator"—this refers to "one who looks at the rainbow."

### Chayei Sarah

**Sicha 1 (5752)**
Rashi 23:17, s.v. *Vayakam sdeh Efron*—"It had a 'rising' (tukumah), for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king"—according to *peshat*, and the lesson from this for the *avodah* of each person.

**Sicha 2 (5751)**
"And Avraham was old, advanced in days" (24:1)—but it was already said (Vayeira 18:11) "And Avraham and Sarah were old, advanced in days"; the interpretation of "advanced in days" according to *peshat* and its alignment with the interpretation of the Zohar (our Parsha 129a); an explanation of the phrasing of the text "And the life of Sarah were" (and not "the days of Sarah") and the language of Rashi (beginning of our Parsha) that "all of them were equal for good."

**Sicha 3 (5750)**
The miracle that occurred to Avraham, that he had six more sons (from Keturah) in his old age, and the miracles that occurred at the birth of Yitzchak (Rashi Vayeira 21:6); the two modes of influence upon the nations of the world, hinted at in the last two chapters of the Rambam's *Sefer HaYad*.

### Toldos

**Sicha 1 (5752)**
An explanation of the words of Rabbi Yehudah HaChassid (cited in *Midbar Kados* by the Chida at the beginning) that for 15 years the Patriarchs studied Torah together 15 hours every day, and in *Sefer Chassidim* (Siman 165) that they engaged in the *avodah* of Hashem all 24 hours of the day; the Chida's reconciliation that they studied Torah for 15 hours and engaged in *tefillah* for 9 hours; the parallel of this connection in the three fathers of Chassidus—the Baal Shem Tov, the Maggid of Mezritch, and the Alter Rebbe.

**Sicha 2 (5751)**
The difference between the statement of the Mishnah (end of Kiddushin), "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given," and the statement of Rav (Yoma 28b), "Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah"; the parallel of these two modes in the *mitzvos* of Avraham to our study of Torah (after Matan Torah).

**Sicha 3 (5750)**
Rashi at the end of our Parsha, s.v. *Al nashav*—"He added wickedness to his wickedness..."; the difference between the view of Rashi and the view of the Midrash Rabbah here regarding the nature of Eisav's wickedness in taking Machalat, and whether Machalat was wicked; the lesson regarding the great caution needed concerning "hypocrisy" and hidden evil, and an explanation in a *maamar* of the Alter Rebbe (*Maamarim HaKetzarim*, p. 452) regarding the reason for the length of this final *galus*.

### Vayetzei

**Sicha 1 (5750)**
Rashi at the beginning of the Parsha: "The departure of a righteous person from a place leaves an impression... his splendor has departed..."; the differences regarding the language of the Bereishis Rabbah (Ch. 68:6) and also the difference in Rashi on Rus 1:7; an interpretation of the three matters of "splendor," "radiance," and "glory" according to *peshat*.

**Sicha 2 (5751)**
The two orders in the three *tefillos*—Shacharis, Mincha, Ma'ariv, or Ma'ariv, Shacharis, Mincha; the two definitions in the *avodah* of *tefillah* according to Halacha and in spiritual *avodah* according to Chassidus.

**Sicha 3 (5752)**
Rashi on the verse 31:10, s.v. *U'verudim*; the reason why, according to Rashi's view, this form of "spotted" (*verudim*) was innovated in Yaakov's dream; the alignment of the details of Rashi's language in interpreting "spotted" with the concept of the "World of *Berudim*" explained in Kabbalistic texts.

### Vayishlach

**Sicha 1 (5752)**
The reason for the change of name—"Peniel" or "Penuel"—according to Rashi's view in the *peshat* of the text; an explanation in the words of the Tzemach Tzedek (*Ohr HaTorah* on the verse) that "Peniel" is the *avodah* of the inner heart, and "Penuel" is the drawing down of Elokus into the world.

**Sicha 2 (5750)**
Rashi on the verse 33:16, s.v. *Vayashav Eisav, etc.*—the necessity in the *peshat* of the text that "the 400 men who went with him slipped away from him one by one," and the reason Rashi brings that the Ribono shel Olam repaid them in the days of David, and the differences in Rashi's language regarding the language of the Bereishis Rabbah (Ch. 75:15); the connection to the redemption of the Alter Rebbe on 19 Kislev in the manner of "He redeemed my soul in peace, etc."

**Sicha 3 (5751)**
Rashi on the verse 34:1, *Vateitzei Dinah*—Dinah's departure was like Leah's departure, which was for the good; the difference between Rachel and Leah in spiritual *avodah*—*tzaddikim* and *ba'alei teshuvah*.

### Vayeshev

**Sicha 1 (5752)**
Rashi on the verse 37:10, s.v. *Habo navo*; a discussion regarding the rule that "there is no dream without nonsense"; how is it possible that Yaakov was waiting and expecting when the dream would be fulfilled that he would bow to his son Yosef; Yaakov's anticipation of Yosef's kingship over his brothers according to the inner dimension of things.

**Sicha 2 (5751)**
Rashi on the verse 40:1, *Achar hadevarim ha'eleh*; the interpretation of the phrase "these things" according to the *peshat* of the text; the need for two interpretations according to *peshat* and according to the inner dimension of things; an explanation of the innovation in the teaching of Rabbi Yirmiyah bar Abba (Chullin 92a) on the verse (40:10) "And in the vine were three branches," and the connection to him and also to Rav who "interprets these verses according to you" (Chullin ibid.); the connection to 19 Kislev.

**Vayeshev — Chanukah (5750)**
An explanation of the debate between Rashi and the Ramban (37:17) as to whether Yosef went to seek his brothers knowing that they sought to kill him or not—according to Halacha; the parallel of Yosef's *mesiras nefesh* to the Chashmonaim in the days of Chanukah; the precision of the language in the text of "HaNeiros Hallalu" that the salvations, etc., were "through Your holy Kohanim."

### Mikeitz

**Sicha 1 (5751)**
Rashi on the verse (41:14), *Vayigalach*—"Out of respect for the monarchy"; the difference between shaving and changing clothes according to the *peshat* of the text; the two matters according to Halacha and according to the inner dimension of things, and the connection to Rosh Hashanah (when Yosef left the prison—Rosh Hashanah 10b).

**Sicha 2 (5750)**
*U'tvoach tevach v'hachen* (43:16)—"He prepared for them a slaughterhouse, etc." (Chullin 91a); a discussion on Rashi's view in his commentary on the Torah as to whether the slaughter was *kasher* or not.

**Chanukah (5752)**
A discussion regarding the doubt of the Rebbe Rayatz (his *Igros Kodesh* Vol. II, p. 308) as to whether one mentions the verses of Rosh Chodesh in the *Haftorah* of Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh; the additional doubt regarding adding the verses of *Machar Chodesh* as well.

### Vayigash

**Sicha 1 (5752)**
The debate regarding the law of "not raising one's voice" in *tefillah*, whether this means even to oneself (according to the Zohar in our Parsha 210a) or only to others (Rambam, *Hilchos Tefillah* Ch. 5, Halacha 7), and the debate of the Tur and the Beis Yosef (Orach Chaim, Siman 1) when it is permitted to raise one's voice to others; several modes in the reason for the prohibition to raise one's voice to others, and an explanation of the words of the Alter Rebbe (*Torah Or*, end of our Parsha) that in the future, the *Shemoneh Esrei* will be recited in a loud voice.

**Sicha 2 (5750)**
*Al tirgzu baderech* (45:24)—do not engage in a matter of Halacha (Ta'anis 10b), "Do not stop yourselves from words of Torah" (Bereishis Rabbah Ch. 94:2), or "Do not interrupt a matter of Halacha" (Midrash cited in Tosfos, Ta'anis ibid.); a discussion regarding the definition of the obligation of Torah study while traveling on the road.

**Sicha 3 (5751)**
The *Haftorah* of our Parsha: "And My servant David shall be king over them... and David My servant shall be a prince to them forever" (Yechezkel 37:24-25)—the difference between the two matters of "king over them" and "prince to them"; an explanation of the words of the Shas (Sanhedrin 98b), "The Ribono shel Olam will raise up for them another David... such as a Caesar and a sub-Caesar."

### Vayechi

**Sicha 1 (5750)**
Rashi on the verse 47:31, s.v. *Vayishtachu Yisrael*—"A fox in its time, bow to it"; the difference between this and the language of Rashi later (48:2), "From here that one shows respect to the monarchy."

**Sicha 2 (5752)**
The conclusion of Maseches Nazir—an explanation of the debate as to whether the one who blesses is preferred or the one who says *Amen* is greater, etc.; the connection of this *sugya* to the content of Maseches Nazir, according to the words of the *Tzafenas Pane'ach* on the Torah (in Parshas Vayeshev) regarding the definition of Yosef's *nezirus* (our Parsha 49:26).

**Sicha 3 (5751)**
An explanation of the story in the Shas (Ta'anis 5b): Rav Nachman and Rav Yitzchok were sitting at a meal, etc. One does not converse during a meal, etc. Yaakov Avinu did not die, etc. Just as his offspring are alive, so too is he alive; a discussion regarding the fact that the effect of *mitzvos* must be in a natural manner specifically.


## בראשית

## Sicha 1

### 1

"And the man gave names to all the cattle, and to the birds of the heavens, and to every beast of the field" — yet fish are not mentioned. We find two opinions regarding this: Some commentators hold that he did not give them names (and regarding the reason, the Radak writes: "He did not mention the fish of the sea, for it is not possible according to the nature that the Almighty gave them that they should come before man, as their life is only in the water, and when they leave the water they die; furthermore, they have no limbs for walking, only for swimming"). Others say that Adam HaRishon did give names to the fish as well (it is simply not explicitly stated in the verse).

Seemingly, one could say that these two opinions depend on how one defines the concept of "giving names." To preface:

It is stated in the Midrash that the Holy One, Blessed be He, said to the ministering angels that man’s wisdom was greater than theirs. He brought the cattle, the beasts, and the birds before them and asked, "What is the name of this?" and they did not know. He then brought them before Adam and asked, "What is the name of this?" and he said, "This is an ox, this is a donkey, this is a horse, this is a camel, etc."

The essence of the matter is that Adam HaRishon’s wisdom lay in his recognition of the characteristics and nature of all created beings, and he gave them names that indicated the essence and nature of those being named, as is explained at length in the commentaries on the Torah and the Midrash.

This can be explained in two ways: 
a) The giving of names is for the benefit of man, so that through these names he might recognize and identify the characteristics of the things named. 
b) The giving of names was for the sake of the creatures themselves; before a creature has a specific name, its characteristics are not (fully) revealed, and by being called by a specific name, they become revealed, etc.. According to this, the name is (also) for the benefit of the one being named (and it can be said that the act of naming itself effects the revelation of the nature and characteristics of the one being named).

Seemingly, one could say that this is the reason for the difference of opinion as to whether Adam gave names to the fish or not:

According to the opinion that he did not give names to the fish, the reason is that since the giving of names is for the sake of man—so that he might be able to utilize them, etc.—this does not apply (to the same extent) to fish, as stated in the Chizkuni: "He did not bring the fish, for they are in the water and do not come out, and if they do come out they die, and he has no dominion over them."

### 2

Even though it is explicit in the verse, "And have dominion over the fish of the sea," the dominion over fish is not like the dominion over all the beasts of the field, etc., over which man rules while they are alive and utilizes them. This is not the case with fish, where the dominion is such that he removes them from the water, and thereby they die and he eats them, etc. Since it is a limited form of utilization, there was no need for Adam HaRishon to give them names.

However, according to the opinion that he did give names to the fish, this is because the giving of names is (primarily) for the sake of the animals themselves, so that through this their nature and characteristics are revealed, and this applies to fish as well.

b. It can be said that this is the essence of Rashi’s commentary on the verse, "And whatever the man called each living soul, that was its name"—"Invert it and explain it: every living soul that the man called by a name, that is its name forever." Seemingly, what is Rashi teaching us by adding that this is its name "forever"? And why did he not explain (as the simple reading of the text suggests) that the name Adam HaRishon gave was due to his recognizing the nature and characteristics of the thing—in the words of the Radak, "That is its name—that is its name appropriate to it according to its nature," or as the Chizkuni writes, "It is the name appropriate to it, for his mind agreed with the mind of its Creator".

[And more deeply, it is stated in the sefarim regarding the interpretation of "that is its name," that the name Adam HaRishon called it is its true and essential name. For example, the ox (*shor*) is called by that name because of its root, which are the three letters *shin-vav-reish* in Lashon HaKodesh, and so on for all of them. Therefore, Adam HaRishon, who possessed superior wisdom and grasped and knew the root and essence of every single species, was able to call them by their true name, as the Maggid has elaborated].

It can be said that Rashi’s approach follows the second way mentioned above: that in the act of naming, there was an action performed by Adam HaRishon to reveal these characteristics. Based on this, Rashi explains "that is its name forever," meaning that Adam HaRishon’s action is a constant and eternal action upon the thing called by this name.

c. Now, the beginning of the matter in the Torah regarding the giving of names is: "And Hashem Elokim formed from the ground every beast of the field and every bird of the heavens, and He brought them to the man to see what he would call it; and whatever the man called each living soul, that was its name." Afterwards, it continues regarding the actual giving of names: "And the man gave names, etc."

Seemingly, from the fact that the verse begins with the matter of "And Hashem Elokim formed, etc." (even though the story of the creation of the creatures had already been stated earlier in the parsha), one can bring proof that the giving of names is something that touches upon and acts upon the body of the creatures themselves. Therefore, this is stated as a continuation of "And Hashem Elokim formed, etc."—the formation of the creatures—because the giving of their names is like the completion of their formation.

However, Rashi has already brought from "the words of the Aggadah" that "this 'formation' is an expression of dominion and conquest,

### 3

like 'When you besiege (*tatzur*) a city', that he conquered them under the hand of man." Seemingly, this is how it should be explained according to the opinion that the giving of names is only for the benefit of man, as is also understood from what is written in the Chizkuni, as mentioned above, that the name relates to the dominion over them.

Nevertheless, it is implied from the general content of the parsha that the giving of names was an action performed upon the one being named—for the verses regarding the giving of names are stated within the parsha of the making of an "ezer knegdo" (the creation of Chava). From this, it is implied that the giving of names to the creatures also relates to the thing being named, similar to the creation of Chava, which is the completion of her (and Adam’s) creation.

[And even though commentators explain the continuation of the verses as follows: when the Holy One, Blessed be He, said, "I will make him an *ezer knegdo*", He brought before him all the beasts of the field, etc., to give them names—meaning to contemplate and recognize their nature. And after Adam saw and understood them and their names, and saw that there was no creature worthy of being an *ezer knegdo* for him—"but for man he did not find an *ezer knegdo*"—then "Hashem Elokim caused a deep sleep to fall, etc., and He took one of his ribs, etc., and Hashem Elokim built the rib, etc., and He brought her to the man"—

(In addition to the fact that the interpretation itself requires study, for since it is revealed before Heaven that there is no creature worthy of being an *ezer knegdo*, what is the reason He brought them all before Adam HaRishon for him to contemplate their nature (by giving them names) until he came to the realization that there was no *ezer knegdo* for him?)

Since all matters of the Torah are with the utmost precision, it is logical to say that the fact that the giving of names is mentioned within the parsha of the creation of Chava is (not only because through the giving of names it became clear to Adam HaRishon that there was no one among them who could be an *ezer knegdo*, but) primarily because there is a connection to the content of their essence as well.]

d. It seems to me that according to all opinions, Adam HaRishon’s giving of names was an action performed upon those being named, but there are two ways in which this occurred, as follows.

To preface: In the creation of the world, there are two aspects: 
a) The actual creation by the Holy One, Blessed be He, is in a manner where a thing is brought into being as a separate entity, in which it is not apparent that its existence is from the Holy One, Blessed be He. 
b) The purpose of creation is—"And He placed him in the Garden of Eden to work it and to guard it"—that man, through his *avodah*, should act upon the world and everything in it so that it becomes *batel* to the Holy One, Blessed be He [just as Adam HaRishon did immediately upon being created—on the sixth day—when he demanded of all the creatures that they accept upon themselves the sovereignty and rule of the Creator, proclaiming, "Come, let us bow down and kneel, let us bend the knee before Hashem our Maker"], until he reveals within it the truth of His existence, in the language of the Rambam, that "all beings... exist only from the truth of His existence."

Based on this, it can be said that these are the two ways of interpreting the content of the giving of names by Adam HaRishon—whether it is a continuation and completion of the actual creation by

### 4

the Holy One, Blessed be He, or whether it is part of the purpose of creation and the *avodah* of man.

e. Explanation of the matter:
From the perspective of the actual creation by the Holy One, Blessed be He, there is no division among the creatures into specific species, only a general division into general categories. As we find in the ten utterances through which the world was created, only general categories were specified [such as: on the third day—"Let the earth sprout vegetation, etc., fruit trees, etc."; on the fifth day—"Let the waters swarm with swarms, etc., and birds, etc."; on the sixth day—"cattle, and creeping things, and beasts of the earth"]—and not the names of specific species (such as in animals: ox, donkey, and the like).

This is the essence of the matter of the giving of names by Adam HaRishon: that through this, every single species became recognizable and distinct in its own right.

[And just as it is according to *halacha*, that by calling something by its name, one acquires the object—meaning that by this, he "brings it out" and transfers it from another domain (or from ownerless property) to his own domain. So too, in the giving of names, he "brings it out" from its connection (the general one) to the general species, so that it becomes a specific thing.]

According to this, Adam HaRishon’s action in giving names was to complete the creation itself, for only then was each species finished in its characteristic that distinguishes it from other species (within that same category).

One can interpret the matter of the giving of names by Adam HaRishon more deeply—that this is part of the *avodah* of man, to effect *bitul* in the creatures:

The words of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe are well known, that for all the creatures in the world, the names by which they are called in Lashon HaKodesh are the very letters of the speech of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which form and animate this specific creature. The source of all names is from the ten utterances in the Torah (through which the world was created). For even though not all the names of the creatures are mentioned in the ten utterances, they are branched out from the ten utterances through combinations, permutations, and transpositions of the letters. According to this, it is understood that the names of the creatures are an essential matter within them, that immediately at the beginning of the creature’s coming into being, it has its name, which is its vitality.

If so, the giving of names by Adam HaRishon after their coming into being is a higher matter.

And it can be said that the explanation for this is:
Although every creature has within it a Divine power that animates it, and this is its name by which it is called in Lashon HaKodesh (whose source is from the ten utterances, as mentioned above), nevertheless, this vitality is not revealed within the creature. In particular, the creatures do not feel the Divine vitality within them, nor how their entire existence is nothing but from the truth of His existence, to the point that there is room for the mistake that "My river is my own, and I have made myself". This is the matter of "And the man gave names, etc."—for "calling" denotes revelation—that Adam HaRishon called out and revealed in every creature the Divine vitality (which is its "name"), and through this, he effected *bitul* in all the creatures.

### 5

And more deeply, it can be said: The vitality that is enclothed in every creature is a "contracted" vitality. This is the essence of the matter of the descent of the names from the ten utterances in the Torah through permutations and transpositions, etc.—which is a *tzimtzum* and a descent of the vitality from level to level "until it can be contracted and enclothed and from it a specific creature can come into being". However, through Adam HaRishon giving them names, he called out, drew down, and revealed in every creature the source and root of its name from the ten utterances, above the level to which it descends and contracts to form these specific creatures, reaching even to the "utterance of the mouth of Hashem" itself, "of which they said, 'But could it not have been created with one utterance?' It is the utterance that includes all ten utterances, and from it the ten specific utterances were drawn down and divided" (as is explained in *Likutei Torah*).

f. It can be said that this is the foundation for the two opinions (mentioned above) as to whether Adam HaRishon gave names to the fish of the sea, or only to all the cattle and the beasts of the field, etc.:

According to the first way (as the simple meaning of the verses implies), that the giving of names by Adam HaRishon was to define the specific nature of every creature, and this means separating their existence from their source—this applies only to creatures on land (all the cattle, the birds of the heavens, and the beasts of the field), which were created from the beginning in such a way that they are distinct and separate from their source. Not so with the fish in the sea, which were not created to be a separate existence, but are always covered and absorbed in the water, and their entire existence is the water [as is emphasized in the *din* that everything in the sea is *tahor*, because their essential existence is the water (and to the extent that according to the opinion of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, anything that is from the creation of the water does not constitute a *chatzitzah*, for their true existence is the water)]—there is no place for the giving of names to separate them from their

### 6

source (more than the separation that exists by virtue of their very creation), because the content of their existence from the perspective of the creation by the Holy One, Blessed be He, is not to be separate.

With this, one can explain the depth of the intention of the words of the early authorities mentioned above regarding the reason Adam HaRishon did not give names to the fish, that "it is not possible that they should come before man, for their life is only in the water, and when they leave the water they die"—this is not an external reason or cause, that they could not be brought before Adam HaRishon for him to give them names, but rather that this is in accordance with the content of the matter of names. Since the existence of fish is an inclusion within their source and not a separation and departure outside of their source, therefore there is no place for the giving of names to fish.

However, according to the internal explanation mentioned above, that the matter of the giving of names is to complete the purpose of the creation of all creatures, to call out and draw down into them the vitality from their source and root, so that through this they might be included in the "utterance of the mouth of Hashem", the source and root of their coming into being, as mentioned above—this applies to all creatures, not only to those on land, which are visibly separate from their source, but also to the fish in the sea. For their existence as it is below is the existence of a creature, but their true existence is the water of the sea, and the *avodah* of man is to effect within them *bitul* and inclusion in their source, a *bitul* that is higher than this.

It can be said that this is in accordance with the content of the creation and formation of Chava explained in this parsha: Externally, (the creation of) Chava led to a descent for Adam HaRishon, as literally for the sin of the Tree of Knowledge, which caused a descent for Adam and for the entire creation. On the other hand, the purpose of her creation was to act upon the creation, as it is written, "Be fruitful and multiply, and fill the earth and conquer it, and have dominion over the fish of the sea and over the birds of the heavens and over every beast that creeps upon the earth." And through man fulfilling his mission in the world, "and fill the earth," this effects upon it something that is incomparable to man himself.

(From the *sichos* of Shabbos Parshas Bereishis 5737, 5746)

## Sicha 2

## 7

### Sicha 2

1. Following the account of the creation of the world, man, and so on, the parsha of Bereishis recounts the descendants and generations of Adam’s two sons, Kayin and Seth. On the face of it, they are diametrically opposed: Kayin represents the antithesis of building the world, for he killed Hevel and was a wanderer on the earth, whereas from Seth the world was established, as after the Flood, the world was rebuilt through Noach, who descended from Seth.

We find a curious phenomenon: both had a descendant named Chanoch—Kayin’s son and one of Seth’s descendants—and in both cases, the Torah emphasizes content that is the opposite of their respective fathers. Regarding Chanoch, the son of Kayin, it is said, "And (Kayin) was building a city, and he called the name of the city after the name of his son, Chanoch." Regarding Chanoch, the descendant of Seth, it is said, "And Chanoch walked with Elokus, and he was not, for Elokus took him." Thus, regarding Kayin’s son Chanoch, the Torah emphasizes the concept of "building" and permanence (the opposite of Kayin’s own nature), whereas regarding the Chanoch who descended from Seth, the Torah explicitly states the opposite of permanence (the establishment of the world), saying, "and he was not".

Furthermore, it must be understood that all the details recorded and explained in the Torah in the account of the "generations of Adam" are relevant to the subsequent generations of humanity. It is understood simply that since the Torah relates only these specific details from a span of over a thousand years—despite the many other events that occurred during that time—it is because only these details hold significance for the generations that followed. Based on this, a question arises: while it is understandable (in addition to the Midrashic interpretation) why the Torah specifies regarding Chanoch, the descendant of Seth, that "Chanoch walked with Elokus and he was not, for Elokus took him"—as this is

## 8

part of the account of those generations, where for each it is said, "And all the days of so-and-so were... and he died," and therefore it specifies that for Chanoch it was different, "and he was not, for Elokus took him"—what is the relevance of the statement regarding Kayin’s son Chanoch, "and he was building a city, and he called the name of the city after the name of his son, Chanoch," to the continuation of the generations of Adam?

[The Midrash on the verse states that this, too, is intended to recount Kayin’s wickedness ("They thought their houses would last forever... what do the wicked think..."). Similarly, many Torah commentators explain that it points to his evil deeds ("He did not say 'he built a city' as one says 'he built' Nineveh... rather, he was building the city all his days because his deeds were cursed; he built a little with toil and labor, and he wandered from that place... "). However, on the face of it, the simple reading of the verses does not imply this. Furthermore, the style of Rashi, who simply states, "And Kayin was building a city, and he called the name of the city in memory of his son Chanoch" (even though elsewhere in the parsha he explains the reason for detailing various matters), does not suggest that this is a detail in the account of his wickedness and deficiency, but rather the opposite: a matter of building.]

Especially according to the Zohar, which states that Torah (*Torah*) comes from the word *hora'ah* (instruction), it is understood that this, too, contains an instruction for the generations.

2. Simply put, one can say:
This verse, "And Kayin knew... and she bore Chanoch, and he was building a city," follows the account of Kayin’s teshuvah, where he said, "My iniquity is too great to bear," and his punishment, etc., "And Kayin went out from before Hashem,"—meaning he went out rejoicing that his teshuvah had been accepted.

The Torah teaches us through the story of the birth of his son Chanoch and the building of the city that in the avodah of teshuvah, confession and recognition of the sin ("My iniquity is too great to bear") are not enough, nor is the punishment decreed upon him by Hashem, even to the point of exile, "And he dwelt in the land of Nod—the land where all exiles wander,"—for "exile atones for iniquity because it causes one to be humbled and to be of a lowly spirit"—rather, one must perform an action, "to do some mitzvah that is the opposite of the sin he committed... for through this his distortion will be rectified,"—so that after he had destroyed one soul, he brought forth another soul: "And Kayin knew his wife, and she conceived and bore Chanoch."

Moreover, he went and built the world, "And he was building a city, and he called the name of the city after the name of his son, Chanoch," emphasizing and publicizing the order of teshuvah: that it is not enough to "cry out constantly before

## 9

Hashem with tears and supplications," but one must effect "building" in the world outside of oneself, which requires a movement opposite to the submission and lowliness of spirit of the *baal teshuvah*.

Just as we learn from him the very essence of teshuvah (as the Midrash says that Adam HaRishon learned from Kayin "the power of teshuvah"), so too is this the content and continuation of this verse: from it we learn the path of teshuvah.

According to this, the contrast in the second Chanoch (from the descendants of Seth) is also explained: "And Chanoch walked with Elokus... and all the days of Chanoch were... and Chanoch walked with Elokus and he was not, for Elokus took him." By this, the verse teaches us that when a person’s conduct is only in the manner of avodah for himself, it is not the desired avodah, for it produces no effect in the world, to the point that his own days were shorter than those of other people of that time, as he was taken before his time.

3. Admittedly, this instruction is derived from the simple interpretation of the verses, which speak of teshuvah for Kayin’s most grave sin. However, according to the inner dimension of these matters, the root of Kayin’s level is extremely lofty from the moment he was born, as he was named Kayin after "I have acquired (*kaniti*) a man with Hashem,", and the entire account regarding Kayin from the time of his birth as it appears in the text—for "the Torah speaks essentially of the supernal realms"—is on a most lofty level.

Therefore, it can be said that the instruction from these verses regarding Kayin’s son Chanoch, and likewise regarding Chanoch from the descendants of Seth, is not only regarding the avodah of the *baal teshuvah*, but also regarding the avodah of every member of Bnei Yisrael in every place and at every time (which is also relevant to the fact that both were named Chanoch). This is especially so according to what the *Tzofnas Paneach* writes on the verse "This is the book of the generations of Adam," that "all the reality that occurred then is for all time, both in the individual person and in the general person... for everything written is not just for the moment, but every single moment is a new reality."

4. Explanation of the matter:
In the creation as a whole, we find two periods: the period before the Flood, which had no permanence, as all existence was wiped out, and after the Flood, when permanence was effected in the world in the manner of "they shall not cease." Similarly, after the Flood itself, there are two periods: the generations before the Giving of the Torah and after the Giving of the Torah. Before the Giving of the Torah, the world was in a state of "earth that fears," and after the Giving of the Torah, it became a state of "and it was quiet."

A parallel to these two periods (and states) is found in the avodah of Bnei Yisrael even after the Giving of the Torah [and furthermore, all of this is part of "This is the book of the generations of Adam," recounted in the Torah, meaning it is an eternal instruction for every Jew until the end of all generations]—that in the avodah of Hashem, there are generally two paths: avodah in the manner of Ben Azzai, who did not engage in the settling of

## 10

the world, did not marry, and said, "What shall I do, for my soul desires Torah," and the avodah of those who engage in the settling and sustaining of the world. In the terminology of Chassidus, this is the avodah of *ratzo* (running), to exit the boundaries of the world, and the avodah of *shov* (returning), to return and engage within the affairs of the world.

Even though the order of avodah of Hashem should be to engage in the settling of the world (and "anyone who does not engage in procreation... is as if he sheds blood and diminishes the Image"), nevertheless, "this is only the advice of the Sages who instruct a person in the straight path he should choose, but there is no prohibition at all in the matter if he desires to study Torah all his days and not marry at all like Ben Azzai,"—meaning this conduct is also according to Torah.

More specifically, the avodah of Hashem in general contains these two lines and movements, as we find in all three pillars of Torah, Avodah (Tefillah), and Gemilus Chasadim (Mitzvos):

In Torah: There are two modes of study: study in the manner of "He will magnify Torah and make it glorious—expound and receive reward," and study to derive the practical halacha.
More specifically and subtly: study of Torah *lishmah* (for its own sake), "that our souls may become one with the essence, spirituality, and holiness of the source from which the Torah originates... clinging to its holiness and to His Shechinah," (as the *Bach* elaborates on the content of the blessing over the Torah), and study for the sake of fulfilling the mitzvos, to know the deed they should do.

In Tefillah: One of the foundations of tefillah is the *kavanah* that "one should see himself as if he is standing before the Shechinah," and "the Chassidim and men of action would isolate themselves and focus in their tefillah until they reached a state of divestment from physicality and the overcoming of the intellectual spirit, until they reached close to the level of prophecy"; the content of the mitzvah of tefillah is requesting one’s needs from the Ribono shel Olam, that He fill one’s lacks and desires, even down to physical needs, "Healer of the sick," "Blesser of the years."

In Mitzvos: Fulfilling the mitzvos *lishmah*, to provide *nachas ruach* to one’s Creator, "for I have spoken and My will has been done," to the point of ultimate perfection in this, avodah with "all your might" (*b'chol me'odecha*), beyond measurement and limitation; and fulfilling the mitzvos "to refine the creatures," for the sake of the *birur* (refinement) and purification of the world, which must be done in an orderly fashion according to the boundaries of the world.

The source for these two positive modes of conduct is the two manners in the generations of Adam, in a positive sense—Kayin and Seth:
Kayin and his descendants had no permanence, and he is the root and general principle for the state of the world before the Giving of the Torah, and for avodah of Hashem in the manner of Ben Azzai, which is above the order and settling of the world. Seth and his descendants, from whom the world was established through Noach,

## 11

who is "He will comfort us from our deeds," is the root and general principle in the "generations of Adam" for the state of the world after the Giving of the Torah, and in the avodah of Hashem itself—the avodah of settling and sustaining the world.

5. According to this, we can explain the lesson from the fact that Kayin had a son named Chanoch and built a city by that name, and among the descendants of Seth there was also a son named Chanoch, but "And Chanoch walked with Elokus and he was not, for Elokus took him"—from this we learn that each of these two movements must also be included in the avodah of the other type. Just as with Kayin, whose nature is the exit from the boundaries of the world, there exists the avodah of "building a city," the order of avodah of building; so too in the order of Seth, from whom the world was established, there is also the order of avodah of "walking with Elokus and he was not," above the world.

This is explained regarding Torah, Tefillah, and Mitzvos:
Even one whose primary study is in the manner of "magnifying Torah and making it glorious," expounding and receiving reward, must also have study for the sake of deriving the practical halacha. Similarly, together with the engagement in Torah to cling to its holiness, there must be study to know the deed they should do [similar to the general concept of Torah and Mitzvos, that anyone who says "I have only Torah" does not even have Torah, but rather there must be Torah and Gemilus Chasadim]. And conversely, in the avodah of order and degree—in study to derive the practical halacha and study to know the deed—there must be a felt sense of the study to magnify Torah and cling to its holiness.

Likewise in Tefillah: Even in the ultimate clinging and divestment from physicality in tefillah, one must intend to effect change in the world outside of oneself, as is known in the explanation of the Sages that one who prays should set his eyes downward and his heart upward. Even in the ultimate clinging and *ratzo* of tefillah, one must intend to effect change in his avodah and soul-powers as he is below after tefillah—"his eyes downward." Likewise, in requesting one’s needs in tefillah, one must feel the ultimate *bitul* (nullification) and clinging of standing before Hashem "like a servant before his master."

The same applies to Mitzvos: even when engaging in mitzvos in the manner of *mesiras nefesh* and the great love of "all your might," one must fulfill the mitzvos in physicality and in an orderly fashion. And conversely, even when engaging in orderly avodah in the refinement of one’s animal soul and the refinement of the world, there must be the feeling of *ratzo* of "all your might."

6. According to this, we can also explain the hint in the name "Chanoch":
"Chanoch" comes from the word *chinuch* (education/initiation). The concept of *chinuch*, even though externally it is not an end in itself—as in the mitzvah of *chinuch*, where the goal is that the child will grow up and subsequently fulfill the mitzvos as one obligated—internally, the concept of *chinuch* is the foundation for all of a person’s life, as it is written, "Initiate (*chanoch*) a youth according to his way, even when

## 12

he grows old, he will not depart from it." For the true essence of *chinuch* is not just that the child learns, but the habituation and entry into holiness, the essence of which is the connection of his soul with holiness and the Ribono shel Olam. Therefore, "even when he grows old" and rises in the avodah of Hashem and the fulfillment of mitzvos, "he will not depart from it," and this provides the permanence and strength in his fulfillment of Torah and Mitzvos in all situations.

This means that the avodah of settling and building, which is included in the avodah of *ratzo* in Torah, Tefillah, and Mitzvos (the aspect of Kayin), and conversely, the avodah of *ratzo* ("walking with Elokus and he was not"), which is included in the avodah of settling the world (the aspect of Seth)—this is not just a detail or secondary to the main avodah, but it is the foundation of his avodah, and it is what effects the permanence in his avodah, similar to the concept of *chinuch*.

For even though these are two opposite movements—a movement of relating to the affairs and boundaries of the world (study to know the deed, requesting needs, refining the world), and a movement of ascent and clinging and *ratzo*—which cannot both be present in a person at the same time, nevertheless, since they are dependent upon one another, it is understood that in practice they can exist together. This is not from the side of the created being, but from the side of the Creator’s power within this avodah, similar to *chinuch*, whose inner content is *bitul* and self-connection to the Ribono shel Olam.

7. The explanation of this:
In order to intend to arrive at the practical halacha, one must reach a higher level in the study and understanding of the Torah. Similarly, in avodah, in order to intend to arrive at the halacha, there must be within him *bitul* to Elokus, as the Sages say, "And Hashem was with him—that the halacha is according to him," for specifically the *Havayah* (Hashem) that is above the name *Elokim* effects that "the halacha is according to him." As explained elsewhere, in order to intend to arrive at the practical halacha, the study must be from a place of complete *yirah* (awe).

The same is true regarding Tefillah: the necessity that even when one is engaged in requesting his needs, it should be with a movement of *bitul*, is not because *bitul* is merely something that assists and strengthens another thing, but because in the depth and inner dimension of the matter, they are one thing. As the Maggid of Mezeritch explained regarding "One should only stand to pray with a heavy head" (solemnity), "Even though he is requesting his own needs, his intention should be that there be no lack above, Heaven forbid, for the soul is a part of Elokus from above and is a limb of the limbs of the Shechinah, and this is the essence of the request—that it be filled and influenced above." As explained elsewhere, the fact that in requesting his needs one must feel that the intention is that the will of Hashem be fulfilled and the lack of the Shechinah be filled—these are not two things, but rather, the fulfillment of his request for needs is from the side of filling the lack above, as it were, because he is a part of Elokus from above in truth.

## 13

Likewise in the fulfillment of Mitzvos: the action upon his animal soul and his portion in the world in refining and purifying them is in its perfection when he feels that his true place and level is not below, but above, as the soul is "embraced and clinging to You," and does not belong to the needs of the body and the world at all (not even for the sake of refining them). For specifically then, there is the power in his avodah in the fulfillment of mitzvos to act upon the animal soul and the world to refine and purify them.

Just as it is in the avodah of *shov*, that for its perfection and permanence as required there must be the feeling of *ratzo*, so too is it conversely: that in the movement of *ratzo*, the ultimate clinging to Elokus, there must also be the movement of *shov*. This is not only so that there will be permanence to the *ratzo* (for otherwise one is liable to exit the vessels entirely to cling to Hashem, and no action in Torah and Mitzvos will come of it), but it is relevant to the very movement of *ratzo*. For the litmus test that his running to Hashem is a true matter and not just because "closeness to Elokus is good for me," to satisfy his thirsty soul, is when together with it there is the movement of *shov*, that he disconnects himself from the greatness of his clinging to Hashem and descends below to fulfill the will of the Ribono shel Olam—in Torah, the study to derive the practical halacha, to know the deed they should do; and likewise in Tefillah and Mitzvos, to work with his body and animal soul and his portion in the world to refine and purify them.

Moreover: this is not just a litmus test for the soul’s clinging, but through this, an ascent and perfection is effected in the *ratzo* and his clinging to Hashem, and even an ascent in the soul above, higher than it was before its descent below.

8. These two matters are especially relevant to Parshas Bereishis, which speaks of the creation and formation of the world. For even the word of Hashem that enlivens the worlds at every moment (according to the well-known teaching of the Baal Shem Tov on the verse, "Forever, Hashem, Your word stands in the heavens"—that Your word which You said, "Let there be a firmament," stands and exists forever within the firmament of the heavens, etc., to enliven them, and likewise in all the creatures in all the worlds, upper and lower), its clinging to its source is in the manner of *ratzo* and *shov*. As explained elsewhere, the Elokic power that constitutes and enlivens the created beings is an aspect of a "separate power." For the Elokic vitality that is in clinging to its source and is not a thing unto itself cannot effect action, and *a fortiori* cannot clothe itself in created beings to constitute them. Therefore, the formation is specifically through the "Ten Utterances," which are the Elokic power as it becomes separate from its source, as it were. But conversely, it is not in the power of a separate power to constitute *yesh mei'ayin* (something from nothing), for creation *yesh mei'ayin* is only in the power and ability of Him, may He be blessed, who has no cause or reason that preceded Him, Heaven forbid. Therefore, in order to constitute and enliven the created beings, it must be felt within it that its source is in Elokus, as it is above giving place to creation, and this means that the clinging is

## 14

in the aspect of *ratzo* and *shov*, and through this, it is constantly renewed from its source.

And in this matter, too, they are not two different things that relate to one another—that the *ratzo* acts in the level of *shov*—but rather, the fact that the word of Hashem from the Ten Utterances clothes itself in all created beings to constitute and sustain them, even though on one hand their nature is a separate power to constitute, yet together with this, it is felt within them themselves that their true place is not as they are a separate power constituting the created beings, but as they are in their source. And the fact that they clothe themselves below in the created beings is only from the side of the intention of the *Atzmus* (Essence). Thus, it is found that in the "place" and state of separation itself, their clinging to their source is felt.

9. According to all this, the connection of Parshas Bereishis to Simchas Torah is also understood:
On Simchas Torah, Bnei Yisrael are after the avodah and revelations of Rosh Hashanah, Yom Kippur, and Sukkos, during which they ascend from level to level from below to above, and there is also an *hemshech* (drawing down) from above to below in an internal way through the taking of the Four Species on Sukkos.

Therefore, on Shemini Atzeres and Simchas Torah, Bnei Yisrael are in a state of clinging, where Bnei Yisrael rejoice with the Torah with joy and dancing that is entirely beyond measurement and limitation, the aspect of "Yisrael and the King are alone together."

But together with this, and as a continuation of this, we read Parshas Bereishis, "In the beginning Elokus created the heavens and the earth," to teach that the true clinging of Bnei Yisrael to the Ribono shel Olam is in the *shov*, that Bnei Yisrael go out into the world to make for Him, may He be blessed, a dwelling place in the lower realms, so that the glory of His Kingdom, may He be blessed, will be revealed in the entire world, and "Hashem shall be King over all the earth; on that day Hashem shall be One and His name One," and new heavens and a new earth are created, with the coming of Mashiach Tzidkeinu immediately, now.


## נח

## Sicha 1

## 15

A. Regarding the verse, "Noach was a righteous man, perfect in his generations," the Zohar states: "But in other generations he is not considered anything, such as the generation of Avraham, the generation of Moshe, and the generation of David."

Now, a precise interpretation of this sort (that "in his generations" is meant to exclude other generations — "to his detriment") is brought in several places in the words of our Sages, with variations regarding which other generations are intended: In the Gemara — "in his generations, but not in other generations," generally; in the Tanchuma — that "had he been in the generation of Avraham, he would not have found his hands and feet" (and similarly in Rashi on the Torah, "had he been in the generation of Avraham, he would not have been considered anything"); and in Bereishis Rabbah — "had he been in the generation of Moshe or the generation of Shmuel, he would not have been a righteous man."

The interpretation of the Tanchuma, that the intent is to exclude "the generation of Avraham," is understood simply, for Avraham was the righteous man who came in close proximity after the generations of Noach. Therefore, it is logical to interpret the intent of the verse, "he was a righteous man, perfect in his generations," as meaning that specifically "in the generations" of Noach he was a righteous man, perfect, but had he been in the generation of Avraham, which came after "his generations," he would not have been considered anything.

Likewise, the interpretation in Bereishis Rabbah, "had he been in the generation of Moshe or the generation of Shmuel," can be understood simply, that its intent is to exclude him by way of "detriment"—that specifically in the generation of Moshe and the generation of Shmuel he would not have been (considered) a righteous man, because Moshe was the choicest of the human species, the father of all the prophets, with whom Hashem spoke face to face, and similarly Shmuel, who was weighed against Moshe and Aharon. Therefore, specifically in their generations, Noach would not have been (considered) a righteous man, but in other generations he would have been (considered) a righteous man, etc.

However, the interpretation of the Zohar, that the precision of the verse concerns these three generations—"the generation of Avraham, the generation of Moshe, and the generation of David"—requires an explanation of the reason why specifically in these three generations he was "not considered anything."

## 16

B. It seems to me that the three righteous men of the three generations mentioned in the Zohar are those through whom the beginning of the three principles in the fulfillment of the purpose and intent of Creation (which is for the sake of Yisrael, who are called "beginning," and for the sake of the Torah, which is called "beginning") was achieved—namely, Yisrael, Torah, and Malchus. Avraham Avinu is the beginning of the people of Yisrael; the essence of Moshe’s greatness is that through him the Torah was given to the entirety of Yisrael; and Yisrael is in a state of perfection in the world when they are in a state of Malchus, and David is the beginning and the start of the "essence of Malchus."

[And these three righteous men are alluded to in the name *Adam* (Man), the acronym for Avraham, David, Moshe, meaning that the completion of the purpose of the creation of "Adam" is through the three modes of avodah of Avraham, Moshe, and David.]

This is the content of the Zohar’s interpretation: although Noach "saw a new world," and simply put, this was after the Flood—similar to Adam HaRishon, from whom the entire human species came, and Noach also fulfilled in his avodah the role of "Adam"—nevertheless, in relation to the three righteous men of these three generations, he is not considered anything.

The reason for this is understood, as mentioned above, because the primary beginning of the avodah in fulfilling the intent of Creation was through these three righteous men ("Adam") in these three generations.

C. More specifically, it can be said:

## 17

The novelty here—that despite the greatness and righteousness of Noach, his avodah is not considered anything in comparison to the level of the avodah of Avraham, Moshe, and David—is because within the avodah of Noach himself, there was a semblance of the unique avodah that was in these three generations through the aforementioned righteous men.

The explanation of these matters will be understood by first noting the novelty in the avodah of the "Adam" in these three generations, which are three stages and levels in the order of avodah:

*The generation of Avraham:* Regarding Avraham, who was the first Jew, it is said, "Avraham was one," meaning "the entire world was on one side, and he (Avraham) was on the other side"—the entire world were idolaters, and "Avraham stood and became wise on his own and served the Ribono shel Olam," and he stood with strength against the entire world, and he knew that the entire world was mistaken, etc.

This is also the fundamental and primary matter in the avodah of man in general: that Bnei Yisrael strengthen themselves in their dveikus to Hashem and stand with strength in their avodah—against the seventy nations and the entire world that prevent and hinder, etc.

*The generation of Moshe, the generation of the Giving of the Torah:* The novelty in the avodah of the generation of the Giving of the Torah is that (not only does the world not interfere with the avodah of man, but on the contrary) the avodah of man brings about the world’s existence and establishment. As our Sages say regarding the verse, "The earth feared and was quiet"—at first it feared, and in the end it was quiet [for the Ribono shel Olam made a condition with the work of Creation: "If Yisrael accepts the Torah, you will exist, and if not, I will return you to chaos and void"]. Before the Giving of the Torah, the earth (the world) was in a state of "fear," and through Yisrael accepting the Torah, it "was quiet."

This is the second stage in the avodah of man: the existence of the world through the Torah, where through the avodah of Bnei Yisrael in learning Torah and keeping mitzvos (which is possible specifically in this world), they bring about the existence of the world's reality.

*The generation of David:* The "essence of Malchus" is the Malchus of the House of David, whose purpose is to make the Ribono shel Olam King over the entire world. Just as it was literally, that through David the final conquest of Eretz Yisrael from the nations of the world was achieved, to the point of a state that was entirely Malchus immediately at its inception, as it was in the days of Shlomo, when the nations brought gifts to Shlomo—that through the Malchus of the House of David, there was a revelation of the Malchus of the Ribono shel Olam in the world.

This is the novelty in the generation of David over the generation of Moshe: through Torah and mitzvos, the world is sustained as it is, while through the revelation of Malchus, a transcendence is effected in the world.

The perfection of this will be through the final "King" from the House of David, who is the "final Mashiach," called "a King from the House of David" upon the arrival of Mashiach Tzidkeinu. Then the Malchus of the Ribono shel Olam will be revealed in the entire world, both among the nations of the world, for "He will rectify the entire world to serve Hashem together, as it is said, 'For then I will turn to the peoples a clear language... to serve Him with one shoulder,'" and "Many peoples will go and say, 'Come, let us go up to the mountain of Hashem, to the house of the G-d of Yaakov, and He will teach us of His ways, and we will walk in His paths'"; and also among all the creatures in the world—for "They will not do evil and will not destroy on all

## 18

My holy mountain, for the earth will be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea."

[The foundation and support for this interpretation (in the words of the Zohar) can be found in the explanation of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, on the Zohar there in our Parsha, which states: "He counts these three: Avraham, Moshe, David. This is because these three are the three crowns: the crown of Kehunah, the crown of Torah, the crown of Malchus. Kehunah is Avraham, the man of Chesed, and the Kehunah was given to Avraham, as it is written, 'You are a priest forever.' Torah is Moshe, for the Torah was given through Moshe, and it is called the Torah of Moshe. Malchus is David, for the kingship was given to David forever."

The matter of the "crown of Kehunah" is similar to what was explained regarding the definition of the "generation of Avraham," for the essence of the *Kohen* is that he was set apart to serve Hashem and minister to Him, which in a general sense is the essence of the people of Yisrael. Furthermore, the title "Kehunah" belongs to all of Yisrael, as the Rambam writes that it applies to everyone, "to be set apart to stand before Hashem to minister to Him and to serve Him and to know Him," and to cast off "from his neck the yoke of the many calculations that human beings have sought (for then) he is sanctified as a holy of holies."]

D. Now, (a semblance of) these three modes of avodah existed in Noach during the period of the Flood:

1) In his own avodah—which is similar to how Avraham was "on one side, etc." For even though the generation was in the lowest possible state—"all flesh had corrupted its way upon the earth"—nevertheless, Noach strengthened himself and "was a righteous man, perfect in his generations," and was not dragged along after the conduct of the entire generation. On the contrary, "Noach the righteous man would rebuke them and say to them, 'Do teshuvah.'"

2) Likewise, in his avodah there was the matter of acting upon the world (similar to the effect of the Giving of the Torah), for through him the salvation of the entire world was achieved, to the point that the Ribono shel Olam swore that "all flesh will never again be cut off by the waters of the Flood, and there will never again be a flood to destroy the earth."

3) And even more than this: in the deeds of Noach there was also an action of "ascent" and change in the world (similar to the effect of the Malchus of the House of David). In the Ark of Noach, there were all kinds of animals and beasts, etc., and among them were also predatory and violent beasts, and yet they did not do any harm to one another at all. It is explained in this regard that this is because in the Ark of Noach there prevailed a state of peace similar to what is said for the future, "The wolf will dwell with the lamb... they will not do evil and will not destroy," because then (in the Ark of Noach) there was a "revelation" that was above the constraints of the world (as it is from its own perspective).

[And in truth, this state existed not only while they were in the Ark, but even before they entered it, as explained in the words of our Sages and in Rashi, that only those who cleaved to their own kind and did not corrupt their way came to the Ark, and furthermore, they came on their own—all of which is a most wondrous and great miracle.

## 20

The fact that Noach "was perfect in his generations," meaning in relation to the people of his generation, is (primarily) in those matters of the wickedness of that generation. And since the primary wickedness of that generation was in matters between man and his fellow—theft and immorality, etc., "and their final verdict (of the generation of the Flood) was sealed only because of theft"—it follows that the avodah of Noach was also (primarily) in matters between man and his fellow. For matters of "between man and his fellow" do not have (in general, in relation to matters of "between man and Hashem") a purpose in and of themselves, but only so as not to disrupt the "settlement of the world," similar to the matter of education, where the purpose is not in the education itself, but to accustom the one being educated and prepare him for the time of obligation (as mentioned above). Furthermore: this itself was not so much from his recognition and faith in the Ribono shel Olam, but from the Flood. Just as his calling to the people of his generation to awaken them to teshuvah was based on the warning of the punishment that the Ribono shel Olam would bring upon them with the Flood, so too it is understood that his own avodah was primarily in fear of punishment, to the point that our Sages say that he did not enter the Ark until the waters pressed him, which detail is also like "education" from fear.

This is unlike the "generation of Avraham," where (a) the primary novelty of Avraham (that the entire world was on one side and Avraham was on the other) was not (only) in matters between man and his fellow, but in matters of faith—the negation of idolatry and the strengthening of faith in Hashem; (b) Avraham’s recognition of the Ribono shel Olam (and likewise his action in the world—that "he began to inform the people that it is not fitting to serve anyone but the G-d of the world... and to call out in a loud voice to the entire world and inform them that there is one G-d for the entire world and He is the one worthy of being served") came from the fact that "his heart wandered and understood until he attained the way of truth and understood the line of justice from his correct understanding," and not from fear of punishment or for his own benefit. On the contrary, he sacrificed his soul for faith in Hashem. And as the Rambam says, that "doing the truth because it is the truth" is the greatness of Avraham Avinu.

And the same is true regarding the second matter—the salvation and existence of the world: the fact that after the Flood it was renewed that "I will not again curse the ground anymore for man's sake... nor will I again strike every living thing as I have done... all flesh will never again be cut off by the waters of the Flood and there will never again be a flood to destroy the earth," is (only) a matter of preventing undesirable things, the negation of the destruction of the world (similar to education to remove the negative inclination in man). This is unlike the generation of Moshe, where through the Giving of the Torah, a positive existence and strengthening were added to the reality of the world, which only then justified the existence of the world.

And likewise regarding the third matter: the state of peace and miracles that were in the Ark during the time of the Flood (and similarly during the time of preparation, etc., for the Flood)—was also due to the necessity of salvation and "to keep seed alive upon the face of all the earth,"

Whereas in the "generation of David"—that is, the Malchus of David, through whom the preparation for the building of the Temple was achieved with the ten miracles that were performed for our ancestors in the Holy Temple—there was then a revelation of

## 21

the Malchus of the Ribono shel Olam due to the resting of His *Shechinah* in the Holy Temple, to the point that all the nations saw the greatness of the work and the superiority of the kings of the House of David and the benefit of the Holy Temple (as mentioned above in the days of Shlomo). And especially when the perfection of this will be in the future, when "They will not do evil and will not destroy on all My holy mountain, for the earth will be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea."

F. And one can say, even more deeply—based on what my father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote (in his notes mentioned above), that Avraham, Moshe, and David are the three crowns: the crown of Kehunah, the crown of Torah, and the crown of Malchus. And he continues there, that "in Noach this was not present, only the crown of a good name, for in his generations he had a good name, which is lower than all these three":

The title of the crown of Kehunah, the crown of Torah, and the crown of Malchus indicates that their essence is above the head, like a crown literally, which is above the head of a person—meaning, a level of avodah that a person does not reach through his own power and intellect, but rather it is above him. In other words: this is something that comes from the perspective of the Creator and not from the perspective of the created being. On the other hand, when a person is crowned with a crown—the crown of Kehunah, the crown of Torah, and the crown of Malchus—it is not in a way that he acquires something external that is an addition to him, but rather it becomes his entire reality (that he is crowned with this crown).

It can be said that this is the difference between the avodah of Noach in these three matters in fulfilling the intent of Creation, which is in the category of "education," and the avodahs of the three generations of Avraham, Moshe, and David, which are in the manner of a "crown":

The avodah of Noach and the state of the world after the Flood were only as much as the created being can reach through his own power, and consequently, both in his avodah for himself and in his action in the existence of the world, there was no departure from the constraints of the world and Creation—Creation remained in its type and constraint, having no connection (revelation) to the Creator. Whereas the avodah in the generation of Avraham, the generation of Moshe, and the generation of David was in the manner of a crown, from the Ribono shel Olam who is above Creation, and it is simple that the manner of avodah of created beings in relation to avodah that comes from a power above Creation "is not considered anything."

G. The explanation of this in detail:

The avodah of Hashem of Noach was connected with Elokus as He, so to speak, contracted Himself according to the measure of Creation. Therefore, it only effected in him and for him his attainment of the Ribono shel Olam so as not to be dragged along after other human beings in the corruption of the world, contrary to the settlement of the world, and not above that. This is unlike Avraham, for even though the beginning of his drawing close to the Ribono shel Olam was through proofs from the matters of Creation, his recognition of the Ribono shel Olam was (also) as He is above Creation,, the level of "crown," and therefore he reached the level of self-sacrifice, that he "does the truth because it is the truth."

And it is the same regarding the second and third matters: that which was effected in the world after the Flood, "I will not again curse... all flesh will never again be cut off, etc.," and the special state that existed in the Ark of Noach, is not (a) a true change in the state of the world, nor (b) a true ascent in the state of the world.

This is unlike the action of the generation of Moshe through the Torah, which is the existence of the world, for the Torah

## 22

preceded the world, and the action through the Torah is conditional (as mentioned above, that the Ribono shel Olam made a condition with the work of Creation: if Yisrael accepts the Torah, good, and if not, I will return you to chaos and void), and the entire reality of the world’s formation is for the sake of the Torah, which is called "beginning."

And above this is the generation of David—the matter of Malchus, the revelation of the Malchus of the Ribono shel Olam in the world. The advantage of this over the action in the world that comes from the Torah is that the action of the Torah in the world does not belong (as much) to the reality of the world itself, for the Torah was given only to Yisrael, except that through Yisrael accepting the Torah and through the learning of Torah and the keeping of mitzvos by Yisrael, it is also effected in the world.

This is unlike Malchus, for just as it is regarding the kingship of the earth, where the definition of kingship is that it relates to the very reality of the people, that their entire reality is the King, to the point that anyone who rebels against the kingship is liable for death—which is not by way of punishment, but a consequence that comes automatically—so too it is in the Malchus of Heaven. The acceptance of the yoke of the Malchus of Heaven, which is through the *bitul* to the King (which is primarily in the Malchus of the House of David), is in a way that the *bitul* is in their very reality.

Thus, it is the same regarding the action of Malchus in the world: when the Malchus of the Ribono shel Olam is revealed in the world, it is not as something added to the reality of Creation and the world, but rather this is the entire reality of the world, that the Ribono shel Olam, King of the world, is felt and revealed in it. As emphasized in the aforementioned verse, "For the earth will be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea," which is the entire reality of the earth, similar to "as the waters cover the sea," where the sea is not something separate, but the entire reality of the sea is that it is full of water.

H. Now, in this matter, there is a lesson in the avodah of man (as in everything in the Torah).
