It can be said that this is the precision of the Zohar’s language, "But in other generations he is not considered anything"—in the present tense, and not in the past tense like (in the Tanchuma) "had he been in the generation of Avraham, he would not have found his hands and feet," or as in Bereishis Rabbah, "he would not have been a righteous man." For the primary intent of the Zohar is not, G-d forbid, to speak to the detriment of Noach, but (primarily) as a lesson in the avodah of every person of Yisrael, that even now there exists the avodah of Noach. And regarding this, it says that in relation to the avodah (now) in the "generation of Avraham, the generation of Moshe, and the generation of David"—the mode of avodah of Noach "is not considered anything."

Meaning:

The avodah in these three general matters exists as it is from the perspective of man, where man (the created being) comes to the conclusion that the desired path is not to be dragged along after the environment and not to be influenced by it, and to stand with strength in his avodah to his Creator, and to act in the world through Torah, and even more than this, to reveal the Malchus of the Ribono shel Olam in the world.

And regarding this, the interpretation of the Zohar teaches us that all three of these avodahs must be done not (only) from the perspective of the recognition of the created being, but from the perspective of the Creator.

## 24

### Sicha 2

A. We are taught in Maseches Zevachim: "Before the Mishkan was erected, private altars (*bamos*) were permitted... and all animals were fit to be offered: cattle, wild animals, and birds, males and females, both unblemished and blemished. For the verse states: 'And Noach built an altar to Hashem, and he took of every clean animal and of every clean bird.' This excludes an animal missing a limb (*mechusar eiver*), which was forbidden to the Bnei Noach. And [regarding the verse] 'and of all the living, of all flesh,' the Torah says: bring an animal that has the heads of its limbs intact." In other words, although for a Ben Noach there is no prohibition against offering a blemished animal when sacrificing on a *bamah* (and similarly regarding sacrifices before the giving of the Torah), there is nevertheless a prohibition against offering an animal that is *mechusar eiver*.

Seemingly, this implies that "blemished" (*baal mum*) and "missing a limb" (*mechusar eiver*) are two distinct categories and prohibitions. A "mum" refers to any blemish whatsoever—to the extent that there are blemishes that do not involve the loss of a limb, but merely a deviation from the standard form of a limb. *Mechusar eiver*, by contrast, means exactly what it says: a deficiency.

However, it appears to me that this is not the case, based on a precise reading of the Gemara (in the story of Kamtza and Bar Kamtza). When the Caesar sent a sacrifice for the Jews to offer on the altar, "while it was being brought, he inflicted a blemish upon it in the lip, or some say in the eyelid—a place that for us is a blemish, but for them is not a blemish." (Rashi explains: "For them, it was not a blemish for offering to the Most High on their *bamah*, only [if it were] *mechusar eiver*.") From the precision of the phrase "for them it is not a blemish" (rather than "it is not a disqualification" or similar), it is implied that even for a Ben Noach, the disqualification of *mechusar eiver* exists precisely because it falls under the category of "blemished." It is just that for them, only *mechusar eiver* is considered "blemished" (and if it is not missing a limb, it is considered *tamim*—whole), whereas for us, even [a blemish] "in the eyelid" is considered a *mum*.

This can be explained based on what is discussed at length elsewhere: that there are several levels and modes regarding the concept of wholeness (*shleimus*) and integrity (*temimus*). This is similar to the teaching of our Sages regarding the verse "a full year" (*shanah temimah*): "This includes the leap month." Even though a regular year is also called a year (a complete one) in which nothing is missing, it is still not a "full year" (*shanah temimah*); this title belongs only to a leap year, which contains thirteen months. This is because the term *temimah* denotes not only the absence of deficiency, but

## 25

an additional integrity and wholeness (which, even without it, the thing is not "missing" anything).

We find these two aspects in the matter of the sacrifices (of the Jews) themselves. In the words of the Rambam: "It is a positive commandment that all sacrifices be *temimim* (unblemished) and *muvcharim* (choice)." These are two distinct laws. *Temimim* means the sacrifice must be whole; furthermore, it must be *muvchar* ("the choice of your vows"). As the Rambam explains, there are conditions required for a sacrifice to be *muvchar* (even though, without them, it is not considered *baal mum*).

According to this, we can say that this is the distinction between the sacrifices of the Jews and the sacrifices of the Bnei Noach: In the sacrifices of the Jews (after the giving of the Torah), a higher level of wholeness and integrity is required, such that even a defect (or deviation) in the limb itself is considered a deficiency and a *mum*. By contrast, regarding the wholeness and integrity required for the sacrifices of the Bnei Noach (and likewise for the sacrifices of everyone before the giving of the Torah), only *mechusar eiver* falls under the category of *mum* (the opposite of wholeness), while a *mum* or defect in the limb itself "is not a *mum*."

B. In this light, we can explain the words of the Rambam regarding bird sacrifices, where he writes: "Blemishes do not disqualify a bird... for the term 'unblemished male' was said only regarding cattle. When does this apply? To minor blemishes. But a bird whose wing has withered, or whose eye has been blinded, or whose leg has been severed is forbidden for the altar, for we do not offer anything that is missing [a limb] at all."

Now, this law (that *mechusar eiver* is disqualified even in a bird) is derived in the Gemara (and likewise in the Toras Kohanim) from the verse "of the bird, and not the entire bird." Based on this, there is room to say that the law of *temimus* (wholeness) does not apply to birds at all, as the simple reading of the Beraisa states: "There is no *temimus*... in birds." Rather, there is a special derivation to exclude *mechusar eiver* even in a bird (as the Minchas Chinuch discusses at length, and see there for the practical halachic differences).

However, the Rambam is clear: the reason *mechusar eiver* is disqualified in a bird is (not because of the derivation of "of the bird," but) because "we do not offer anything that is missing [a limb] at all." This law (a) is not unique to birds but is a general rule for every sacrifice, and (b) is learned (as the Rambam wrote previously) from the verse "they shall be *temimim* for you." This means that even the disqualification of *mechusar eiver* in a bird is rooted in the law of *temimus*.

## 26

Following this, it is difficult to understand: since a bird sacrifice is also included in "they shall be *temimim* for you," what is the explanation for why it is not subject to the law of *tamim* that excludes a *baal mum*?

Based on the above, we can say that the Rambam holds that *baal mum* and *mechusar eiver* are not two separate categories, where only one of them (missing a limb) applies to a bird. Rather, the standard of *temimus* required for a bird excludes only *mechusar eiver* and not a mere *mum* (similar to the aforementioned regarding the sacrifices of Bnei Noach and sacrifices before the giving of the Torah).

The explanation for this:
A cattle sacrifice is a "wealthy" sacrifice compared to a bird sacrifice (as per the law of the *olah v'yored* sacrifice, where the poor person brings a bird). Now, the definition of "wealth" is not merely the filling of a deficiency (for the wealthy person is not lacking anything), but an additional wholeness. As our Sages said regarding the mitzvah of *tzedakah*: "You are commanded to provide for his needs, but you are not commanded to make him wealthy." This means that although one is obligated to fill all the needs of the poor person [up to "that which he lacks," including "even a horse to ride upon and a servant to run before him," if he is of noble birth and accustomed to such], there is no obligation to make him wealthy. Thus, wealth is an additional wholeness, beyond the mere filling of a deficiency.

Therefore, regarding a bird sacrifice (the sacrifice of a poor person), only *mechusar eiver* constitutes a deficiency that contradicts the concept of *temimus*, while a minor defect "is not a *mum*," and the sacrifice is included in "they shall be *temimim* for you." Only in a cattle sacrifice—a wealthy sacrifice—does even a minor defect remove it from the category of *tamim*.

C. Based on this point—that the fact that a mere *mum* (without a missing limb) is not a disqualification in sacrifices before the giving of the Torah (and similarly after the giving of the Torah in the bird sacrifice of a Jew) is not because the law of *temimus* is absent, but because the level of wholeness and integrity required is not compromised by a mere *mum*—we can resolve (according to the inner dimension of these matters) the question of the Tosafos regarding the language of the Gemara where Rabbi Akiva permits blemished animals if "they have ascended, they shall not descend." This is only "in the case of a blemish in the eyelid, since they are fit in birds." Tosafos asks: "This is difficult, for there are many blemishes that do not disqualify [that are not *mechusar eiver*], so why does it specify a blemish in the eyelid? And likewise in Gittin [in the story of Kamtza and Bar Kamtza], he could have inflicted a blemish in another place" (rather than specifically in the eyelid or the lip), and they remained with the question.

Based on the above, we can say that with this example, the Gemara hints that the inner content of the additional wholeness that was innovated by the giving of the Torah regarding sacrifices—because of which "any *mum*" is excluded (in the cattle sacrifice of a Jew)—is specifically related to the sense of sight.

## 27

D. This will be understood based on what is written in the Chinuch regarding the root of the commandment "that every sacrifice be *tamim*": "With what we have prefaced... regarding the sacrifices, on the level of *peshat*, that they are to awaken and direct the thoughts of man toward the Holy One, Blessed be He. For man is influenced by the power of his actions; therefore, it is fitting that the sacrifice be without blemish, for the thoughts of men will not rest or expand upon a lowly species as they do upon a valuable one, for hearts are awakened by that which is valuable and whole in its species more than otherwise."

This can be explained more deeply based on the words of Rabbeinu Bachya regarding the term *korban* (sacrifice), which comes from the word *kiruv* (drawing near). The matter of sacrifices "is the drawing near of the powers and the *middos*" to Hashem. Based on this, we can say that the fact that a sacrifice must be *tamim* without blemish is because it must "awaken and direct the thoughts of man" to draw all of his powers and senses "to the Holy One, Blessed be He."

This is why, for a Ben Noach, a "blemish in the eyelid" is not in the category of a *mum*, because for a Ben Noach, the matter of "sight" in Divinity is not required.

Explanation of the matter:
Although even for a Ben Noach there is the matter of faith in the Creator of the world—as they call Him "the God of gods"—there is nevertheless a fundamental difference between the faith of a Ben Noach and the faith of the Jewish people, to the extent that according to many opinions, Bnei Noach are not warned against *shituf* (associating other powers with Hashem).

The point of the difference is: For a Jew, the matter of faith is a result of the fact that they possess "a part of G-d from above, truly," where "Hashem is One" shines within their soul. Since the foundation of faith is not in the existence of the believer (his intellect and understanding), but rather that "Hashem is One" shines and is revealed in his soul, it is understood that in him is revealed the true oneness of the Holy One, Blessed be He—"Hashem is One" and "there is nothing else besides Him." By contrast, for a Ben Noach, who does not possess a holy soul, their entire faith in G-d is a result of their perception and grasp of Divinity. Since it is founded upon their own existence, this faith does not (necessarily) exclude another existence (or even another power) besides the Holy One, Blessed be He. For this reason, they were not warned against *shituf*, because the faith required of them (according to their capacity, as they do not possess "a part of G-d from above

## 28

truly") is only that the Holy One, Blessed be He, "is the God of gods."

E. The difference between these two modes is similar to the difference between the two senses of hearing and sight. The definition of sight is that the object seen is revealed and shines as it is to the person seeing; that is, the perception of the object is not according to the parameters of the seer, but rather the object seen shines to him. By contrast, hearing is from a distance; when a person hears about a certain thing, his perception of the thing is by hearing detail after detail, and through this, the thing is depicted in his intellect. It is understood that this is not a revelation of the thing as it is, but according to the perception of his intellect and understanding.

So it is regarding faith. For a Ben Noach, for whom "Hashem is One" does not shine and reveal itself, but who has the capacity to reach faith in Hashem through intellectual proofs, etc., this is the category of "hearing." Whereas for a Jew, for whom "Hashem is One" shines in their soul, the matter of faith for them is in the category of "sight."

This is also the hint in the language of the Gemara, that the example of a *mum* that does not disqualify in sacrifices before the giving of the Torah (and sacrifices of Bnei Noach after the giving of the Torah on their own altars) is a "blemish in the eyelid." For the matter of "sight" in Divinity was effected in the Jewish people (primarily) at the time of the giving of the Torah, as it is written: "You have been shown to know that Hashem is the G-d; there is nothing else besides Him"—"shown truly, with sensory sight." Therefore, when the Jewish people offer a sacrifice to Hashem, it is required that their service of sacrifice be with all their powers and senses, including the sense of sight—sight in Divinity. By contrast, for a Ben Noach, for whom there is no sight in Divinity, "a blemish in the eyelid... is not a *mum*."

[And similarly among the Jewish people themselves: one who is at the beginning of his service, in a state of being "poor"—and there is no poverty but in knowledge—and he lacks the knowledge of Divinity in the manner of "you have been shown to know," in the manner of sight—for him, a blemish in the eyelid (in a bird) is not considered a *mum*.

On the other hand, since the revelation of the giving of the Torah acted also upon the Bnei Noach, to the extent that their fulfillment of the seven Noahide laws must be "because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah... through Moshe Rabbeinu," therefore, even for them, there exists at least a semblance of this faith. This is why a Ben Noach can bring sacrifices on the altar in the Beis Hamikdash (of the Jews), and in that case, his sacrifice must be truly *tamim*.]

F. Just as it is regarding the revelation of the oneness of Hashem at the giving of the Torah, whereby the matter of *shituf* was excluded (even though in the state before the giving of the Torah (and likewise for a Ben Noach after the giving of the Torah), even the faith in the manner of "God of gods" has no defect)—so too is it after the giving of the Torah. Regarding the oneness of Hashem itself, a deeper mode of His oneness was revealed in the inner dimension of the Torah (based on the Zohar's explanation of "Hashem is the G-d"), to the extent that not only is there no other power besides Him, but furthermore, there is no existence besides Him (and this is the meaning of "there is nothing else besides Him"—that there is no existence without Him). And within this itself, throughout the generations, deeper modes are revealed from time to time in the inner dimension of the Torah, in the understanding of the oneness of Hashem in greater subtlety.

Although before this understanding of the oneness of Hashem was revealed, one cannot say that the lack of knowledge of this is a matter of deficiency or defect, Heaven forbid, nevertheless, after these explanations of the true meaning of the oneness of Hashem have already been revealed in the world, one who does not study and believe in them has a defect in his faith in Hashem, and this is, in the subtlest of subtleties, a matter of heresy in the oneness of Hashem.

This is similar to what we find in the Rambam, who ruled as halacha that "one who says there is one Master, but that He is a body and has a form" is a *min* (heretic). The Raavad objected: "Why did he call this a *min*? Many greater and better than he followed this line of thought, according to what they saw in the Scriptures and even more so in the Aggadic texts that confuse the mind." The explanation of the Rambam's position is that even though there were "great and good" people who thought that the Holy One, Blessed be He, is "a body and has a form," and Heaven forbid to call them *minim*, nevertheless, after the explanations, etc., have been revealed and publicized in the books of the great men of Israel, that it is impossible to say so and that it contradicts the oneness of Hashem (as the Rambam explained in Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah), then from now on, one who thinks so because of the literal meaning of the Scriptures or what he saw in Aggadic texts is in the category of a *min*.

So too is it in our case: after the true meaning of the oneness of Hashem has been revealed in the inner dimension of the Torah—that there is no existence besides Him—one who does not believe in this is, in the subtlest of subtleties, a matter of heresy in His oneness. As mentioned above, one cannot bring any proof from the fact that in the generations before the revelation of the inner dimension of the Torah, this meaning of the oneness of Hashem was not known, and therefore it was possible for faith in Hashem to be complete even without this knowledge—for before this revelation in the world, there was no defect in the lack of this knowledge, Heaven forbid. But on the other hand, when "He has made everything beautiful in its time" and the inner dimension of the Torah has been revealed, including the deeper modes in the understanding of His oneness, which go on being revealed from generation to generation, it is an obligation upon every Jew to study these matters and believe in them.

## 30

The study of these matters is a direct preparation for "that time" when "the Jewish people will be great sages and know the hidden things, and will attain knowledge of their Creator to the extent of human capacity, as it is said: 'For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea.'"

(From the Sichos of 10 Shevat 5713, 19 Kislev 5712)

## Sicha 3

### 31

A. "And Hashem said, 'This is the sign of the covenant... for all generations'". It is stated in Midrash Rabbah: "'For the generations' is written [defectively, without the letter vav], to exclude two generations [in which the sign of the rainbow was not seen in the world]: the generation of Chizkiyahu and the generation of the Anshei Knesses HaGedolah. Rabbi Chizkiyahu removes the generation of the Anshei Knesses HaGedolah and brings in the generation of Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai."

Rashi on this verse brings this teaching, stating: "'For the generations' — written defectively, because there are generations that did not require the sign because they were completely righteous, such as the generation of Chizkiyahu, King of Yehudah, and the generation of Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai."

Commentators have already noted that in the Midrash, only these two generations are excluded—"to exclude two generations"—meaning that only in those two was there no need for the sign of the rainbow [and this is also evident from the fact that "Rabbi Chizkiyahu removes the generation of the Anshei Knesses HaGedolah and brings in the generation of Rashbi", for simply put, the reason Rabbi Chizkiyahu felt the need to remove the generation of the Anshei Knesses HaGedolah (rather than simply adding the generation of Rashbi as well) is because there were more than two generations]. Rashi, by contrast, writes "because there are generations that did not require [the sign]" (and does not write "two generations"), because according to the *peshuto shel mikra*—Rashi's approach—it is not necessarily limited to a specific number of generations [and what Rashi writes afterward, "such as the generation of Chizkiyahu... Rashbi" (mentioning only two), is cited as an example; it is possible according to the *peshuto shel mikra* that there were more, as indicated by his phrasing "such as the generation of Chizkiyahu... Rashbi"].

They explain that this difference depends on how the word "l'doros" (for the generations) is interpreted: The Midrash expounds upon the deficiency of the two vavs, and therefore says "to exclude two generations." Rashi, however, learns this from the fact that it is "written defectively," meaning that the general deficiency in the word comes to teach us that there is a "deficiency" in "for all generations", as not all generations are included, since there are several generations that did not require the sign of the rainbow.

Seemingly, it is logical to say that in addition to

## 32

their disagreement regarding the interpretation of the verses—whether "l'doros" excludes only two generations or an indefinite number of generations ("there are generations")—there is also a difference between these two approaches in terms of the content of the matter, as follows.

B. It can be said that the Midrash and Rashi differ on the reason why these generations did not require the sign, which can be explained in two ways: 1) It is due to the merit of the tzaddikim in that generation, who protect the world. 2) It is due to the state of all the people of the generation, who, by virtue of their own righteousness, do not require the sign of the rainbow, as they are not deserving of punishment to begin with.

Rashi explicitly states, "because there are generations that did not require the sign because they were completely righteous," where the word "were" refers back to the word "generations," meaning the generations themselves were completely righteous. From this, it is understood that the examples Rashi brings afterward, "such as the generation of Chizkiyahu, King of Yehudah, and the generation of Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai," are not intended to emphasize the greatness of the righteousness of these two tzaddikim, but rather that the people of Chizkiyahu's generation and the people of Rashbi's generation were completely righteous. As we find explicitly in Chazal regarding the generation of Chizkiyahu, that in his days "they searched from Dan to Beer Sheva and did not find an *am ha'aretz*... they did not find a boy or girl, man or woman, who was not expert in the laws of *tumah* and *taharah*, and of that generation it is said..." — and this is the reason they did not require the sign.

[And even though this came about through Chizkiyahu the King (as recounted in Chazal there), nevertheless, what is relevant here (the reason they did not need the sign) is not the greatness of Chizkiyahu's merit in this, but the fact that, in practice, the people of his generation were completely righteous.]

However, the Midrash holds that the reason these two generations were excluded from the sign of the rainbow is due to the greatness of the righteousness of Chizkiyahu and (the Anshei Knesses HaGedolah, or) Rashbi themselves. This is also understood from the continuation of the Midrash, which recounts the words of Rashbi to Eliyahu HaNavi regarding Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, that since the rainbow appeared in his days, he is not worthy of seeing his face. From this, it is understood that the virtue of not seeing the rainbow in the days of Rashbi relates to Rashbi himself (and not to the people of his generation), that due to the greatness of Rashbi's righteousness, the rainbow did not appear in his days. This is also understood from the fact that the Midrash continues to explain several other virtues and praises of Rashbi, as these praises add explanation to the reason the rainbow did not appear in his days.

[It is also precise that one of the virtues of Rashbi brought in the Midrash there is the words of Rashbi that his merit is so great that he can sustain all generations "until the King Mashiach" (in the words of Rashbi brought in the Gemara, "I am able to exempt the entire world from judgment") — which is consistent with the fact that in his generation the rainbow did not appear, for even if they were

## 33

deserving of punishment, the merit of Rashbi protects them and exempts them from judgment, and consequently, they do not need the sign of the rainbow].

C. Based on this, it can be said that this is also the content of the difference between the opinion of the Midrash and the opinion of Rashi on the Torah regarding whether only two generations were excluded, or more than that:

For the Midrash, which holds that only two generations were excluded, this is consistent with its view that the exclusion from the sign of the rainbow comes from the greatness of the righteousness of the tzaddik in that generation, who has the ability to protect the entire world; a tzaddik of such a level is not generally found, and this is self-evident from the fact that only Rashbi said of himself, "I am able to exempt the entire world from judgment," meaning that this is a novelty even relative to other Tannaim and tzaddikim.

Similarly, this is the intent of the Midrash regarding Chizkiyahu, for with him too we find a special wonder, as the Sages say that the Holy One, Blessed be He, wanted to make Chizkiyahu the Mashiach, and since he was worthy of being the redeemer of Israel, it is understood that his merit was great enough to protect the entire world.

In contrast, according to Rashi's view, that the intent is that the generation as a whole were completely righteous (because they were not liable for the punishment of the Flood, as follows), this is not something unique only to the generation of Chizkiyahu and the generation of Rashbi, for there were other such generations (similar to what is brought in Chazal on the verse "False is grace... a woman who fears Hashem, she shall be praised" regarding the generation of Rabbi Yehudah bar Ilai, and others).

D. The root of the disagreement between the Midrash and Rashi (whether the fact that these generations did not require the sign is due to the merit of the tzaddik or the virtue of the people of the generation) can be explained as depending on the definition of the need for the sign of the rainbow.

To preface, there is a seemingly tremendous question on this teaching of Chazal, that only in a few generations did they not require the sign of the rainbow: The "sign" of the rainbow is, as explicitly stated in the verse, to remember the covenant that "never again shall there be a flood to destroy all flesh". Simply put, the need for the sign exists only when there is room for the punishment of a flood (due to evil conduct, etc.), yet despite this, there will not be a flood—because of the promise of the Holy One, Blessed be He.

It is puzzling: How can it be said that only when there are "completely righteous people" (as Rashi puts it) is there no need for the sign of the rainbow? Even if they are not completely righteous, and even if they are wicked, as long as they are not in the state of the people of the generation of the Flood—where the world was at its lowest state, "the wickedness of man was great

## 34

upon the earth...", to the point that "the earth was corrupt... all flesh had corrupted its way upon the earth"—there is no room for the punishment of a flood [as we see that before the Flood, even though all the generations before it "were angering and coming", and already in the generation of Enosh (son of Seth) they worshipped idols, nevertheless, there was no such punishment of destroying the entire world]—and if so, how can it be said that only in generations that "were completely righteous" were they not in need of the sign of the rainbow?

E. According to the *drash* (homiletical) approach, one can answer that it does not mean that every time we see a rainbow, it is a sign that the world is in a state deserving of the punishment of a flood and the world is saved only by the merit of the promise of the Holy One, Blessed be He (mentioned by the sign of the rainbow). Rather, the Holy One, Blessed be He, established the sign of the rainbow in the nature of creation permanently, as an eternal remembrance, that even if the world were in such a low state that it would be deserving of the punishment of a flood, we are promised (by an oath "I will not add" and "I will not add") that "there will not be another flood to destroy the earth." Therefore, the rainbow appears even in generations that are not deserving of the punishment of a flood.

This is also the foundation for the opinion of the Midrash, that the generations in which the rainbow was not seen was due to the merit of the tzaddikim of that generation (the merit of Chizkiyahu the King and the merit of Rashbi)—and not because of the greatness of the righteousness of the people of the generation:

The reason for the absence of the rainbow in those days is not because only those generations were not in a state deserving of the punishment of a flood—that is certainly not the case; on the contrary, most, if not all, generations are not deserving of the punishment of a flood, Heaven forbid. Rather, the fact that the rainbow did not appear in their days comes to indicate the greatness of the merit of the tzaddik present in that generation, that even if one were to imagine that his generation was in a low state like the generation of the Flood, and there was no promise that "there will not be another flood," nevertheless, by his merit, they would not be punished (as the aforementioned statement of Rashbi, "I am able to exempt the entire world from judgment").

However, in Rashi on the Torah, it is impossible to explain it this way, because on the verse "And it shall be, when I bring clouds over the earth, and the rainbow is seen in the cloud," he explains, "When I bring clouds — when it arises in My thought to bring darkness and destruction to the world". From this, it is understood that according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, the fact that "the rainbow is seen in the cloud" is a result of the fact that it arose in His thought "to bring darkness and destruction to the world."

[For while in what is written afterward, "And the

## 35

rainbow shall be in the cloud, and I will see it to remember the eternal covenant," it is possible to explain that the rainbow in the cloud is a constant matter, but when it arises in His thought to bring darkness and destruction to the world, then the seeing of the rainbow ("and I will see it") will be effective, so that the Holy One, Blessed be He, will, as it were, remember the eternal covenant. This is not the case in the verse "And it shall be, when I bring clouds...", where it says "and the rainbow is seen in the cloud" (and not "and I will see it...").]

And the question remains: How is it possible to say that throughout all the generations (except for the few generations in which the rainbow was not seen) the world was in such a state that it arose in the thought of the Almighty "to bring darkness and destruction to the world" (like the generation of the Flood)?!

F. It seems possible to say regarding this—that according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, the definition of the punishment of the Flood that was negated by the promise of the Holy One, Blessed be He, is not only the negation of bringing a flood upon the entire world, but even upon a part of the earth [and the meaning of "nor shall all flesh be cut off", "nor shall there be another flood to destroy all flesh"—does not mean all flesh together ("all" in the sense of the totality of things), but "all flesh" means even one of the "flesh" ("all" in the sense of "any," like "you shall not do any work", "and no man shall be in the Tent of Meeting", and more)].

For the punishment of the Flood is different from other punishments recounted in the Torah, for regarding the Flood it is said, "And He wiped out all existence that was upon the face of the earth," which indicates a matter of absolute destruction, and the reason for this is explicitly stated in the text, because the Flood did not come only to punish the people of the generation of the Flood, but because "Hashem regretted that He had made man... and Hashem said, 'I will wipe out man... for I regret that I have made them,'" meaning that the Holy One, Blessed be He, regretted, as it were, the very creation of man (and other creatures), and therefore came to wipe out and destroy the creation He had made.

This punishment is a punishment of a special type. There are severe punishments, even punishments of annihilation, and even in a way where not only the species of man is destroyed but also the animals (like the punishment of an *ir hanidachas*), and even where the land itself is destroyed, similar to the overturning of Sedom and Amorah. But nevertheless, all of this is only by way of punishment, where the people of the place sinned so much in the eyes of Hashem that they became liable for destruction in that place (and even where there is a need to destroy their place of residence). But in all of this, the language of "wiping out" and regret (repentance) over the very creation of them is not said, as we find regarding the Flood.

And this is also the reason for the main difference between the Flood and the overturning of Sedom, for regarding the overturning of Sedom

## 38

I. The inner reason why, according to the Midrash, the rainbow was not seen only in the two generations of Chizkiyahu the King and Rashbi [and Rashi also specifically cited these two generations (even though according to Rashi there were other such generations)]—can be said to be:

It is explained in many places that the Flood came "to purify the earth," which is also the intent of Chazal that Noach "saw a new world," because through the Flood, a renewal was made in the work of creation, that the world was purified and elevated to a new level, to the point that it is called a "new world."

And this is the matter of the sign of the rainbow, that "there will not be another flood to destroy the earth"—to indicate that through the Flood, the earth was purified in such a way that it is no longer possible for the world to be in such a low state that it would be deserving of a flood.

Now, such a renewal in creation will be at the coming of Mashiach Tzidkeinu, when there will be "the new heavens and the new earth".

And this is also the hint that specifically with Chizkiyahu the King and Rashbi we find that they did not require the sign of the rainbow, because both of them are connected to the coming of the Mashiach: Regarding Chizkiyahu, it is said that the Holy One, Blessed be He, wanted to make him Mashiach (as above), and likewise, Rashbi authored the Sefer HaZohar, through which "they will emerge from exile with mercy".

It turns out that through their service, they were worthy of bringing about a renewal in creation, and therefore "they did not require the sign" of the rainbow, since they were able to bring about the renewal of creation (similar to the renewal brought about by the Flood) through their own service.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Noach, 5745)


## לך לך

## Sicha 1

## 43

"I do not need its light... it is a testimony to all who dwell in the world that the Shechinah dwells in Yisrael" — this is in the category of a "possession" (*kinyan*), which did not change in its essence or its stature through its expansion outward.

And this is why, regarding the Beis Hamikdash, he also brings two verses: "The place for Your dwelling which You have made, Hashem..." and it says, "And He brought them to His holy border, this mountain which His right hand acquired (*kanah*)." The first verse, "The place for Your dwelling," speaks of the holiness of the Mikdash itself, while the second verse, "And He brought them to His holy border, this mountain which His right hand acquired," refers to the expansion of the holiness of the Mikdash outward, to the entire Har Habayis ("this mountain"), and even to the entire land of Eretz Yisrael, which is called "His holy border" (because of the holiness of the Mikdash within it).

Similarly, regarding "Heaven and earth are one possession": the matter of the "possession" of the Ribono shel Olam is not only in the spiritual heavens and earth (as they are "My throne... and My footstool"), but this extends also to the physical heavens and earth. For in them there are matters of the revelation of Elokus (which is above nature), similar to the fact that the hosts of heaven are "as strong as the day they were created," where this eternity is a revelation of the power of the Ein Sof, and likewise the power of growth in the earth, which is a revelation of the power of the Ein Sof whose power and ability alone can bring forth *yesh mei'ayin*.

Therefore, here too, the Tanna was not satisfied with bringing the verse "Heaven is My throne and the earth is My footstool," but adds a second proof, "And it says, 'How many are Your works... the earth is full of Your possessions,'" for even in this lower earth, the "possession" of heaven and earth is drawn down. 

5. Based on the above, we can also explain the reason why Avraham is listed here as a "possession" in his own right, and is not included in the general "possession" of "Yisrael," and brings as proof the verse "And He blessed him... Possessor of heaven and earth":

It is explained in the Midrash that the reason Avraham is called "Possessor of heaven and earth" is because Avraham made the Ribono shel Olam known among the creatures, through which "he caused the Ribono shel Olam to possess heaven and earth." Therefore, the Ribono shel Olam said to him, "I credit you as if you were My partner in the creation of the world" (as if Avraham himself is a "possessor of heaven and earth"). This is to say that the mission of Avraham is to reveal the unity of the Ribono shel Olam within the world itself, so that the creatures will recognize Hashem.

And therefore, Avraham is specified as a "possession" in his own right. For the fact that "Yisrael is one possession" refers to the soul of every individual Yisrael, which, even upon its descent into this world and its enclothement in a physical body, remains the "possession" of the Ribono shel Olam, as explained at length above. But the fact that "Avraham is one possession" is from the perspective of his activity in the world, as emphasized by the verse brought as proof, that Avraham is called "Possessor of heaven and earth." For the fact that Avraham was able to effect the recognition of Elokus among the creatures even before the giving of the Torah indicates the greatness of the wonder of Avraham and the manner of his unification with his Creator, "El Elyon," to the extent that not only...

## 45

### Sicha 2

a. Before the commandment of *bris milah* in our *parsha* — "And you shall keep My covenant... This is My covenant, which you shall keep... every male among you shall be circumcised" — the promise was made to Avraham : "And I will give to you and to your descendants after you the land of your sojournings, the entire land of Canaan, as an eternal possession."

The implication of the verses is that the inheritance of the land is dependent upon the mitzvah of *milah*. This is explicitly stated in the *drashah* of our Sages at the beginning of the *parsha* of *milah*: "Walk before Me and be perfect" — "Walk before Me through the mitzvah of *milah*"; "And I will establish My covenant between Me and you" — "A covenant of love and a covenant of the land, to bequeath it to you through this mitzvah." In *Bereishis Rabbah*, regarding this verse : "If your children fulfill the *milah*, they enter the land; if not, they do not enter the land... Thus said the *Ribono shel Olam* to Avraham Avinu: 'And I will give to you and to your descendants after you...' on the condition: 'And you shall keep My covenant.'" This is also explained in the *Zohar* *Va'era* : "Whoever is circumcised inherits the land."

This requires understanding: Was the land of Eretz Yisrael not already promised to Avraham Avinu in the *Bris Bein HaBesarim* (Covenant between the Pieces), as it is written : "On that day, Hashem made a covenant with Avram, saying: 'To your descendants I have given this land...'" ? What was added by this promise, which is dependent upon the mitzvah of *bris milah* ?

Even if, on the face of it, there is a novelty in this promise — that it is specified that the giving of the land is "as an eternal possession" — aside from the fact that it requires explanation why specifically this detail is dependent upon the fulfillment of the mitzvah of *milah*, according to the literal meaning, the previous promise was also "as an eternal possession," for it was given via a "covenant," and a covenant is eternal and everlasting, without interruption.

b. Seemingly, one could explain that the novelty in this promise is, as the *Midrash* implies, "If your children fulfill the *milah*, they enter the land." That is to say, the subject here is not the giving of the land to Yisrael as an "eternal possession" (for that had already occurred in the *Bris Bein HaBesarim*), but rather the actual entry of Yisrael into the land, which is dependent upon the fulfillment of the mitzvah of *milah*. One could also seemingly infer this from the aforementioned language of the *Zohar*, "Whoever is circumcised inherits the land," that it is not speaking of the inheritance of the land by the people of Yisrael in general, which was accomplished in the *Bris Bein HaBesarim*, but rather the merit of each individual in his portion of the land, which is achieved at the time of the actual entry into the land.

However, this cannot be the interpretation, for the *Zohar* there says this regarding the explicit verse : "And I have also established My covenant with them, to give them the land of Canaan," stating that "Whoever is circumcised inherits the land," meaning that the giving and inheritance of Eretz Yisrael is dependent upon and was achieved through the merit of *milah* — that the giving of the land to the entirety of Yisrael (and not merely the entry into the land and its giving to each individual) is dependent upon *milah*.

c. This can be understood by prefacing it with what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote in his notes on the *Zohar* there. He writes: "'And he inherits' (*yoresh*), as it is written in the verse : 'They shall inherit (*yirshu*) the land' — an inheritance (*yerushah*) specifically, for an inheritance has no interruption. For this is through *milah*, and *milah*, once one is circumcised, has no interruption, for it is impossible that he should ever again be uncircumcised, and this remains with him forever without interruption. Therefore, likewise, his taking of the land is an inheritance that has no interruption." He continues: "And as for the fact that Yisrael was exiled from their land, and this is surely not an inheritance that has no interruption?" He explains this in two ways: (1) "This is only for a duration of time, and afterwards they will return." (2) "Furthermore, this is due to sins and iniquities, through which the *orlah* is drawn back, similar to one who cohabits with an Aramean woman, whose *orlah* is drawn back and he no longer appears circumcised, *chas v'shalom*. This is the meaning of the acronym of *milah* (*Mem-Yud-Lamed-Heh*): *matanah yesh lah hefsek* (a gift that has an interruption)".

Seemingly, this requires clarification: From the simple reading of his words in the second explanation, it implies that only according to this explanation is the exile categorized as an "interruption" ("a gift that has an interruption"). Conversely, according to the first explanation (that "this is only for a duration of time, and afterwards they will return"), the exile is not called an interruption, because since it is "only for a duration of time," even during the time of exile it is their land and in their possession, and the inheritance of the land has not been interrupted from them. But if so, why does he explain in the second answer that it is an interruption? Even an exile that comes as a result of sins and iniquities does not nullify the ownership of the land, as we say: "Because of our sins we were exiled from our land and distanced from our soil." Even when they were exiled from the land "because of our sins," it remains our land and our soil [and according to some opinions, even one who has no land can write in a deed, "I have acquired for him four cubits," because "there is no person in Yisrael who does not have four cubits in Eretz Yisrael"]. Why then does he state in the second explanation that it is a gift that has an interruption?

Therefore, it must be that both are true, and there are two levels and modes, both in Eretz Yisrael and in the mitzvah of *milah*: (a) As it is in the mode of an "inheritance" that has no interruption, in which case an interruption for a time is not considered an interruption. (b) As it is in the mode of a "gift (that) has an interruption."

d. This can be explained based on what we find regarding *milah*. In addition to the fact that every Jew, even a Jewish man who cannot fulfill the mitzvah of *milah* in practice ("such as when his brothers died due to the *milah*," etc.), is called "circumcised", we also find different *halachos* regarding one who draws his *orlah*: The *Rambam* writes: "Anyone who breaks the covenant of Avraham Avinu and leaves his *orlah* or draws it..." — meaning that according to the *Rambam*, drawing the *orlah* is included in "breaking the covenant of Avraham Avinu." On the other hand, it is stated in the *Gemara*, and the *Rambam* rules accordingly : "One who is drawn (*mashuch*) is permitted to eat *terumah*, even though he appears as an *arel*." (As for the conclusion, "The Sages decreed that he must be circumcised a second time until he appears circumcised," many commentators on the *Rambam* explain that this is only for the sake of the mitzvah, and it does not prevent the eating of *terumah*, even according to the Sages). From this it is proven that even though he appears as an *arel*, he is not an *arel* and is not called an *arel*, which is why he is permitted to eat *terumah*.

From the continuation of the *Gemara*, which questions this from the *Baraita* that states, "'He has broken My covenant' — to include the *mashuch*," and answers that this is only "by Rabbinic decree, and the verse is merely an *asmachta*," it is proven that by Torah law he is not included in "He has broken My covenant" [and according to the above regarding the *Rambam*'s view, that even by Rabbinic decree he is not an *arel* and is permitted to eat *terumah* (and the requirement to be circumcised a second time is only because he appears as an *arel*), it turns out that even by Rabbinic decree he is not in the category of "My covenant he has broken"].

e. It is proven from all this that in the covenant of *milah* there are two categories: "The covenant of Avraham Avinu," in which a breach is possible through human action, such that through his deed he breaks the covenant; and "My covenant" of the *Ribono shel Olam*, which, once one is circumcised, it is impossible for a person to nullify or break this covenant. Therefore, a *mashuch* is not called an *arel* regarding the eating of *terumah*.

The explanation for this:
The covenant between the *Ribono shel Olam* and Avraham is different from a "covenant" between two people who connect to love one another. In the covenant between the *Ribono shel Olam* and Avraham (between Creator and created), there is a distinction between the covenant as it is from the side of the *Ribono shel Olam* and the covenant as it is from the side of Avraham. Even though the essence of the covenant is from the side of the *Ribono shel Olam* — from the power of the Creator the covenant is made as an "eternal covenant" — there is also the aspect of the created being: "And you shall keep My covenant."

These are the two aforementioned details in the covenant of *milah*: as it is from the side of the *Ribono shel Olam*, "My covenant," and as it is from the side of Avraham Avinu.

That is to say, regarding the act of establishing the covenant and the connection with the *Ribono shel Olam*, there are two details: as it is from the side of the created being and his action — the covenant of Avraham Avinu — in the revelation of this connection, an interruption is possible, and this is what is meant by "the acronym of *milah* is a gift that has an interruption." Therefore, it is possible for human action to damage this, as a *mashuch* is called "one who breaks the covenant of Avraham Avinu." And as it is from the side of the Creator, which is a connection in which there cannot be an interruption, because it is not a thing done from the side of the created being but from the side of the *Ribono shel Olam*. Therefore, it is called "My covenant," and from this side, even a *mashuch* is not called an *arel*, because no human action can break this covenant.

It can be said that even though both of these matters were given in the covenant of Avraham, more specifically, this is the distinction between the covenant as it was before the Giving of the Torah and as it is after the Giving of the Torah, when we were commanded, "And on the eighth day he shall be circumcised".

As the *Rambam* emphasizes: "And set your heart upon the great principle... that everything we distance ourselves from or do today, we do only by the commandment of the *Ribono shel Olam* through Moshe Rabbeinu, and not because the *Ribono shel Olam* said this to the prophets before him... We do not circumcise because Avraham Avinu circumcised himself and his household, but because the *Ribono shel Olam* commanded us through Moshe Rabbeinu that we should circumcise."

The covenant as it was before the Giving of the Torah, before the nullification of the "decree" that the upper realms shall not descend to the lower realms (as per the well-known *Midrash*), was primarily and openly the covenant from the side of the created being, from the side of Avraham Avinu. Whereas the covenant as it is after the Giving of the Torah is primarily as it is from the side of the *Ribono shel Olam*.

This is similar to the general distinction between the mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah and the mitzvos that the Patriarchs fulfilled before the Torah was given, as our Sages said : "All the mitzvos that the Patriarchs performed before you were merely a fragrance, but we are 'Your name is like oil poured forth'..." In a "fragrance," there are two aspects: (a) It is nothing more than an extension of the thing and not part of the essence of the thing (therefore, through the fragrance, nothing is lacking from the thing itself). (b) After a duration of time, the fragrance dissipates. Likewise, the mitzvos that the Patriarchs fulfilled are compared to a "fragrance," because (a) they did not contain the power of the Creator as the mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah do, for their fulfillment was by their own power. (b) They did not have the power to establish the holiness of the mitzvos eternally in the physical things through which the mitzvos were performed.

From this, it is also understood regarding the mitzvah of *milah*, that even though there was a commandment for it, which means (also) the giving of power from above, nevertheless, since it was before the nullification of the decree that the upper realms shall not descend to the lower realms, its fulfillment before the Giving of the Torah did not have the same strength as the mitzvos after the Giving of the Torah, when we were commanded through "I am Hashem your G-d," *Anochi* — I am He who I am.

f. Based on this, we can explain two expressions we find in the *Midrashim* regarding the matter of *bris milah*:
In *Bereishis Rabbah* it is stated: "Rabbi Levi said: In the time to come, Avraham will sit at the entrance of Gehinnom and will not allow any circumcised person from Yisrael to descend into it. And those who sinned excessively, what does he do to them? He removes the *orlah*... and places it upon them and lowers them into Gehinnom..." In *Shemos Rabbah* it is stated: "That circumcised Jews do not descend into Gehinnom. Rabbi Berachiah said: So that the heretics and the wicked of Yisrael do not say, 'Since we are circumcised, we do not descend into Gehinnom,' what does the *Ribono shel Olam* do? He sends an angel and draws their *orlah*, and they descend into Gehinnom, as it is written..."

One must examine this: Since Avraham Avinu "does not allow any circumcised person from Yisrael to descend into it (Gehinnom)," and "circumcised Jews do not descend into Gehinnom," why do those "who sinned excessively" (and the like) descend into Gehinnom?

Also, one must understand the difference between the two *Midrashim*: in *Bereishis Rabbah* it says that Avraham Avinu himself removes the *orlah* and places it upon them, and in *Shemos Rabbah* it says that an angel draws their *orlah*.

It must be that their actions caused the name "circumcised" to be revoked from them, but there are two modes in this. When they "sinned excessively," it means they broke their covenant with the *Ribono shel Olam*. Therefore, regarding Avraham Avinu and his covenant, they are in the category of an *arel* who belongs to Gehinnom. Thus, Avraham "removes the *orlah*... and places it upon them," meaning that from the side of "Avraham," they are in the category of *arelim*. However, in *Shemos Rabbah*, it refers to the covenant of the *Ribono shel Olam*, from which side even one who sinned "excessively" is not called one who breaks the covenant and does not become an actual *arel*. Only "heretics and the wicked of Yisrael," who are like apostates, *chas v'shalom*, have room to be considered as *arelim* [and furthermore, from the words of the *Midrash*, it is understood that even they are not in the category of actual *arelim* (which is why the angel does not bring an *orlah* and make them actual *arelim*, but only draws their *orlah*, which according to *halacha* is not in the category of actually breaking the covenant), but rather "so that the heretics and the wicked of Yisrael do not say, 'Since we are circumcised, we do not descend into Gehinnom,'" meaning this is only to negate their statement and the permission they take to continue in their behavior of heresy and wickedness].

g. Based on this, we can also explain the matter of the inheritance of Eretz Yisrael:
Eretz Yisrael was given to the people of Yisrael in an eternal covenant by the *Ribono shel Olam* to Avraham Avinu, and it is obvious that there is no possibility of an interruption in any way; it is always Yisrael's, it is in their possession.

But just as there were three modes in the actual giving of the land (to the tribes and families): inheritance (as a *nachalah*), division according to reason and intellect, and division according to the lot, as explained at length elsewhere — that these three matters correspond to the three matters of inheritance, sale, and gift — so too were these three matters (the source of them) in its giving to Avraham Avinu (to the people of Yisrael in general).

The difference between these three matters, simply put, is that a sale is something given to the buyer in exchange for payment, and it is therefore understood that it is in accordance with and proportional to the value of the buyer's payment.

A gift comes primarily from the side of the giver, without payment from the recipient as in a sale, but ultimately the situation and action of the recipient have a relationship and value to the gift, for "if he did not provide satisfaction to the giver, he would not have given him the gift".

Conversely, an inheritance is something that is not dependent at all on the work of the heir or his status and situation, for in whatever situation he is, he inherits everything. The reason may be that an inheritance is not a giving to another, but as the verse says : "Instead of your fathers shall be your children," the heir stands in the place of the bequeather; he is the bequeather, and consequently he has ownership of the bequeather's assets (and there is no change of ownership in an inheritance).

A parallel to these three matters (and more subtly) exists in Eretz Yisrael:
"On that day, Hashem made a covenant with Avram, saying: 'To your descendants I have given this land'" — this covenant was in connection with the decree of the *Bris Bein HaBesarim*: "Know for certain that your descendants will be strangers in a land that is not theirs, and they will enslave them and afflict them for four hundred years... and the fourth generation will return here," which was like a "debt" that the children of Yisrael had to pay for the inheritance of the land. In the language of Rashi in many places, it is a "promissory note" of the decree that "your descendants will be strangers," which is incumbent upon the descendants of Yitzchak (and therefore "And Esav went to a land because of his brother Yaakov", "He said: 'I will go from here; I have no part in the gift that was given to him, nor in the payment of the note'").

However, in the covenant of *milah*, the *Ribono shel Olam* added: "And I will give to you and to your descendants after you the land of your sojournings, the entire land of Canaan, as an eternal possession, and I will be to them a G-d," which is not dependent or connected to the action and work of man.

Yet, within this itself, there are two modes — a mode of gift and a mode of inheritance, and both belong to the mitzvah of *milah*, as is emphasized in the verse in the *parsha*:
At the beginning of the *parsha* it is said: "And I will establish My covenant between Me and you" (which is, as Rashi says, "A covenant of love and a covenant of the land, to bequeath it to you through this mitzvah," as mentioned above) after the commandment (of *milah*) "Walk before Me and be perfect" was stated.
And later in the *parsha*, it precedes with "And I will give to you and to your descendants after you the land of your sojournings, the entire land of Canaan, as an eternal possession," and afterwards continues: "And G-d said to Avraham: 'And you shall keep My covenant... This is My covenant, which you shall keep... every male among you shall be circumcised.'"

It can be said that these two matters in the giving of the land are hinted at: the mode of gift and the mode of inheritance.
Even the first giving mentioned in the *parsha* of *milah*, "to bequeath it to you through this mitzvah," is not in the manner of a condition and debt, but "And I will establish My covenant between Me and you," which is like a gift that comes primarily from the side of the giver, except that it comes to him because of the recipient's ascent to a higher state — he provides satisfaction to the giver — which is the content of the mitzvah of *milah*.

Higher than this is what is said: "And I will give to you and to your descendants after you... as an eternal possession, and I will be to them a G-d." In this statement of giving the land "as an eternal possession," it is said that the *Ribono shel Olam* is the G-d of the children of Yisrael, and this is the covenant of the land in the mode of inheritance, without conditions or connection to the situation of Yisrael, but from the side of their very existence, that they are entirely one with the *Kudsha Brich Hu*. It is just that He adds in a separate statement: "And G-d said to Avraham: 'And you shall keep My covenant.'"

These are the two details in Eretz Yisrael, corresponding to the two definitions in *milah*. From the side of the covenant of Avraham Avinu in the mitzvah of *milah* — that is, as it still relates to the created being and his status and situation — the connection to Eretz Yisrael is in the mode of a gift, for ultimately the status and situation of the recipient plays a role here. Consequently, in a change of status and situation of Yisrael (when it is not in a state of providing satisfaction), an interruption is possible; that is, the connection to the covenant of Avraham Avinu, and consequently to Eretz Yisrael, is not openly apparent.

Therefore, this matter is hinted at in the acronym of the letters of *milah*: *matanah yesh lah hefsek* (a gift that has an interruption). The word *osiyot* (letters) indicates a revelation of the thing; *os* (letter) is from the expression "the morning *ata* (comes)".

But from the side of the covenant of the *Ribono shel Olam* in the mitzvah of *milah*, no nullification or interruption is possible, *chas v'shalom*, since it is entirely from the side of the Creator, and there is no place for the status and situation of man. Therefore, it exists always, in whatever situation a Jew finds himself, for since he was circumcised once, he is in an eternal covenant with the *Ribono shel Olam*. The same is true regarding Eretz Yisrael, in which there is no interruption at all.

This is what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, answers in the first explanation: "This is only for a duration of time, and afterwards they will return." There is no interruption here; rather, on the contrary, this itself expresses and indicates that even in a state where the revelation of the connection of the children of Yisrael is lacking, and therefore Yisrael was exiled for a duration of time, nothing is lacking in their covenant and connection, and they know even then that afterwards they will return.

Just as it was in Egypt, where "by the merit of the covenant of *milah* and the blood of the *Pesach* I redeemed you from Egypt", so too will it be in the future, that "by their merit you are destined to be redeemed in the end", "the *Ribono shel Olam* redeems them by the merit of the *milah*". And very soon, Yisrael, who are called an inheritance, will come to the land that is the eternal inheritance of the children of Yisrael, with the coming of *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*, *b'egla di'dan*.

## Sicha 3

## 53

1. Regarding the source of the law that *milah* overrides *Shabbos*, we find (in general) three derivations: two in the *Gemara* and one in the *Mechilta*.

According to Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak, it is learned through a *gezeirah shavah*: we compare the word "covenant" (*bris*) and "generations" (*doros*) from the context of *Shabbos* (where it is written "for it is a sign", "for their generations, an eternal covenant") to the context of *milah* in our *parsha* (where it is written "and it shall be a sign of a covenant, etc." and "and at eight days old every male among you shall be circumcised for your generations").

According to Rav Yochanan, it is learned from the verse "on the eighth day he shall be circumcised"—meaning, "on the day," even on *Shabbos*.

In the *Mechilta* on the verse "and the children of Israel shall keep the *Shabbos*, to perform the *Shabbos*," it states: "Rabbi Eliezer says: a matter to which a covenant is bound, and what is this? This is *milah*." The commentators write that the *Mechilta* refers to the continuation of the verse, "for their generations, an eternal covenant" —"this is *milah*, which is permitted to be performed on *Shabbos*."

Seemingly, the difference between these three derivations is as follows: according to Rabbi Eliezer in the *Mechilta*, the teaching that *milah* is permitted on *Shabbos* is derived from a verse stated in the *parsha* of *Shabbos*; according to Rav Yochanan in the *Gemara*, the teaching is from the *parsha* of *milah* (in *Parshas Tazria*); while according to Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak, the law is learned from a verse in our *parsha* (via *gezeirah shavah*), in the *parsha* of Avraham Avinu’s *milah*.

## 54

It can be said that the difference in the source of these matters also implies a difference in the definition of the law that *milah* overrides *Shabbos* :

According to the opinion that it is learned from the verse in *Parshas Tazria* ("on the eighth day he shall be circumcised"), it follows that the fact that we circumcise even on *Shabbos* is a law of *milah* (and not a law of *Shabbos*). Even though the prohibitions of *Shabbos* have already been stated (and exist), there is a special law regarding *milah* that it overrides *Shabbos*. According to this approach, it implies that the fact that *milah* overrides *Shabbos* is in the category of *dichuy* (deferral), or perhaps even *hutrah* (permitted)—meaning that in relation to the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* of *milah* on the eighth day, the prohibition of *Shabbos* was permitted.

According to the *Mechilta*, which learns it from the verse in the *parsha* of *Shabbos*, it implies that it is a law of *Shabbos*.

## 55

It can be said that this means it is not merely a condition within the prohibitions of *Shabbos* —that they were not stated to begin with in a place where *milah* is due—but rather that it is a fulfillment of the *mitzvah* of *Shabbos*: "and the children of Israel shall keep the *Shabbos*, to perform the *Shabbos* for their generations, an eternal covenant." One of the ways to "perform the *Shabbos*" is to circumcise on *Shabbos*, because through this they "keep and perform" the (holiness of) *Shabbos*. As is understood simply, for *Shabbos* is (as the verse continues) "between Me and the children of Israel, it is a sign forever", and the fulfillment and perfection of the sign and covenant of *Shabbos* is through the fulfillment of the sign and covenant of *milah*, which is a covenant and sign in the flesh, as it is written "My covenant in your flesh for an eternal covenant".

However, according to the teaching of Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak that the source of this law is from the *parsha* of *milah* stated to Avraham, it follows that the fact that we circumcise on *Shabbos* dates back to the time of Avraham Avinu, before the Torah was given and before the entire *parsha* of *Shabbos* was stated. It can be said that according to this, it is not just a law of *milah* or a law of *Shabbos*, but rather it is above the measurement of the Torah, and above every definition of *Shabbos* —[similar to the language of our Sages : "Great is *milah*, for thirteen covenants were cut over it (whereas over all the commandments of the Torah, only three covenants were cut)... for it overrides *Shabbos*"].

## 56

This can be explained by prefacing the well-known concept that the content of the *mitzvah* of *milah* emphasizes the matter of *mesiras nefesh*. As our Sages said regarding the verse "for Your sake we are killed all the day long"—"Is it possible for a person to be killed every day? Rather, this refers to *milah*". As is explained at length in the *Midrashim*, the *mitzvah* of *milah* is like a sacrifice, and "its power is greater than a sacrifice, for a sacrifice is with one's money, while *milah* is with one's body... and therefore it is considered for him as an *Akeidah* and a sacrifice, as if he bound himself, and it is written 'those who cut My covenant over a sacrifice'." Similarly, it is explained in many places in the *Zohar* that when a person of Israel circumcises his son, it is as if he offered him as a sacrifice.

Now, the matter and power of *mesiras nefesh* that exists in all of Israel is an inheritance to us from our forefathers, as the Alter Rebbe explains in *Tanya*, and specifically from Avraham Avinu, as is known that Avraham "opened the channel" for *mesiras nefesh*.

Seemingly, this requires explanation: the *mesiras nefesh* that exists in Israel is that they sacrifice their lives for the *yichud* and holiness of His Name, blessed be He, to fulfill the Torah and *mitzvos* that were given to every individual of Israel at the Giving of the Torah. How does this come as an inheritance from Avraham, who existed before the Giving of the Torah and before the general matter of Torah and *mitzvos*? [And as it is regarding the *mitzvah* of *milah* in its literal sense, that "we do not circumcise because Avraham Avinu, peace be upon him, circumcised himself and his household, but because the Holy One, blessed be He, commanded us through Moshe Rabbeinu that we should circumcise"].

The explanation for this is:
The innovation of the Giving of the Torah is explained in the *Midrash* : before the Giving of the Torah, there was a decree that the upper realms should not descend to the lower realms, and the lower realms should not ascend to the upper realms. At the Giving of the Torah, this decree was nullified, "and I am the one who initiates," for "Hashem descended upon Mount Sinai", and afterwards "and to Moshe He said, 'Ascend to Hashem'". Therefore, the holiness of Israel and its strength, which is created through the Torah and *mitzvos* after the Giving of the Torah, is entirely above how it was before the Giving of the Torah, before the nullification of the decree.

However, all the definitions of "upper" and "lower" are definitions that belong to the categories of the world. But in this level, which is entirely above the category of upper and lower, it is not applicable to say that a decree exists between upper and lower.

## 57

In the terminology of *Chassidus*, the *Atzmus* (Essence) of the Holy One, blessed be He, is found in the upper and lower realms without any difference at all. Just as it is not applicable to speak of a difference in place, so too it is not applicable to speak of a difference in time—before the Giving of the Torah and after the Giving of the Torah. That which is explained in the *Midrash* that the innovation of the Giving of the Torah is the nullification of the decree between upper and lower realms is in relation to and from the perspective of the definitions of "upper" and "lower." Within the upper and lower realms themselves, the power was given to connect them at the Giving of the Torah, through the Torah and *mitzvos* of the children of Israel.

Based on this, it is understood regarding the children of Israel that the fact that a special holiness applied to them at the Giving of the Torah is from the perspective of the level of "revelation" of the souls of Israel. For from the perspective of the essence of the soul—the fact that the essence of the soul is unified with Him, blessed be He, in such a way that they are, as it were, one thing—it is not applicable to differentiate between before the Giving of the Torah and after the Giving of the Torah. Just as regarding the *Atzmus* of the Holy One, blessed be He, the entire category of the decree is not applicable, so too it is regarding the essence of the children of Israel.

The innovation of the Giving of the Torah is in the level of revelation: that this matter, as it is in its truth from the perspective of the essence of the children of Israel, should be felt in the powers of man as they are clothed in the body in this world.

## 58

Based on this, it is understood that the matter of *mesiras nefesh* of the children of Israel after the Giving of the Torah is an inheritance to us from our forefathers. For Avraham Avinu (and the forefathers in general) points to the level of the essence of the children of Israel, from which perspective all the definitions of upper and lower do not exist, and from this level comes the power of *mesiras nefesh*.

However, at the Giving of the Torah, it was innovated that this level of *mesiras nefesh* is drawn down also into the category of revelations, of upper and lower realms.

It can be said that there are two categories in the matter of *mesiras nefesh*:
1) *Mesiras nefesh* as it is according to the definitions of the Torah, which is in the three transgressions regarding which we were commanded "be killed and do not transgress."
2) *Mesiras nefesh* in general and in all one's matters, that one is always prepared to sacrifice his life to the Holy One, blessed be He, even when he is not obligated to do so according to the law of the Torah. And furthermore, even when according to Torah there are questions and doubts whether it is permitted to sacrifice one's life —this *mesiras nefesh* is from the level of Israel that is above the Torah. As our Sages said, "The thought of Israel preceded everything, even the thought of the Torah."

This is why, according to Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak, we learn *milah* on *Shabbos* from the verses in the *parsha* of *milah* of Avraham, because it speaks of this category in *milah* that is above the commandments of the Torah. From this perspective, there exists in every Jew the nature to sacrifice his life for the *yichud* of His Name, blessed be He, even when he is not obligated to do so according to Torah [and this is also relevant to the matter of *milah* in its literal sense, as it is found in the *sefarim* that the matter of *milah* is "to refine... Israel... and this refinement is a test to see if he will sacrifice his life for the holiness of His Name, blessed be He. And when he accustoms himself from his youth with one of his limbs, it will be easy in his eyes, even when he grows old, to sacrifice his entire soul to Heaven"].

## 59

Since this category in *milah*—the category of *mesiras nefesh* that is above the Torah—is learned from the fact that *milah* overrides *Shabbos*, it must be said that this has an expression also in the actual performance of the *mitzvah* of *milah* on the day of *Shabbos*.

It can be said that this is visible in the very fact that a man of Israel circumcises a young child of Israel on the day of *Shabbos*:
Even though we are speaking of a professional *mohel*, and there is a remote concern that he might err in the *milah*, and it might be that he will not complete the *milah* as required, and thus he would be desecrating *Shabbos*—as is implied in the *Gemara* that this is possible even with a professional. Furthermore, even when he circumcises as required, why must he involve himself in this and perform an act of desecrating *Shabbos*? It is understandable that the father has a *mitzvah* to circumcise his son on the eighth day, but for someone else, since it is not his *mitzvah*, and he circumcises in place of the father, why should he perform an act that is the opposite of his keeping of *Shabbos* ?

Rather, because the category of the matter of *milah* on *Shabbos* is the revelation of the essence of Israel that is above the Torah, therefore this is emphasized also in the act of *milah*: that the *mohel* is prepared to sacrifice his life in a spiritual sense, to perform an act of desecrating *Shabbos* to circumcise the infant.

A *mohel* who is not prepared to sacrifice his life and circumcise on *Shabbos* cannot circumcise even on a weekday. For through the *milah*, the beginning of the entry of this holy soul is accomplished, and this is the essential connection of this Jew to the Holy One, blessed be He, which is above the Torah. This is possible if this trait is visible in the *mohel* who comes to circumcise the infant to bring him into the covenant of Avraham Avinu, peace be upon him.

## 60

It can be said that this matter—that from the *milah* of Avraham this matter in *milah* which is above the definitions of the Torah comes and is drawn down to Israel—is visible also in the actual *milah* of Avraham:
It is stated in *Pirkei D'Rabbi Eliezer* that the *milah* of Avraham was on Yom Kippur. Seemingly, this requires explanation: since Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah before it was given, how did he circumcise himself on Yom Kippur, when *milah* that is not at its proper time does not override *Shabbos* nor the *Yom Tov* ?

According to the literal sense, it is not difficult, for since he was commanded regarding the *milah* at that time, "on the day He commanded him, he circumcised", this is *milah* at its proper time, which overrides *Shabbos* and *Yom Tov*. But according to the inner dimension of matters, it can be said that because the *milah* of Avraham was in this level that is above the definitions of the Torah, therefore his *milah* in practice was also in a manner that the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* overrode *Shabbos* and also the *Shabbos Shabbaton* of Yom Kippur.

According to the above—that the fact that we learn from the *parsha* of *milah* of Avraham that *milah* overrides *Shabbos* is related to the essential connection of Israel to the Holy One, blessed be He, and the matter of *mesiras nefesh* that is above the Torah—the fact that the *milah* of Avraham was specifically on Yom Kippur is most understandable. For this is related to the atonement of Yom Kippur, as it is stated in *Pirkei D'Rabbi Eliezer* there (following the teaching that Avraham was circumcised on Yom Kippur, "the word 'essence' is derived from Yom Kippur"): "And every year, the Holy One, blessed be He, sees the blood of the covenant of the *milah* of Avraham Avinu and atones for all our sins, as it is written 'for on this day He shall atone for you to purify you, etc.'" For the true atonement of Yom Kippur is that the essence of the day atones, and its matter is the revelation of the essential connection of Israel to the Holy One, blessed be He, which is above any definition of defect (and above the Torah). In this level, the entire matter of sins and iniquities does not reach it at all, and therefore when this level is revealed in Israel, all defects are atoned for automatically, as is explained at length in other places.

This is why the three derivations mentioned above express three matters that exist in *milah*:
1) That it is one of the 613 commandments of the Torah, but in this itself it is "greater" than them, and therefore it overrides the commandments of *Shabbos*.
2) That it is a general *mitzvah*, which is equivalent to all the *mitzvos*, and therefore its fulfillment is also on *Shabbos*, for this effects perfection in the sign of the Holy One, blessed be He, in Israel: "between Me and the children of Israel it is a sign forever, My covenant in your flesh for an eternal covenant".
3) That it is above the measurement and limitation of Torah and *mitzvos*, which belongs to the covenant and essential connection of the children of Israel to the Holy One, blessed be He. This connection is expressed in the *milah* of Avraham himself, "on that very day Avraham was circumcised" —that he was circumcised on Yom Kippur, a *Shabbos Shabbaton*.


## וירא

## Sicha 1

### 61

A. Regarding the obligation to educate children, it is stated in many places that "education is Rabbinic", but there is no obligation of education from the Torah. This requires explanation:
