Since, from the Torah, a minor becomes a *gadol* and is immediately obligated in the fulfillment of all the *mitzvos* of the Torah the moment they reach maturity, if there were no Torah obligation to prepare them for the time of their adulthood, would they not transgress the *mitzvos* of the Torah due to a lack of knowledge and a lack of preparation?

Simply put, one might say that this is included in the general *mitzvah* of *Talmud Torah*: "It is a positive *mitzvah* from the Torah for a father to teach his young son Torah... as it is stated, 'And you shall teach them to your children, speaking of them'". This is as the Ramban explains regarding the verse, "And you shall teach them to your children": "Since He commanded a permanent statute for your generations... we are commanded that our children know the *mitzvos*, and how will they know them if we do not teach them?". According to this, there is a Torah obligation of education as preparation for the fulfillment of *mitzvos* in adulthood; on the contrary, this is the foundation of the general *mitzvah* of *Talmud Torah* for children, as mentioned above.

However, from the Torah, the *mitzvah* is only "that our children know the *mitzvos*," and the Sages came and enacted that there is also a *mitzvah* to educate the son in the actual fulfillment of the *mitzvos*. As the *Rishonim* write, this is in order "to accustom the minor to the *mitzvah*... so that he will be educated and accustomed to the *mitzvos*", "to guide him so that he will be accustomed to his practice when he matures", similar to what is stated, "Educate a youth according to his way; even when he grows old, he will not depart from it".

But seemingly, this is not sufficient : since

### 62

the knowledge of the *mitzvos* itself is not enough for him to fulfill them in practice when he matures. This is proven by the very fact that the Sages obligated educating him in the actual fulfillment of the *mitzvos* so that he would become accustomed to them. Thus, from the Torah, when the minor matures and reaches the age of thirteen years and one day, there is a deficiency in his preparation and complete qualification to be able to fulfill all the *mitzvos* in practice as required when he matures. For if the minor has become accustomed to behaving without observing the Torah and *mitzvos*, even if he knows (in his intellect and thought) the *halachos* of all the *mitzvos*, it is impossible for him to begin fulfilling them all in practice as required in an instant (contrary to his previous nature and habit).

B. Seemingly, one could say that there is indeed an obligation of education from the Torah —and not only in the knowledge of the *mitzvos*. To preface:

When the Torah obligates the fulfillment of a certain *mitzvah*—for example, wearing *tefillin*—it is clear that the obligation is not only for one who happens to have a pair of *tefillin* before him that he is obligated to wear. Rather, the *mitzvah* obligates him to do everything necessary to obtain *tefillin*, and if he did not exert himself and consequently did not wear them, he has nullified a positive *mitzvah*. It is the same with many *mitzvos*, where one is obligated from the Torah to do everything necessary to be able to fulfill the *mitzvah*. In the words of the Rambam : "There are positive *mitzvos* that a person is obligated to exert himself and pursue until he performs them, such as *tefillin*, *sukkah*, *lulav*, and *shofar*." Even though the essence of the obligation is only a specific action, such as wearing *tefillin* or taking a *lulav*, nevertheless, this obligation itself obligates him in all the preparations required to be able to fulfill the (action of the) *mitzvah*. Furthermore, if he did not exert himself to learn and know how to fulfill the *mitzvah*, and therefore did not fulfill it according to its *halacha*, this too is considered nullifying a positive *mitzvah*.

If so, regarding a minor who becomes a *gadol*, at which point he is immediately obligated from the Torah in the fulfillment of the entire Torah, since it is impossible for him to fulfill all that is incumbent upon him without prior preparation—both learning and knowing the *mitzvos*, and his preparation and qualification so that he will be accustomed to fulfilling them in practice (which is similar to the exertion to obtain *tefillin* or a *lulav* and the like)—then this preparation is also, seemingly, an obligation from the Torah. And since there is an obligation upon the minor to prepare himself for his adulthood, but a minor is not subject to obligation, there is room to say that this obligation transfers to the father [similar to what the Rivash writes regarding *pidyon haben*, that "the essence of the *mitzvah* is upon the person himself, but in his childhood it is impossible", and therefore it was placed upon the father].

However, even so, this obligation is not in the category of a *mitzvah*, but only a qualification and preparation, similar to the example of chopping wood to make charcoal to make iron (a scalpel for *bris milah*). And this is what is added in the *mitzvah* of education that is Rabbinic, that it has the status of an obligation of the

### 63

*mitzvah*. It can be said that from the perspective of the Torah obligation, the minor's action itself is not in the category of fulfilling a *mitzvah*, but rather an action so that he will become accustomed, whereas from the Rabbinic perspective, the minor's action is in the category of a *mitzvah*. Therefore, from the Torah, the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* that the minor performs does not need to be in every detail, with complete qualification, because from the perspective of preparation, the manner of his performance does not make much of a difference. This is not the case from the perspective of the Rabbinic *mitzvah* of education, where the minor must fulfill the *mitzvos* in all their details like a *gadol*.

C. If you would say that we find something similar to this in the commentary of Rabbeinu Manoach on what the Rambam writes in *Hilchos Shevisas Asor* : "A minor of nine years or ten years, we educate them in hours. How so? If he was accustomed to eating at two hours of the day, we feed him at three; if he was accustomed to three, we feed him at four. According to the strength of the child, we add to afflict him in hours. A child of eleven years, whether male or female, fasts and completes the fast by Rabbinic decree, in order to educate him in *mitzvos*."

Rabbeinu Manoach wrote regarding this: "And what the Rav wrote here, 'in order to educate him in *mitzvos*', and above it is also written 'we educate him in hours', comes to inform us that there are two types of education, as we say in the Gemara : Rabbah said, there are two types of education: one for affliction and one for completion. And the education of hours is not a Rabbinic obligation, but an obligation placed upon every person himself to teach and accustom his children to walk in the paths of righteousness, as it is written, 'Educate a youth according to his way,' etc., and in order to bring them under the wings of the *Shechinah*... But in the second education, which is when they are eleven years old, where they are obligated to complete the fast by Rabbinic decree, a boy and a girl are equal in it... And this is what the Rav wrote here, 'fasts and completes the fast by Rabbinic decree,' and above he wrote 'we educate him' and did not write 'we educate him in hours by Rabbinic decree,' because the education of hours is not Rabbinic at all, for there is no Rabbinic obligation of education without completion, meaning that he should perform the *mitzvah* as ordained and as its law, in completeness," end of quote.

From his words, it appears that there is a general obligation (which we derive from the verse "Educate a youth...") to educate one's children that they should walk in the path of the Torah and *mitzvos*, and this obligation, since it is only to accustom them to the *mitzvos*, does not require that the *mitzvos* be fulfilled as ordained and according to their *halachos*. And in addition to this, the Sages obligated that the minor perform each and every *mitzvah* as its law and in all its details, like the performance of a *gadol*.

## 64

It can be said that what Rabbeinu Manoach says there, that "the education of hours is not a Rabbinic obligation, but an obligation placed upon every person himself, etc.," means that the responsibility of the father to educate his son (in a general sense) is proven by logic, and a person is obligated in it himself without there being an explicit command and obligation regarding it.

That is to say: since from the Torah every person is obligated from the moment he reaches the age of thirteen years and one day to fulfill the entire Torah and *mitzvos*, it is understood and simple that every Jewish man will educate and accustom his children "to walk in the paths of righteousness," the path of the Torah and the *mitzvah*, just as it is simple that every upright person guides and educates his children in the path of good and uprightness without having a command regarding it, for this is the nature of a father, and in the words of the verse regarding Avraham, "He will command his children and his household after him to do charity and justice."

Based on this, it can be said that there is a category of Torah law in this, as is explained in many places in the *Shas* that a matter proven by logic is from the Torah [and what is written in the *Divrei Kabbalah*, "Educate a youth, etc.," is merely a revelation of the matter].

D. However, seemingly, it is a great innovation to say that there is an obligation of education from the Torah, and this is not mentioned in the *Poskim*. Rather, all have maintained that the obligation of education is only by Rabbinic decree, as is explicit in the words of the Alter Rebbe in the *Shulchan Aruch* at the beginning of *Hilchos Talmud Torah* mentioned above: "that the minor is exempt from all the *mitzvos*, and also his father is not obligated to educate him in *mitzvos* from the Torah, but only by Rabbinic decree, etc.".

Based on this, since from the Torah, the moment a minor becomes a *gadol* he is immediately obligated in the fulfillment of the entire Torah, and if he was not educated and accustomed to the *mitzvos* beforehand, it is impossible that he could be careful as required in all the positive and negative *mitzvos* immediately when he matures—if so, how can it be said that there is no obligation from the Torah to prepare and qualify him for the time of his adulthood so that he will not transgress the *mitzvos* of the Torah due to (a lack of knowledge and) a lack of preparation ? Furthermore, according to what we stated above, that from the perspective of the obligation of *mitzvos* that will take effect immediately when he matures, there is an obligation of preparation so that the moment he

## 65

matures he will be able to fulfill them, and this obligation exists and stands—only that it is impossible for it to take effect upon the minor who lacks understanding—why did the Torah not transfer the obligation to the father or the *Beis Din*, as mentioned above?

E. It appears that what we stated above (section B)—that since immediately when the minor matures he is obligated in all the *mitzvos*, therefore if he did not fulfill them or transgressed them due to a lack of prior knowledge and preparation, it is considered as if he nullified a positive *mitzvah* or transgressed a negative *mitzvah*—is not necessarily true. To preface:

When the Torah obligates a certain *mitzvah*, it is clear that the obligation takes effect only in a way that it can be fulfilled by way of nature, for the Torah was not given to ministering angels. If its fulfillment requires a duration of time, he does not transgress the obligation during that entire time. For example, regarding the *mitzvah* of *biur chametz*, as long as he is occupied with its removal until it is actually removed, he does not transgress the *mitzvah* of "you shall remove it." For this is the definition of the *mitzvah*—to do what is in his power to remove the *chametz*, and if he is occupied with this, he is occupied with the fulfillment of the *mitzvah*. In the words of the Alter Rebbe : "From the beginning of the seventh hour... every moment that *chametz* is found in his possession and he is not occupied with removing it, he transgresses a positive *mitzvah* of the Torah," whereas when he is occupied with its removal, the occupation is part of the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* (meaning the occupation with the preparation for the removal, and not only the occupation with the actual removal). And this is not related to the law of *ones rachmana patrei* (that it is said that from that moment of finding the *chametz* he is transgressing the *mitzvah* of removal, only that all the time required to remove it is considered as if he is coerced, and the Merciful One exempts him). For this only applies if the obligation had already taken effect and he was coerced by a side issue that prevents its fulfillment, but if by the very nature of things it is impossible to fulfill the obligation without a duration of time or prior preparation and qualification, this is part of the obligation.

Now, regarding *mitzvos* whose fulfillment is at specific times, if the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* requires preparation and qualification—such as blowing the *shofar*, where one needs to practice and become accustomed so that he can blow, or if one needs prior exertion to obtain the objects of the *mitzvah* like the Four Species and the like—it is understood, seemingly, that the obligation of preparation begins before the time of the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* so that when its time arrives he will be able to fulfill it as ordained. And it should be noted that the obligation to fulfill the entire Torah is a constant obligation, and even *mitzvos*

## 66

whose essence of fulfillment must be at a specific time, nevertheless, the obligation that they be fulfilled at their time is constant. Therefore, one who was lazy in obtaining the Four Species before the holiday, and therefore could not fulfill the *mitzvah* of taking them at its time, is considered as having nullified a positive *mitzvah*.

However, regarding a minor who has matured, since a minor is exempt from the entire Torah, and there is no trace of any obligation from the Torah applicable to him (while he is a minor), and the beginning

## 67

of the entire reality of obligation is only from when he matures, therefore, if by the nature of things a duration of learning and preparation is necessary until he can fulfill all the *mitzvos*, then this time of preparation (from the Torah) begins only after he matures. Consequently, it cannot be said that he is transgressing any *mitzvah* until the time of the preparation required for the fulfillment of that *mitzvah* has passed.

And even though this is a great innovation in *halacha*, nevertheless, it appears that it is logically necessary, for how can any obligation take effect in a way that by its very nature it is impossible to fulfill? And since the possibility of fulfilling the *mitzvah* as required is only through a prerequisite of learning and education that requires a certain amount of time, therefore it must be that all of this is included in the essence of the obligation. Therefore, there is no logic that the Torah would obligate educating him while he is still a minor so that he could fulfill all the *mitzvos* when he matures; on the contrary, the beginning of the taking effect of the obligation is only at the moment he matures, and if a time of education and qualification is needed, this is included in the essence of the obligations that take effect after he matures.

F. A proof for this logic can be brought from a convert who comes to convert, for we hold that we inform him (only) of some minor *mitzvos* and some major *mitzvos* [and even this is only so that he might withdraw, as it is in the Gemara]. And the Rambam added: "And we inform him of the fundamentals of the religion, which is the unity of Hashem and the prohibition of idolatry, and we elaborate on this matter, and we inform him of some *mitzvos*... and we do not elaborate on this matter." Now, it is simple that if he was not taught and educated in the performance of all the *mitzvos* before his conversion, he will not be able to fulfill them immediately after the conversion. If so, why do we not teach him all the *mitzvos* and all their details? On the contrary—"we do not elaborate on this matter." Therefore, we must say as above regarding the minor: since the entire essence of the obligation in Torah and *mitzvos* takes effect only after the conversion, therefore the obligation of preparation and learning also begins only then. And if it requires a duration of preparation and habituation until he can fulfill certain *mitzvos* according to their *halacha*, then during that entire duration of time he is not transgressing these *mitzvos*.

Similar to a minor who matured (and a convert who converted), this occurred to our ancestors at the time of the Giving of the Torah, when they were obligated for the first time in Torah and *mitzvos*. Certainly, a duration of learning and preparation was required until they were able to fulfill the *mitzvos*, and likewise until they were able to prepare the objects of the *mitzvos* like *tzitzis* and *tefillin*,

## 68

and it is simple that one cannot say that during all that time they were occupied with preparation they were nullifying a positive *mitzvah* (and were exempt only because *ones rachmana patrei*). Rather, since the entire essence of the obligation began then, therefore it took effect in a way that they could fulfill them, meaning with the prerequisite of all the necessary preparations, including also education and habituation.

And so it is with every minor who matures (and similarly a convert who converts), that his entire obligation in Torah and *mitzvos* begins only upon his reaching adulthood. Therefore, all the time that he needs to be occupied with learning, preparation, and education until he can fulfill a certain *mitzvah* according to its *halacha* or be careful regarding a certain prohibition, he is not nullifying that positive *mitzvah* and is not transgressing that negative *mitzvah*; on the contrary, he is occupied with their fulfillment. And for this reason, there is no obligation of education while he is a minor from the Torah.

G. Based on the above, one can explain a puzzling matter regarding Avraham Avinu:

In the verse, "And Avraham was old, advanced in days," our Sages say that Avraham Avinu performed his service throughout all the days of his life, until all his days were "complete days". Seemingly, this requires explanation: how is it possible to say that all the days of Avraham were in completeness, when his first three years of service were missing, as our Sages say, "At three years old, Avraham recognized his Creator, as it is stated, 'Because Avraham listened to My voice,' [which implies] he counted one hundred and seventy-two years." And all the more so according to the opinions that he recognized his Creator at forty years old, or forty-eight, it requires explanation how it is possible to say about him "advanced in days," that not even one day was missing for him, to the point that our Sages said (regarding Sarah, from which it is seemingly understood that it is so regarding Avraham), "All of them were equal for good"?

And based on the above, that the matter of preparation and qualification for a *mitzvah* is a result and a detail of the essence of the obligation, it can be said similarly regarding Avraham Avinu:

The fact that Avraham recognized his Creator when he was three years old came about through his preparation and qualification during the time before that, to investigate and seek out the Almighty. In the words of the Rambam : "He began to wander in his mind... and he was puzzled: how is it possible that this sphere should move constantly and have no leader, and who causes it to rotate? And his heart wandered and understood until he attained the path of truth, etc." And since during all three years he was occupied with the qualification and preparation for the recognition of his Creator, it is appropriate to say that they were "complete days," even in the years before he recognized his Creator, and also regarding the first three years.

However, even so, this is not included in "Avraham listened to My voice" as long as he did not recognize his Creator in practice. Nevertheless, there is no deficiency in the fact that he did not yet recognize his Creator then, because it is necessary by the nature of things that it required a duration of preparation and qualification.

[Similar to the above, that the fact that a thirteen-year-old and one-day-old cannot begin immediately with the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* in its completeness does not mean that there is a deficiency in his fulfillment of the *mitzvah* and that he is only not punished because he is in the category of *ones* that the Merciful One exempts, but rather that from the outset it is conditioned in the essence of the obligation, that at first he is occupied with preparation and qualification, etc., and afterward he fulfills it in practice and in completeness, etc.]

H. Now, all of the above is from the Torah, but from the words of the Sages there is an explicit command and obligation in the education of a minor, and on the contrary—the words of the Sages are more stringent and dear than the words of the Torah.

It should be added that the entire matter of the fulfillment of *mitzvos* during the time of *Galus* is a matter of "education" regarding the fulfillment of *mitzvos* after the *Geulah*, as it is stated in the *Sifri* : "Even though I exile you from the Land to outside the Land, be marked with *mitzvos*, so that when you return they will not be new to you... Set up markers for yourself," for "the essence of all the *mitzvos* is for those who dwell in the Land of Hashem".

And through the fulfillment of the *mitzvos* during the time of *Galus*, we hasten and accelerate the time of the fulfillment of the *mitzvos* in absolute completeness, as is the *mitzvah* of His will, in the true and complete *Geulah* through Mashiach Tzidkeinu, speedily in our days, truly.

(From the *Sichos* of *Acharon Shel Pesach* and *Shabbos Parshas Shemini* 5747; *Shabbos Parshas Vayeira* 5748)

## Sicha 3

### 76

**A.** "And He said, 'Do not stretch forth your hand against the lad, and do not do anything to him, for now I know that you are a G-d-fearing man...'". Rashi, in his commentary on "for now I know" (s.v. *Ha'ah*), cites the statement of Rabbi Abba : "Abraham said to Him, 'I shall explain my grievance before You: Yesterday You said to me, "For in Yitzchak shall your seed be called", and then You went back and said, "Take now your son". Now You say to me, "Do not stretch forth your hand against the lad."' The Holy One, blessed be He, said to him, 'I will not profane My covenant, and that which has gone out of My lips I will not alter. When I said to you "Take," I did not say to you "Slaughter him," but rather "Bring him up." You brought him up; take him down.'"

Commentators have already raised difficulties :
1) From where does Rashi derive all this from the plain meaning of the verse ? Where is it hinted in "for now I know" that Abraham wondered, "Yesterday You said to me... and then You went back and said...", and that the Holy One, blessed be He, replied to him, "That which has gone out of My lips I will not alter; I did not say to you 'Slaughter him'..."?
2) Why did Abraham wonder only when he was commanded "Do not stretch forth your hand," and not before—immediately when he was commanded "Take now your son" (which is in contradiction to the promise of the Holy One, blessed be He, "For in Yitzchak shall your seed be called")?

One must also note:
The source of this statement is in *Bereishis Rabbah*, but there, in addition to it being brought in the name of Rabbi Acha (not Rabbi Abba), and with other linguistic variations, there is also a fundamental change in content:
In the Midrash it says, "Rabbi Acha said: Abraham began to wonder, 'Are these not words of wonder? Yesterday You said...'"—meaning that Abraham was wondering about the conduct of the Holy One, blessed be He. And even according to the version (which is close to Rashi’s wording), "I shall explain my grievances (in wonder) before You," the intention is, as stated in the commentary on the Midrash attributed to Rashi (and similarly in the *Yalkut*), "Abraham said: As it were, there are grievances before You like human beings who change their words."
This is unlike Rashi’s version, "Abraham said to Him, 'I shall explain my *shihati* (my grievance/my discourse) before You'"—for this phrasing is not an expression of wonder toward Heaven, and it does not refer to the "grievances" (words) of the Holy One, blessed be He, but rather to the words of Abraham ("my discourse").

It must be understood: (a) Why did Rashi choose this version specifically? (b) What is the content of this introduction, which seemingly is superfluous, and why did he not begin immediately with the substance of the matter, "Yesterday You said..."? (c) Which "discourse" of his did Abraham come to explain ?

**B.** It seems to me that this is indeed Rashi’s intention in transcribing this phrasing ("I shall explain my discourse before You"), to clarify that Abraham Avinu’s intention in these words was not to wonder about the words of the Holy One, blessed be He.
[For according to the plain meaning of the verse, one cannot say that Abraham Avinu harbored doubts about the attributes of the Holy One, blessed be He. As is explicitly stated in Rashi earlier, that the Holy One, blessed be He, tested Abraham "to see if he would harbor doubts about the words of the Holy One, blessed be He, who said to him to go to the land of Canaan, and now was causing him to leave it"—that even though they were contradictory words, he did not harbor doubts "about the words of the Holy One, blessed be He." And we find similarly in Rashi later (at the end of *Parshas Shemos*) regarding this matter, that this was Abraham’s superiority over Moshe Rabbeinu, that the Holy One, blessed be He, said to Moshe, "You have harbored doubts about My attributes, not like Abraham, to whom I said, 'For in Yitzchak shall your seed be called', and afterwards I said to him, 'Bring him up for a burnt offering', and he did not harbor doubts about My attributes" (meaning, not only was there no weakness in Abraham regarding the actual fulfillment of the command, but even in his thought, no doubt arose "about My attributes"). If so, certainly, even at the end of the matter, when the contradiction grew that "now You say to me, 'Do not stretch forth your hand,'" Abraham did not harbor doubts about the attributes of the Holy One, blessed be He (and certainly not to express his wonder in speech)],
Rather, [his intention was] to explain to the Holy One, blessed be He, his own (Abraham’s) intention in his previous words.

## 78

For in the passage preceding this, Rashi explains: "'Do not stretch forth'—to slaughter. He said to Him, 'If so, I have come here for nothing. I will inflict a blemish upon him and draw from him a little blood.' He said to him, 'Do not do anything to him, do not inflict a blemish upon him.'" That is to say, there was an exchange of words between Abraham and the angel, where Abraham wanted to inflict a blemish upon Yitzchak (for otherwise, it would turn out that "I have come here for nothing"), and to this the angel replied, "Do not do anything to him, do not inflict a blemish upon him."
After this, Abraham explained this "discourse" of his—that the reason he wanted to inflict a blemish upon Yitzchak was because "Yesterday You said... and then You went back and said..." And to this, the reply of the Holy One, blessed be He (via the angel), came again: "For now I know..." (and there is no need even for a blemish), as follows.

**C. Explaining the matter:**
After Abraham said, "If so, I have come here for nothing; I will inflict a blemish upon him," and was answered in the negative ("Do not do anything to him"), Abraham thought that perhaps the reason he was commanded "Do not stretch forth your hand" (contrary to the command "Take now...") was because he was unworthy of it and had not stood the test properly, because his heart was not whole with Him (for even though he was prepared to fulfill this command in practice, he was nevertheless harboring doubts about His attributes, etc.), and therefore he was not being permitted to fulfill the command of "Take now."
Abraham also thought that this was the reason his request "I will inflict a blemish upon him" was not accepted, because by this he "admitted" that he was truly not prepared to fulfill the command of "Take now your son," and therefore he was asking that at least he might be able to fulfill it in part (by inflicting a blemish and drawing blood), so that his coming here would not be "for nothing"—and therefore he was answered in the negative.
To negate this mistaken assumption, Abraham continued to argue—"I shall explain my discourse before You"—that his request "I will inflict a blemish upon him" after being commanded "Do not stretch forth your hand to slaughter" was (not to detract from the fulfillment of the Divine command, a request that he might at least fulfill a "portion" of "Take now," but rather) because, in his opinion, it had become clear then that this was the original intent of the command "Take now"—not that he should slaughter his son, but that he should draw blood to sprinkle it upon the altar.
The reason he thought this was the command from the outset, he explained by saying, "Yesterday You said to me, 'For in Yitzchak shall your seed be called,' and then You went back and said, 'Take now your son.' Now You say to me, 'Do not stretch forth your hand against the lad.'" That is to say: Initially, Abraham indeed did not understand how the two contradictory Divine utterances ("For in Yitzchak..." and "Take now...") could both be fulfilled (but since he was explicitly commanded "Take now... and bring him up," he fulfilled it without harboring doubts about the attributes of the Holy One, blessed be He). But now that he was commanded "Do not stretch forth your hand to slaughter," it was possible to reconcile the two verses and fulfill both —by inflicting a blemish and drawing blood (and sprinkling it upon the altar).
This was Abraham’s argument: "I will inflict a blemish upon him," so that through this he could fulfill the Divine command in its entirety, and all three Divine utterances would be fulfilled: "(Yesterday You said to me) 'For in Yitzchak...';" "'Take now...';" "(Now You say to me) 'Do not stretch forth...'"
And to this he was answered that "now I know that you are a G-d-fearing man," and there is no deficiency in him—for he was not prevented from fulfilling the command of the Holy One, blessed be He, since from the beginning, "I did not say to you 'Slaughter him,' but rather 'Bring him up.' You brought him up; take him down" (and this is the reason there is no contradiction between the two matters of "For in Yitzchak..." and "Take now..."). Thus, it turns out that Abraham fulfilled the command of the Holy One, blessed be He ("Bring him up") in its entirety.

## 79

**D.** According to this, the necessity for Rashi’s interpretation is simply explained: that there were further exchanges between Abraham and the Holy One, blessed be He ("Yesterday You said... The Holy One, blessed be He, said to him, 'I will not profane My covenant...'")—the necessity being derived from the words of the Holy One, blessed be He, to Abraham, "For now I know" (concerning which he wrote his commentary).
The explanation of this:
Seemingly, it requires explanation: why did the Holy One, blessed be He, need to tell Abraham that he had stood the test (even without the slaughter of Yitzchak) and "now I know that you are a G-d-fearing man"? Just as Abraham Avinu did not harbor doubts about the attributes of the Holy One, blessed be He, when he was commanded "Take now your son," but hurried to fulfill the command without seeking an explanation or excuse for this command, surely when He commanded him again, "Do not stretch forth your hand," there was no need to explain to Abraham the reason for this (that even without the slaughter of Yitzchak he had already stood the test and "I know that you are a G-d-fearing man"), for Abraham did not harbor doubts about the attributes of the Holy One, blessed be He [and especially since he certainly could have understood by himself that all this was only a test, and there was no need for the Holy One, blessed be He, to tell him explicitly "For now I know..."].
One must therefore say that the words of the Holy One, blessed be He, "For now I know..." did not come (only) to explain to Abraham the purpose of the test of the *Akeidah*, but that this was also a response to Abraham’s words, so that he would not mistakenly say that he was not G-d-fearing and that the purpose of the test had not been fulfilled; rather, on the contrary, "now I know that you are a G-d-fearing man."
This means, as above, that when Abraham heard the command "Do not stretch forth your hand... and do not do anything to him," even though the Holy One, blessed be He, had commanded him "Take now your son... and bring him up there for a burnt offering"—he concluded that perhaps the reason the Holy One, blessed be He, was not letting him fulfill the command of "Take now" was because he was unworthy of it. To this, the Holy One, blessed be He, answered him ("Do not stretch forth..."), "For now I know that you are a G-d-fearing man," meaning that the fact that He commanded him "Do not stretch forth" is not because of a deficiency, Heaven forbid, in Abraham; on the contrary, "now I know that you are a G-d-fearing man," but rather it is because "I did not say to you 'Slaughter him,' but rather 'Bring him up.'"

**E.** Now, in this way, we can also resolve why Rashi transcribed the name of the author of the statement there, and in this very point chose specifically the version "Rabbi Abba" (and not "Rabbi Acha"—as in the *Bereishis Rabbah* before us).
For according to the above, it can be said that Rashi’s intention in transcribing the name of Rabbi Abba is to hint that the fact that Rabbi Abba held that Abraham’s words, "Yesterday You said to me...", were not said as wondering about the words of the Holy One, blessed be He (but only to explain his discourse, that he wanted to fulfill the Divine command in its entirety)—is consistent with his own position:
In *Maseches Chagigah* we learned, "Whoever does not show concern for the honor of his Creator, etc.," and in the Gemara there —"What is this? Rabbi Abba said: This is one who gazes

## 80

at the rainbow... as it is written, 'As the appearance of the bow that is in the cloud in the day of rain, so was the appearance of the brightness round about. This was the appearance of the likeness of the glory of the L-rd.'"
Now, although in the plain sense Rabbi Abba’s intention is that gazing at the rainbow is akin to gazing at the reflection of the *Shechinah* (since "the appearance of the bow... so was the appearance of the brightness round about"), nevertheless, we find that the prohibition of gazing at the rainbow also relates to the sign of the rainbow that there will no longer be a flood (which is why one recites a blessing upon seeing the rainbow, "Blessed is He who remembers the covenant", as is brought in the continuation of the passage there).

**F.** This will be understood by prefacing the precision of the expression "one who gazes at the rainbow," that the intention here is not mere seeing—for on the contrary, the Sages instituted a special blessing upon seeing the rainbow—but rather gazing in a manner of study and contemplation.
The precision in this must be explained: The connection between the rainbow and the honor of his Creator is because of the "appearance of the bow" that Yechezkel saw in the *Merkavah* ("As the appearance of the bow... so was the appearance of the brightness round about"), and especially according to what is related in the Gemara there earlier, that when Rabbi Yehoshua lectured on the *Maaseh Merkavah*, "the heavens became clouded and it appeared like a kind of rainbow in the cloud," from which it is understood even more that the appearance of the rainbow is related to the *Maaseh Merkavah*. This is the content of the prohibition to gaze at the rainbow, because it hints at the gazing and contemplation of the matters of *Maaseh Merkavah*, which is the opposite of the honor of his Creator, because these matters must be kept hidden (in the words of the verse, "It is the glory of G-d to conceal a thing"), since they are above the grasp of human intellect, and contemplation of them will lead to questions and doubts, etc. [And according to this, the two matters in the Mishnah —(1) the prohibition to lecture on *Maaseh Merkavah* even to an individual "unless he is a sage, etc.," and (2) "whoever does not show concern for the honor of his Creator" (which according to Rabbi Abba refers to one who gazes at the rainbow)—their content is one.]
According to this, it can be said that the content of this prohibition also relates to the sign of the rainbow, because from the perspective of the "honor of his Creator," it is not fitting to gaze and contemplate the content of the rainbow, since a question and a grievance against the conduct of the Holy One, blessed be He, could be aroused through it, in the continuation of which the sign of the rainbow was given.
For the reason that there will no longer be a flood upon the earth (concerning which the sign of the rainbow was given) is explicitly stated in the verse, "I will not again curse the ground anymore for man's sake, for the imagination of man's heart is evil

## 81

from his youth." Yet this very reasoning served as the reason for bringing the flood, as it is written, "And the L-rd saw that the wickedness of man was great in the earth, and that every imagination of the thoughts of his heart was only evil continually." What changed, that initially this reasoning necessitated bringing the flood, and afterwards it turned that this very reasoning would necessitate not bringing a flood upon the earth anymore ?!
Furthermore: Since the Holy One, blessed be He, created man from the beginning in such a way that "the imagination of man's heart is evil from his youth," it was certainly known before Him, may He be blessed, that eventually he would reach a state where "every imagination of the thoughts of his heart was only evil continually," and yet the Holy One, blessed be He, created His world. What was renewed afterwards, that because of this "the L-rd regretted that He had made man"?
This is what Rabbi Abba says, that one who gazes at the rainbow—meaning one who studies and contemplates the content of the sign of the rainbow—is in the category of "not showing concern for the honor of his Creator", because this contemplation can cause him to have doubts regarding the conduct of the Holy One, blessed be He.
And Rabbi Abba is consistent with his own position, that certainly Abraham did not intend to wonder about the contradiction in the words of the Holy One, blessed be He—"Yesterday You said... and then You went back and said..."—which would be the opposite of the conduct of one who "shows concern for the honor of his Creator," but his intention was only and exclusively to explain to the Holy One, blessed be He, "my discourse," as explained at length above.

(From a *sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Vayeira* 5748)


## חיי שרה

## Chayei Sarah

## Sicha 1

1. Regarding the verse, "And the field of Efron stood," Rashi explains: "It had a *tekumah* (a rise in status), for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king. The literal meaning of the verse is: 'And the field and the cave that was in it and all the trees were established for Avraham as a purchase...'"

Simply put, Rashi provides two interpretations for the word "stood" (*vayakam*): 1) from the root *tekumah* (a rise in status), and 2) from the root *kinyan* (acquisition), which is the literal meaning of the verse, "similar to 'the house that is in the city that has a wall shall be established (*vayakam*) in perpetuity to the buyer,' where Rashi explains: 'It departed from the power of the seller and stood in the power of the buyer.'"

The commentators ask why Rashi did not content himself with the interpretation of *vayakam* as "acquisition," as per the literal meaning of the verse. They explain that it is because "the buyer is not written after the 'standing' (as it is in the verse 'the house... shall be established in perpetuity to the buyer'), for 'to Avraham as a purchase' is not connected to the preceding verse due to the cantillation marks. Thus, the word *vayakam* can only be interpreted as *tekumah*. However, according to the literal meaning of the verse, which does not concern itself with the cantillation marks, they interpreted it according to its plain meaning, as an acquisition, similar to 'the field and the cave... were established.' This is why he said, 'The literal meaning of the verse is: "And the field and the cave... were established for Avraham as a purchase,"' connecting 'for Avraham as a purchase' to the word *vayakam* in the preceding verse, in contrast to the *baalei hamidrash* who did not connect them."

Seemingly, this explanation is forced. Rashi established a great principle regarding his commentary: "I have come only to explain the literal meaning of the verse (*peshuto shel mikra*) and the *aggadah* that resolves the words of the verse." That is, only when there is a difficulty in the text that is not resolved by the literal meaning does he bring *aggadah* to resolve the literal meaning. Since, according to the "literal meaning of the verse (which does not concern itself with the cantillation marks)," there is no difficulty (as we find many times that verses are connected even though they are two separate verses), why did Rashi need to bring an additional interpretation from the *baalei hamidrash*? Furthermore, he prioritizes it as the first and primary interpretation within the literal meaning of the verse.

2. Some explain that according to the literal meaning of the verse, the words "the field of Efron which is in Machpelah which is before Mamre" are superfluous. It should have said, "And the field and the cave... were established for Avraham as a purchase," for did we not know until now that it was the field of Efron? Therefore, he explains that there was a *tekumah* for the field of Efron—the entire field experienced a rise in status. That is why it says "the field of Efron," meaning that the field which was associated with Efron, and which was in Machpelah and before Mamre, experienced a *tekumah* in that it entered the domain of Avraham. But on the other hand, according to the *midrashic* interpretation that "it had a *tekumah*," it is difficult: since it already said "And the field of Efron stood," why mention "the field and the cave..." a second time? It should have said, "And the field of Efron stood... and the cave and all the trees." Also, according to the interpretation that "it had a *tekumah*," it has no connection to the subsequent verse "for Avraham as a purchase." Therefore, he also needs the interpretation according to the "literal meaning" that the field and the cave and all the trees were established for Avraham as a purchase.

According to this explanation, Rashi’s intent is that the verse itself necessitates two interpretations. By saying "the field of Efron" first and then repeating "the field and the cave," it is proven that he is teaching us two things: that there was a *tekumah* for the field of Efron, and that the field and the cave were established for Avraham as a purchase.

But it is still not understood: why does Rashi prioritize the interpretation according to the *midrash* over the literal meaning of the verse? [Simply put, one could say that the order of the interpretations in Rashi follows their order in the explanation of the verse ("And the field of Efron stood — it had a *tekumah*," and the continuation of the verse "the field and the cave..." is explained as "were established... for Avraham as a purchase"). But seemingly, this is not a sufficient reason to prioritize the *midrash* before the interpretation according to the "literal meaning of the verse."]

Therefore, we must say that Rashi holds that the interpretation of *vayakam* as "a rise in status" (*tekumah*) is not merely a *midrash*, as is also proven by the fact that Rashi does not preface it with "our Sages said" or "our Rabbis explained" or the like.

The fact that he emphasizes in the second interpretation, "And the literal meaning of the verse is: 'And the field... were established for Avraham as a purchase,'" is to teach us that the advantage of the interpretation "it had a *tekumah*," for which he prioritizes it over the second interpretation, is [not from the perspective of the literal meaning of this verse—for the "literal meaning of this verse," both the interpretation of the word *vayakam* and the literal meaning of the continuation of the two verses, is like the second interpretation, that "were established... for Avraham as a purchase"—but rather] because through it, a difficulty in the entire episode here is resolved [even though the literal meaning of this verse is like the second interpretation].

3. The explanation for this: Initially, when Avraham spoke with the children of Ches, he asked them, "Give me a burial estate with you, that I may bury my dead from before me," and subsequently, when he asked, "If it is your desire to bury my dead from before me, hear me and intercede for me with Efron the son of Tzochar," he asked, "that he may give me the cave of Machpelah... which is at the end of his field... for a burial estate." In other words, the entire negotiation was only about the cave. However, after Efron said to Avraham, "No, my lord, hear me; the field I have given to you, and the cave that is in it, I have given to you," Avraham replied, "I have given the money for the field; take it from me, and I will bury my dead there," accepting Efron’s offer for the field, without even mentioning the cave.

This requires explanation: Efron’s offer, "No, my lord, hear me; the field I have given to you," can be interpreted even according to the literal meaning of the verse as Efron’s intent being merely to show his integrity and good heart, as the content of his speech generally was, "No, my lord, do not buy it with money," "I have given it to you." But in truth, he wanted to extract more money from Avraham (as Rashi brings in the *parsha* that he spoke much and did not even do a little). But why did Avraham accept this offer from Efron? [The Torah commentators explained this, but seemingly this is a question even according to the literal meaning of the verse, and Rashi should have resolved it; seemingly, we do not find an explanation for this in Rashi.]

Also, it requires explanation (as the commentators point out) regarding the verses here: "And the field of Efron... were established... for Avraham as a purchase... and after this Avraham buried Sarah his wife... and the field and the cave that was in it were established for Avraham as a burial estate from the children of Ches." Why was it necessary to say "stood" (*vayakam*) twice?

[The *Ohr HaChaim* discusses this at length, explaining that this is to inform us that "the acquisition here was according to all the laws of acquisition," that "after Avraham weighed out the money for the cave to Efron, Efron removed his rights from the land, as the Rambam writes... that a gentile, from the moment he takes the money, removes his rights, and a Jew does not acquire until the deed reaches his hand, and these assets were like ownerless property." The completion of the acquisition was later "through the *chazakah* (act of possession) that he performed by burying Sarah in it." This is the second "stood," that only then, after he buried Sarah, was it acquired by Avraham. But this explanation is according to *halacha*, for in the literal meaning of the verse there is no hint of all this, and simply, the field was acquired by Avraham through the money itself, as per the literal meaning of the verse.]

To resolve all this, Rashi prefaces the verse, "And the field of Efron stood — it had a *tekumah*, for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king," as follows.

4. This will be understood based on the explanation of Rashi’s precise wording, "from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king." Seemingly, Efron had become a prince before the sale, as Rashi brought earlier, "That day they appointed him a police officer over them because of the importance of Avraham." He should have used the terms "wicked" and "righteous" or the like, and not "commoner" and "king" (and in the *midrash* it indeed says, "He was a small person and became a great person").

The explanation is: The difference in the definition of an acquisition when a commoner buys from a commoner—even when one is more important than the other—versus an acquisition by a king, is that the acquisition of a commoner from a commoner is essentially a change of domain. The name of the acquisition is a departure from one person's ownership to another's. Consequently, even though there are differences in the modes of acquisition—money, deed, and *chazakah*—in all cases, the name of the acquisition is a departure from one domain to another.

But when a king buys from a commoner, it is a matter of a complete expropriation of domain, and it cannot be attributed here to the private name of the person who sold the field to the king.

In other words: When one buys from another, since the definition of an acquisition is a joint act of the seller (the giver) and the buyer (the receiver)—whether in the act of acquisition or in the meeting of minds—the transfer of the seller, then just as the seller's name is present in the act of acquisition, the name also remains on the field itself, that this field was taken by so-and-so from so-and-so.

Not so in a king's acquisition. The primary definition of the acquisition is not from the side of the giver and seller, but from the side of the king (and it is in the king's power to take everything from the citizens of the country in practice). Therefore, the previous name of ownership does not remain on the field.

This is why Rashi emphasizes, "It had a *tekumah*, for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king." This is not just a general definition of an acquisition from seller to buyer, where the field of Efron was acquired by Avraham, but "it had a *tekumah*," a departure from the name of a commoner to the name of a king. Consequently, there is no connection here to a private name, and there is no difference in this regard between a literal commoner and a prince—for in this, they are equal.

5. It can be said that this matter is in accordance with the general content of Avraham’s conduct, that he did not want to receive the cave as a gift but rather to buy it. In this itself, he emphasized that his purchase would be "for full money," "I will pay its full value"—Avraham’s intent in this was to remove Efron’s (name) entirely from the field. That is, not only that Efron would have no ownership or right (or even connection) of money in the field [whereby there would be no trace of "a righteous man buried in a grave that is not his"], but furthermore, that Efron’s name would also be removed from the field, even as a "giver" or "seller" of a burial place for Sarah’s burial.

For this reason, he emphasized "for full money," meaning that the money he gives is not in the category of a monetary acquisition, but that he is giving the full value of the field. For in a monetary acquisition, there is the participation of the giving and the meeting of minds of the acquirer (as mentioned above), whereas when he pays the value of the object in money, in a way that the seller lacks nothing, the primary acquisition is from the side of (the giving of the value of the object by) the buyer, and not the transfer of the acquirer (the seller).

According to this, the emphasis of the verse after the story that "Avraham weighed out to Efron the money..."—"And the field of Efron stood"—is even more understood. Seemingly, according to the literal meaning of the verse that the intent is that it was acquired by Avraham, what is it teaching us here, and seemingly the entire verse is superfluous? This is what Rashi explains, that the content of the verse here is "it had a *tekumah*, for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king." The verse comes to say that in addition to the removal of Efron’s name from the field by the very act of buying the field "for full money," there is also this: "And the field... stood—it had a *tekumah*, for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king," that Efron’s name was removed entirely from the field, since "it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king," as mentioned above.

This is also the precision of the language in the subsequent verse—"And after this Avraham buried Sarah his wife in the cave of the field of Machpelah" (for seemingly it is obvious that he buried Sarah only after he bought the field)—because only after "it had a *tekumah*," that the name of a commoner was entirely removed from it—meaning not only that no ownership remained for Efron, but furthermore, that there is no matter of a commoner here (even though he sold the field to Avraham), for it is the field of a "king" without any connection even in name (which was retroactive) to the name of Efron—then Avraham buried Sarah his wife.

6. In this way, one can resolve another precision in the continuation of the negotiation between Avraham and the children of Ches:

Avraham initially asked, "Give me a burial estate with you," and Rashi explains, "a burial estate for a cemetery," meaning that Avraham asked not only for a place for Sarah’s burial alone (or even for a few graves), but "a land estate for a cemetery." The children of Ches replied, "In the choicest of our graves bury your dead; no man of us will withhold his grave from you from burying your dead," from which it is understood that they did not want to give Avraham "a land estate for a cemetery."

Similarly, we find later, when he asked Efron for the cave of Machpelah "which is at the end of his field," Avraham said, "For full money let him give it to me... for a burial estate." However, after Efron offered him the entire field with "the cave that is in it I have given to you... bury your dead," Avraham agreed and said, "And I will bury my dead there" (and again did not emphasize the matter of "a burial estate").

On the other hand, at the conclusion of the matter, after the story of Sarah’s burial in practice, it is said again: "And the field and the cave... were established... for a burial estate"!

According to the above, the explanation for this is: Avraham’s intent in asking for "a burial estate—a land estate for a cemetery" was that he would make the land estate into a cemetery. That is, first of all, he would buy a land estate, and only after it was in Avraham’s possession would he make it "a cemetery," because through this, it is more emphasized that Efron’s name is not on this "cemetery."

But after Efron offered to give him the entire field (not just the cave designated for burial), there was no longer a need to emphasize that he was buying "a burial estate," because the entire field (not just the cave) was in Avraham’s possession, in a way that his activity and deed in the field had no connection at all to the giver and seller of the field. And then, "And I will bury my dead there," for there is no content in Sarah’s burial in the field that has any connection to Efron, but a person acts within his own property and does not need them to sell it to him.

According to this, the reason is also understood why at the conclusion of the matter the verse adds one more time, "And the field and the cave that was in it were established for Avraham (and it emphasizes) for a burial estate," to emphasize that, in the end, Avraham’s request was fulfilled that he be given "a burial estate." This is the second "stood" (*vayakam*), for by the field becoming Avraham’s "burial estate," his ownership of the field was strengthened. Consequently, it is understood that this addition to Avraham’s connection to the field also added to the field’s status ("it had a *tekumah*"). However, this *tekumah* could only be after "the field of Efron stood—it had a *tekumah*, for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king," and Efron’s name was already removed entirely from the field [and what is also written in the second "stood," "from the children of Ches," is only because initially the field was in their general ownership].

7. It is known that Chevron, and especially the place of the cave of Machpelah, is the first place in Eretz Yisrael that was actually bought for the Jewish people in perpetuity, as it is in the *midrash* that it is "one of three places that the nations of the world cannot deceive Israel by saying they are stolen in your hands, and they are: the cave of Machpelah, the Beis Hamikdash, and the burial place of Yosef."

It can be said that from this it is understood that there is a directive in the spiritual *avodah* of every single Jew to "make Eretz Yisrael here," that in every place he finds himself, a Jew must make Eretz Yisrael—that the making of Eretz Yisrael is in a way that "it had a *tekumah*, for it passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king."

The explanation of the matter: Just as the *tekumah* of the field was even before Sarah’s burial, but through the fact that it entered Avraham’s domain, so too in spiritual *avodah*: when a Jew comes to any place, or has a connection to a matter in the world—for it is simple that "from Hashem are the steps of a man established and He desires his way"—to fulfill his mission to make Eretz Yisrael out of it, he must recognize and know that by the very fact of his presence in that place, "it had a *tekumah*." Even before he approaches to fulfill a *mitzvah* in it, a matter of elevation and *tekumah* has already been created in the place, a departure from the hand of a commoner, through the very fact that it is in the domain of Israel.

From this, another matter is understood: it is impossible for there to be any obstacle to his *avodah* or hindrance to making "Eretz Yisrael here," for since "it had a *tekumah*," because it has already passed from the hand of a commoner to the hand of a king—and up to the King of the world—by entering the domain of Israel, for all of Israel are children of kings, and furthermore, they are kings. That is, even when the thing in the world is not just a physical thing but an important matter and thing in the world (like Efron, who was not a commoner, for he rose to greatness, they appointed him a police officer over them), nevertheless, regarding a Jewish person, this is a commoner, for it does not take up space regarding a Jewish person, and regarding his power and *avodah* to make Eretz Yisrael here.

And the manner of his acting is like a "king," *amalka akar tura* (a king uproots a mountain), that even when it seems like a mountain here—the *yetzer hara* that appears like a mountain—he uproots it from its place and brings it into the domain of holiness.

And afterwards, he goes to fulfill his *mitzvah* to draw down holiness into the matters of the world to make Eretz Yisrael in practice.

8. From all this, there is a clear directive also regarding Eretz Yisrael literally, for the advantage of the place of the field and the cave of Machpelah in Chevron is that it is one of three places that the nations of the world cannot deceive Israel (as mentioned above).

Even though regarding all of Eretz Yisrael there is no place for the claim of theft, as Rashi explains at the beginning of his commentary on the Torah, "The entire land belongs to the Holy One, blessed be He; He created it and gave it to whomever was right in His eyes; by His will He gave it to them and by His will He took it from them and gave it to us," nevertheless, this is an answer to the nations of the world who claim to Israel, "You are thieves," but in these three places, there is not even a remote possibility of a claim that they would need to answer, since these three places came into their hands through the payment of money.

The reason for this is understood according to what is written in the commentators, that the uniqueness of these three places is that these places are always special in their holiness, even during the time of exile: the place of the *Mikdash*, for it always stands in its holiness, as our Sages said and the Rambam ruled as *halacha* that the holiness of the *Mikdash* is because of the *Shechinah*, and the *Shechinah* is never nullified, "The *Shechinah* has not departed from the *Heichal*... the *Shechinah* does not depart from the Western Wall." Similarly, the cave of Machpelah, the place of the burial of the Patriarchs, through which the *tefillah* of all Israel passes, and similarly regarding the place of the burial of Yosef.

According to this, it can be said that of these three places themselves, the place of the cave of Machpelah is unique in that, in addition to the fact that it was bought for Avraham before the conquest of the land, it was the first place in Eretz Yisrael that was bought for Avraham, who bought it for the burial of Sarah, the mother of Yitzchak, without any connection to Yishmael, for Sarah’s connection is only to the people of Israel. And since then, it has been the place of the burial of the Patriarchs of the children of Israel: Avraham and Sarah, Yitzchak and Rivkah, Yaakov and Leah.

And through Chevron and the cave of Machpelah, power and strength were given—the power of a king—that Eretz Yisrael will remain for the children of Israel even in the final moments of the exile. And very soon we will merit the coming of *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*, when all of Eretz Yisrael will be in its completeness, "Hashem will expand your border," also the land of the Kenite, the Kenizzite, and the Kadmonite, which were given in the Covenant Between the Parts to Avraham our father, in the way of "To your seed I have given this land, from the river of Egypt to the great river, the river Euphrates, the Kenite and the Kenizzite and the Kadmonite," together with the land of the seven nations, speedily in our days, truly.

## Sicha 3

### 94

A. "And Abraham added and took a wife, and her name was Keturah, and she bore him...".
The well-known question regarding this is: In several previous verses, it is explicitly stated that the birth of Isaac, when Abraham was one hundred years old, was a miraculous event (not only because of Sarah’s age, but also) because Abraham was at an age where, according to nature, he could not father children:
"And Abraham fell on his face and laughed, and said in his heart, 'Shall a child be born to him who is one hundred years old? And shall Sarah, who is ninety years old, bear?'"; "And Abraham and Sarah were old, advanced in days... and Sarah laughed within herself, saying, 'After I have withered, shall I have pleasure, my lord being old?'"; "And she said, 'Who would have said to Abraham... that I have borne a son to his old age?'", and even to the point that "the scoffers of the generation were saying that Sarah became pregnant from Abimelech".
Based on this, one must understand why, when Abraham fathered six more sons forty years after the birth of Isaac, when he was one hundred and forty years old, the verse does not explicitly state that this was a miraculous and wondrous event, etc.
Now, the Ramban writes regarding the birth of Isaac that it is not a wonder for a one-hundred-year-old to father children, and he brings proof from the aforementioned fact—that "after this, forty years later, he fathered many children from Keturah." However, one cannot interpret Rashi’s opinion and view in this way, for the plain meaning of the aforementioned verses does not imply this. If Rashi held this view, he should have explained it. Furthermore, his own commentary implies the opposite, as he writes: "Even though the earlier generations would father children at five hundred years old, in the days of Abraham the years were diminished and weakness came into the world; go and learn from the ten generations from Noah to Abraham, who hastened their procreation to sixty and seventy years old".
And even if you were to say that for Abraham there was no weakness until he was one hundred [or that (after the time of "And Abraham and Sarah were old") he returned to his youth and fathered when he was one hundred], yet after this it is stated in our parsha (once again) "And Abraham was old, advanced in days", meaning

## 95

that he experienced a state of old age that was added thereafter. If so, the birth of the sons of Keturah when he was one hundred and forty is surely a miraculous event.
This is understood from the words of the Midrash on the verse "And Abraham was old...": "Rabbi Yochanan said: It is already written 'And Abraham and Sarah were old'; what does it mean to say 'And Abraham was old'? Rather, the Holy One, Blessed be He, returned him to the days of his youth, so it was necessary to write it a second time, 'And Abraham was old.' Rav Ami said: Here it refers to an old age that has moisture, and there it refers to an old age that has no moisture". Thus, even if you were to say that the birth of Isaac was a miracle on Abraham’s part (that the old age and weakness associated with procreation did not govern him), nevertheless, a miracle was required for the birth of the sons of Keturah, which occurred after it had already been stated once again, "And Abraham was old, advanced in days".

B. The core explanation for this can be stated as follows:
Regarding the story of Isaac’s birth, it is said, "And Sarah said, 'God has made laughter for me; everyone who hears will laugh for me,'" and Rashi explains, "Many barren women were remembered with her, many sick were healed on that day, many prayers were answered with her, and there was much laughter in the world." Thus, the miracle of Isaac’s birth was in such a manner that it caused salvation and rescue throughout the entire world, to the point that it brought about joy and "much laughter in the world."
With this, we can understand what Rashi says there regarding the verse, "that Sarah would nurse children": "Why 'children' in the plural? On the day of the feast, the noblewomen brought their children with them, and she nursed them, for they were saying, 'Sarah did not give birth; she brought a foundling from the marketplace'". Similarly, in Parshas Lech Lecha, Rashi explains: "And at the nursing of breasts—when she needed to, on the day of Isaac’s feast, they were gossiping about them, saying they brought a foundling from the marketplace and saying, 'It is our son.' So she brought each one her son, and the one who did not bring [her child]—she nursed them all."
Seemingly, if the reason for this was only to prove that Sarah gave birth to Isaac and he was not a foundling from the marketplace, it would have been sufficient for everyone to see that she was nursing Isaac, or that she would nurse a few other children. But why was she required to nurse "all of them"? Rather, there was an additional matter here: the miracle of Isaac’s birth caused many matters of *hashpaah* (influence), even to the point of "much laughter... in the world," and therefore this was expressed also in the fact that she nursed the children of all the noblewomen.
Based on this, it can be said that just as we find with Sarah that the miracle of Isaac’s birth caused other miracles for her and in her merit—miracles that brought salvation and rescue to the world ("many barren women were remembered with her... answered with her," and even nursing the children of all the noblewomen)—so too it is regarding Abraham: the miracle of Isaac’s birth caused other miracles for him, in that he fathered more sons in a miraculous and supernatural way (after "Abraham was old, advanced in days"), and these sons are not of the Jewish people, but of the nations of the world (similar to the aforementioned regarding Sarah).

C. However, a reason is required: What is the reason that the miracle of Isaac’s birth caused further miracles so that there would be "laughter... in the world"?
Also, one must understand the difference in this regard between Abraham and Sarah:
1) With Sarah, this effect occurred in the world immediately: "Many barren women were remembered on that day," as well as her nursing the children of Sarah, whereas with Abraham, this occurred over a long period—forty years—after the birth of Isaac.
2) With Sarah, this came about as a natural consequence of Isaac’s birth, without a specific action on Sarah’s part, whereas with Abraham, this was a distinct matter: the miraculous birth of sons—from the nations of the world (from Keturah).
It can be said that this hints at a primary and fundamental matter regarding the influence of the Jewish people upon the nations of the world.
For we find that before the birth of Isaac, there had to be a change in the names of Abraham and Sarah, as Rashi explains : "Abram has no son, but Abraham has a son; and similarly, Sarai will not give birth, but Sarah will give birth." The reason is that "I call you by another name, and the *mazal* changes." Together with this, it is logical to say that the birth of Isaac belongs to the content of the two new names: "Abraham" and "Sarah."
Seemingly, this is puzzling: The content of the names "Abraham" and "Sarah" (in relation to "Abram" and "Sarai") indicates dominion over the entire world—"For I have made you a father of a multitude of nations", not just "Abram"—"father of Aram," but "father of the entire world". And "Sarah" means (as Rashi explains) "[Not] Sarai (which implies) for me and not for others, for Sarah is a general name, that she would be a princess over all." What is the connection between the change of these names and the birth of Isaac, who is the beginning of the Jewish people, "For in Isaac shall your seed be called" ?
This implies that a primary matter in the birth of Isaac (and the Jewish people) is the action (and dominion) over the nations of the world.
Based on this, it can be said that this is the content of the miracles that followed the birth of Isaac, which caused there to be "much laughter... in the world," for thereby their dominion over the entire world was emphasized—that they are the ones who cause salvation to be in the world. In this very matter, there were two types—the miracles belonging to Sarah and the miracles of Abraham (the birth of the sons of Keturah)—corresponding to the difference between the nature of Abraham and the nature of Sarah, as follows.

D. This will be understood by first noting the words of the Rambam regarding the obligation of the seven Noahide laws: "Moses our teacher commanded from the mouth of the Almighty to compel all who come into the world to accept the commandments that the descendants of Noah were commanded," and "Anyone who accepts the seven commandments and is careful to perform them is one of the pious of the nations of the world and has a share in the World to Come, provided that he accepts them and performs them because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moses our teacher, etc. But if he performs them because of his own logical conclusion... he is not one of the pious of the nations of the world."
It is difficult to understand: "Moses our teacher did not bequeath the Torah and commandments except to Israel". What is the connection of the Noahides to the giving of the Torah and to the Jewish people, to the point that the Jewish people must compel all who come into the world to accept the commandments that the Noahides were commanded, and that they should perform them because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded so in the Torah?
This has been explained many times based on the Sages that the entire creation is for the sake of Israel, who are called "beginning," and for the sake of the Torah, which is called "beginning."

## 97

Meaning, this is not something added to the existence of creation, but is connected to its very existence. Therefore, after the birth of the Jewish people and the giving of the Torah, it is impossible for there to be any Divine command to the world without the medium of the Torah and Israel—and therefore the fulfillment of the seven Noahide laws depends on the Torah and Israel.
One must be precise in the aforementioned Rambam, that the two details mentioned above—(a) "Moses our teacher commanded... to compel all who come into the world to accept the commandments that the descendants of Noah were commanded," and (b) that one must perform them "because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moses our teacher"—are written by the Rambam in two separate *halachos*.
Seemingly, from the fact that the Rambam did not mention, in the continuation of the obligation "to compel all who come into the world to accept the commandments," that this obligation includes that their acceptance of the commandments must be because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah, and he wrote this condition ("provided that he accepts them, etc.") in a separate *halacha* regarding the definition of "the pious of the nations of the world," it is understood that this law ("provided that he accepts them... because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah") is a separate law [and it is a matter of inquiry whether there is an obligation upon the Jewish people to compel all who come into the world to become pious of the nations of the world].
But from the fact that the Rambam also brought this condition in the continuation of the obligation for Israel to compel, etc., it implies that even though it is not part of the first obligation, nevertheless, the completeness of the Noahide’s acceptance (which begins through the compulsion of Israel), that he accepts the commandments because of the command of the Holy One, Blessed be He, in the Torah, also has a connection to the Jewish people.
Perhaps it can be said that there are two ways in the actions of the Jewish people upon the nations of the world: 1) Influence through compulsion (whether literally or through the compulsion of matters)—to accept the commandments that the Noahides were commanded; 2) Action indirectly, whereby as a result of this compulsion, the nations of the world come to the recognition that they must accept the commandments "because the Holy One, Blessed be He, commanded them in the Torah and informed us through Moses our teacher, etc.".

E. It can be said that these two ways are also hinted at in the conclusion of the last two chapters of the Rambam’s work, at the end of *Hilchos Melachim* (and their wars):

## 98

At the end of chapter 11, the Rambam concludes: "And He will gather the dispersed of Israel... and will rectify the entire world to serve Hashem together, as it is said, 'For then I will turn to the peoples a clear language, that they may all call upon the name of Hashem, to serve Him with one consent.'" And at the end of chapter 12, he concludes: "And at that time there will be no hunger, etc., for goodness will be abundant, etc., and the entire world will be occupied only with knowing Hashem alone. Therefore, Israel will be great sages and will know the hidden matters and will attain the knowledge of their Creator according to the capacity of man, as it is said, 'For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea.'"
Meaning: At the end of chapter 11, it speaks about the action of Mashiach and the Jewish people upon the nations of the world, "and He will rectify the entire world, etc.," whereas at the end of chapter 12, it speaks about what is brought about in the nations of the world as a matter of course. For the plain meaning of the language "and the entire world will be occupied only with knowing Hashem alone" (which refers to the entirety of the human race, including the nations of the world) is not in a way that the Jewish people will be occupied with them. But nevertheless, this belongs to the Jewish people—meaning that this is a result of the work of the Jewish people, as is proven by the fact that he includes in that same *halacha* both the excellence of the knowledge of Hashem of the Jewish people and the fact that the occupation of the entire world will be to know Hashem. This means that the fact that "the entire world will be occupied only with knowing Hashem alone" will be connected to and a result of the state of the Jewish people at that time.
With this, we can understand the precision in the words of the Rambam in the preceding *halacha* : "The Sages and the prophets did not yearn for the days of Mashiach in order to rule over the entire world, nor to dominate the nations, nor to be exalted by the nations, nor to eat and drink and rejoice, but in order to be free for the Torah and its wisdom." Seemingly, this is puzzling: How could it occur to anyone to interpret that "the Sages and the prophets yearned for the days of Mashiach... in order to rule over the entire world... to dominate the nations... to be exalted by the nations," when even regarding the sages of the nations of the world we find that their main occupation and aspiration is in intellectual matters and they have no desire to rule and dominate, and all the more so is this true regarding the sages and prophets of Israel?
Rather, the Rambam’s intention is that one might have thought that the ultimate level for which the Sages, etc., yearned is to rectify the world—meaning to teach the world the knowledge of Hashem in a way of action and influence (as stated in chapter 11), which is a matter of true rule and domination (spiritually). The Rambam teaches us here that this is not the completeness of the matter, and it is not for this that the Sages and prophets yearned for the days of Mashiach, but in order to be free for the Torah and its wisdom and to be great sages and know the hidden matters.
But nevertheless, he continues, as mentioned above, that even this exalted level of the Jewish people acts upon the world, so that as a matter of course, the state of the entire world will be in an extremely exalted manner, that "the entire world will be occupied only with knowing Hashem alone, etc., as it is said, 'For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea.'"

F. Based on all the above, we can also understand the content of the matter that before the birth of Isaac there had to be a change of name for Abraham and Sarah, "Father of a multitude of nations" and "Princess over all"—because at the beginning of the creation of the Jewish people through the birth of Isaac by Abraham and Sarah, it had to be emphasized that the existence of Israel (is not a matter and detail in creation, but) is the essence and goal of the entirety of creation.
This is also the reason that the birth of Isaac in

## 99
