a miraculous way was in such a manner that it acted upon the nations of the world as mentioned above: "Many barren women were remembered with her, many sick were healed on that day, many prayers were answered with her, and there was much laughter in the world," and the additional influence that was in Sarah in the way of "Sarah would nurse children," and similarly in Abraham, that the wonder and kindness of Abraham in the birth of Isaac also caused him to father more (six) sons from Keturah—because in order for the Jewish people to be able to act upon the nations of the world, there was a special action by the Holy One, Blessed be He, so that the nations of the world would also feel this very thing: that the essence of the world’s existence, including the existence of the Noahides, is Israel.
[Similar to what we find regarding the Torah—that the Sages said : "When the Torah was given to Israel, its voice went from one end of the world to the other, and all the kings of the idolaters were seized with trembling in their palaces, etc. They all gathered to Bilaam the wicked and said to him, 'What is this tumultuous voice that we have heard? Perhaps a flood is coming to the world, etc.' He said to them, 'A good treasure He has in His house of treasures, etc., and He sought to give it to His children, as it is said, "Hashem will give strength to His people."' Immediately they all opened their mouths and said, 'Hashem will bless His people with peace'"].
Based on this, the question is answered simply: It is known that the Holy One, Blessed be He, does not perform a miracle for nothing. This is understood simply, for since the Holy One, Blessed be He, established creation in an orderly and natural way, "they shall not cease", He will not change the orders of creation except for a special need.
If so, it is puzzling: What is the need and necessity that the miracle of Isaac’s birth should bring and cause further miracles—"Sarah would nurse children," etc., as mentioned above—that such a great miracle should be performed, that many barren women were remembered, etc., contrary to the nature of creation?
Rather, there was no additional miracle here, but rather a continuation and result of the totality of the miracle of Isaac’s birth. For the birth of Isaac was not a private miracle, only for Abraham and Sarah, but a general miracle from the outset. In order for the birth of Isaac to take place, Abraham and Sarah were made "father of the entire world" and "princess over all"—and as a matter of course, when the miracle occurred, it was acted upon and extended into "the entire world" and "all," and the orders of creation (including the nations of the world) were changed.

G. Now, in the action of the miracle of Isaac’s birth upon the nations of the world themselves, there were two details (on the part of Abraham and on the part of Sarah, as mentioned above)—which one can say are the root of the two aforementioned ways in the action of Israel upon the nations of the world—corresponding to the difference in the content of the work of Abraham and Sarah, which is also emphasized in their new names given to them before the birth of Isaac:
Abraham, by the name "Father of a multitude of nations," "Father of the entire world," indicates a connection to the "multitude of nations," "the entire world." Therefore, the miraculous influence that was brought about through him upon the "multitude of nations" was through a special action on Abraham’s part (to add to the world)—"And Abraham added and took a wife, etc., and she bore him, etc." (And for this reason it came (not close to the time of Isaac’s birth, but) after a time, because this emphasizes that it came by way of a new action).
This is unlike Sarah, who is "princess over all," which is a category of dominion and royalty, where the king is

## 100

distinct from his people. Therefore, Sarah’s action upon the nations of the world, which came together with the birth of Isaac, was in a way of "as a matter of course," an abundance of blessings among the nations of the world.
The completeness of this matter will be at the end of the time of exile with the coming of Mashiach our righteous one, in the fulfillment of the prophecy : "And it shall be in the end of days that the mountain of Hashem’s house shall be established at the top of the mountains, etc., and all the nations shall flow unto it. And many peoples shall go and say, 'Come, and let us go up to the mountain of Hashem, to the house of the God of Jacob; and He will teach us of His ways, and we will walk in His paths,' etc." —for from the fact that the revelation to the Jewish people will be in ultimate completeness, this will influence as a matter of course many nations and peoples, until the state of "For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea."
(From the *sichos* of Shabbos Parshas Chayei Sarah and Shabbos Parshas Toldos 5745; 19 Kislev 5743)


## תולדות

## Sicha 1

A. The Chida writes in the sefer *Midbar Kedumos* (in the name of R. Yehuda HaChassid): "The fathers of the world, Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov, saw one another, and for fifteen years they studied Torah [together] fifteen hours every day and every night, and 'at fifteen one is fit for Talmud'" (and he enumerates several matters associated with the number fifteen).

The Chida explains there the calculation of "fifteen hours" (specifically), because "nine hours are for sleeping, eating, and attending to one's needs, and fifteen are for Torah and tefillah." He provides a hint for this: "Let every soul praise *Yah* "—that one should study Torah and pray—"praise *Yah* in *Yah* (fifteen) hours."

Now, in *Sefer Chassidim* it states: "Why did the Holy One, blessed be He, love the Patriarchs? Because all day and all night their hearts did not turn away from contemplating the concerns of Heaven... 'And Yaakov awoke from his sleep', and our Sages said 'Yaakov awoke from his sleep' [meaning that even during the time of sleep he was occupied and reviewing Torah], twenty-four verses—Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov in one verse in order... for all twenty-four hours were in one order, that they had no leisure from the concerns of Heaven, even in a dream."

The Chida explains in his commentary on *Sefer Chassidim* there that according to this, one cannot say that R. Yehuda HaChassid’s statement that "they studied Torah... (only) fifteen hours" is because in the other nine hours they were occupied with bodily needs (as he wrote in *Midbar Kedumos* there), for "our Rabbi himself wrote that all twenty-four hours, even in a dream, they were occupied with the concerns of Heaven." Rather, "he means that fifteen hours they were studying Torah, and nine hours they directed their hearts to the Omnipresent to think of His fear, His exaltation, His love, and His attachment"—that is, the engagement in tefillah, as our Sages said that the early Chassidim would occupy themselves with tefillah and its *kavanah* for nine hours a day.

It should be added that this interpretation (that the number "fifteen hours" refers only to Torah study) seems necessary from the words of R. Yehuda HaChassid himself (as cited by the Chida), who wrote, "And fifteen years they studied Torah... fifteen hours, and 'at fifteen one is fit for Talmud,'" from which it is implied that the hours of the service of tefillah are not included in this calculation.

This requires explanation:
From the fact that the Patriarchs "saw one another" for only fifteen years, as R. Yehuda HaChassid says that "fifteen years they studied, etc., fifteen hours" [because the birth of Yitzchak occurred when Avraham Avinu was one hundred years old, and Yaakov was born when Yitzchak was sixty years old, at which time Avraham was one hundred and sixty, and the years of Avraham’s life were one hundred and seventy-five, so only fifteen years (at the end of Avraham’s life) were all three Patriarchs together]—it is understood that there is a special elevation in these fifteen hours that they studied Torah together (corresponding to the fifteen years they saw one another) relative to the other hours of the day.

Now, according to the Chida’s explanation in *Midbar Kedumos* that the fifteen hours are hours of Torah and tefillah, and the remaining nine hours were for bodily needs, the elevation of these fifteen hours is understood. For although it is simple that even the bodily needs of the Patriarchs were performed for the sake of Heaven in absolute perfection [as the Alter Rebbe writes in *Tanya*, "The Patriarchs... all their days never ceased even for one hour from connecting their consciousness and their soul to the Master of the Worlds," and in another place he specifies and explains that they were "in the state of a *merkavah* in all their actions, even in their sleep"], nevertheless, this is not comparable to the elevation of the engagement in Torah and tefillah itself.

However, according to his explanation in his commentary on *Sefer Chassidim*—that the remaining nine hours are the time for the engagement in tefillah—it is not understood why the fifteen hours of Torah study should be so different from the nine hours of tefillah, such that for this reason they "saw one another" only fifteen years (corresponding to the number of the fifteen hours of the day and night that they studied Torah together)?

B. One may explain this according to the path of Chassidus:
It is known that the three Patriarchs, Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov, correspond to the three pillars upon which the world stands : Torah, *avodah*, and *gemilus chasadim*. The primary *avodah* of Avraham was *gemilus chasadim*; Yitzchak—*avodah* (as our Sages said that Yitzchak was a "perfect burnt-offering"); and Yaakov’s essence is Torah, as it is written, "And Yaakov was a wholesome man, dwelling in tents." Thus they are divided in the *avodah* of the *midos*: the *midah* of Avraham is the *midah* of *Chesed* and love; the *midah* of Yitzchak is *Gevurah* and fear; and that of Yaakov is *Tiferes* and mercy.

Accordingly, the fact that the Patriarchs "saw one another" and were together for fifteen years indicates the connection of them all together. Although each of these three modes of *avodah* is distinct from the other, the three Patriarchs connected together to serve Hashem. This is not merely a physical connection, but a connection of the modes of serving Hashem together.

Therefore, their connection was only regarding the study of Torah, because this connection of three distinct *avodos* together exists only on the level of Torah study, as the matters and *avodos* are on the level of Torah, in which "these and these are the words of the living G-d." This is not the case in *avodah* in practice, in the *avodah* of the *midos*, and certainly not in actual deed, where it is impossible for all these types and modes of *avodah* to be identical.

This is also the reason for the distinction: the fact that the Patriarchs studied Torah together fifteen hours a day found expression in the physical life of the Patriarchs, that they lived together for fifteen years. Whereas the fact that they served Hashem all twenty-four hours of the day is hinted at only in that there are "twenty-four verses—Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov in one verse in order"—because the matter of the connection (the *avodah*) of the three Patriarchs together on the level of Torah study was in actuality. This is not the case for their connection together in the *avodah* of Hashem during all twenty-four hours of the day; that is only as it is in Torah ("twenty-four verses—Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov in one verse in order"), but in actual deed, the *avodah* of one of them is not similar to that of another, etc..

C. Explaining the matter:
Torah, even when it was given below, is His wisdom, may He be blessed, as it is above. Just as with Him, may He be blessed, all the *midos* dwell together in complete unity—for they are all details in the revelation of His perfection, and He "includes them all together"—so too it is in His Torah, that there is no contradiction between all types of *avodah*, for His wisdom, may He be blessed, necessitates them all ("these and these are the words of the living G-d"). This is not the case in *avodah* in practice, from the side of man, for every created being is an entity, and therefore every other *avodah* (and *midah*) is contrary to the other, and thus there is a contradiction between the types of *avodah*.

This is also the case from the side of man himself, for in the *mochin* of a person there is not as much division as in the *midos* of the heart. As we see, it is difficult for a person to feel closeness (love) and distance (fear) toward one thing at the same time. This is not the case from the side of the *mochin*; it is possible for one person to grasp at once opposing arguments regarding one matter, both the arguments for obligation and the arguments for merit. Likewise in *avodah*: when a person is occupied with understanding the differences in the modes of *avodah*, it is possible that the understanding of *avodos* that are utterly distinct from one another can be "placed" in his intellect, but when it comes to action, he is limited to one line specifically.

And more subtly:
It is known that *mochin* are called "nothingness" (*ayin*) relative to the *midos*, which are "somethingness" (*yesh*). This is because the essence of intellect is the grasping and attainment of the concept as it is in its truth, without the mixture of the entity and state of the person attaining it. This is not the case with the content of the *midos*, which is the opposite—one's relationship to the thing outside of oneself, that is, the effect of the thing upon the person who feels it, whether to draw close to it (love) or to distance oneself from it (fear).

So it is in the *avodah* of Hashem: the *avodah* of the *midos* is the feeling of the person, that he loves Hashem because "the closeness of G-d is good for me", and therefore he wants to draw close to Him, may He be blessed (and similarly with fear, etc.). This is not the case with the *avodah* of the *mochin*, which is what "emerges" from the boundaries of his own entity and grasps and attains His wisdom, may He be blessed, as it is.

Therefore, in the *midos* of man, which are connected with his "entity," there is the matter of division with force. This is not the case in the *mochin*, in which a person attains according to what His wisdom, may He be blessed, has necessitated; it is possible for there to be a connection of all types of *avodos* together.

D. One may say that the fact that the connection of the *avodos* of the three Patriarchs together is only in their Torah study (*mochin*) is because they were before the Giving of the Torah, before the nullification of the decree between "the upper ones" and "the lower ones," as explained in the Midrash that before the Giving of the Torah there was the decree that "the heavens are the heavens of Hashem, and the earth He gave to the children of man", that the upper ones...

[...The text continues to discuss the historical parallel between the Patriarchs and the "Fathers of Chassidus," noting that the Patriarchs were together for fifteen years, and the "Fathers of Chassidus" (the Baal Shem Tov, the Maggid, and the Alter Rebbe) were also together for fifteen years. It further notes that the "Fathers of Chabad Chassidus" (the Alter Rebbe, the Mitteler Rebbe, and the Tzemach Tzedek) were together for twenty-four years, paralleling the twenty-four hours of the day, symbolizing the penetration of Chassidus into all aspects of life, including the physical, just as the Giving of the Torah connected the upper and lower realms.]

(From a Sicha of 19 Kislev, 5722)

## Sicha 2

### 107

### Sicha 2

a. We learned in the end of Kiddushin: "We find that Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given, as it is stated: 'Because Avraham listened to My voice and kept My charge, My commandments, My statutes, and My teachings.'"

And in Yoma we read: Rav said, "Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah, as it is stated, 'Because Avraham listened to My voice, etc.'" (And the Gemara challenges: "But perhaps it means only the seven [Noachide] commandments? But there is also circumcision, etc.")

It has already been asked: Since it is an explicit Mishnah at the end of Kiddushin that Avraham performed the entire Torah, what is Rav coming to teach us in his statement in Maseches Yoma? (Furthermore: Instead of challenging Rav’s statement here with "Perhaps it means the seven commandments," why not challenge the Mishnah in Kiddushin?)

The Maharsha answers that the Mishnah uses the phrasing "Avraham Avinu *performed* (asah)," which implies only that he performed the positive commandments. However, Rav’s phrasing, "fulfilled (kayam) the entire Torah," implies the negative commandments as well (and therefore, the challenge to Rav, "Perhaps it means the seven commandments," is valid).

This requires study: Even if the language of the Mishnah is "performed... the entire Torah," why would it enter our minds that Avraham performed only the positive commandments and did not guard himself from violating the negative ones? Surely Avraham strove to fulfill the entire Torah to the extent that was possible for him (especially according to the version of the Mishnah before us — "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah").

[As for why the Shas does not challenge "Perhaps it means the seven commandments" at the end of Kiddushin as it does in Yoma regarding Rav, one can say simply that in Kiddushin this is not the primary location of this subject (Avraham’s fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos). It is brought in the Mishnah only as additional proof for the effect of Torah "in his old age" (following the first proof: "In his youth, what does it say? 'Those who hope in Hashem shall renew their strength.' In his old age, what does it say? 'They shall still bring forth fruit in old age.' Note that in the Beraisa there

## 108

only the first proof is brought, and the proof from Avraham is not brought)].

Simply put, one can say that the innovation in Rav’s statement regarding the Mishnah is that the Mishnah refers only to Avraham’s *study* of Torah, whereas Rav innovates that he also *fulfilled* all the mitzvos.

This is also understood from the continuation of the matters there. In the Mishnah, this is brought at the end of Rabbi Nehorai’s statement: "I set aside every craft in the world and teach my son only Torah, for a person eats of its fruit in this world and the principal remains for the World to Come... and so it says regarding Avraham Avinu: 'And Avraham was old, and Hashem blessed Avraham with everything.' We find that Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given, etc." — this refers to the matter of Torah study. In contrast, Rav’s statement in Yoma appears in continuation of what is explained there previously, that Avraham was old and sat in the Yeshiva (and the Yeshiva never departed from our forefathers, etc.), regarding which the Shas brings Rav’s statement: that in addition to the fact that Avraham studied Torah, furthermore, Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah (and regarding this, the Shas asks from where we know that Avraham Avinu fulfilled the mitzvos, etc.).

However, even though this interpretation fits well with Rav’s language, "Avraham Avinu fulfilled," it is still difficult to explain the language of the Mishnah (in Kiddushin), "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah," as referring only to Torah study and not to the actual performance of mitzvos. After all, it brings proof from the verse, "Because Avraham listened to My voice and kept My charge, My commandments, My statutes, and My teachings," which explicitly mentions the keeping and performance of mitzvos.

b. Some commentators explain that the Mishnah implies that Avraham did so of his own volition (as emphasized in the Mishnah, "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah *before it was given*"), whereas Rav used the word "fulfilled," which implies that he was commanded to do so and fulfilled that command, and he derives this from the fact that it is written, "Because Avraham listened to My voice," which implies that Hashem commanded him to do so.

This is also implied in Rashi in Yoma there: Regarding the Shas’s challenge to Rav’s statement, "Perhaps it means the seven commandments," Rashi explains, "The seven commandments that the rest of the children of Noach were commanded, but the Torah was not given to him" — this implies that he interprets Rav’s words as meaning that Avraham fulfilled the Torah *after it was given* (and Rav Shimi’s challenge refers to this).

But on the face of it, it is a great innovation to say that Rav holds that Avraham was commanded to fulfill the entire Torah, as the Chida asked: "There is no opinion that Avraham Avinu, peace be upon him, was commanded more than circumcision and the seven commandments; rather, he reasoned and accepted it upon himself."

c. It seems to me regarding this, and by way of introduction: regarding the definition of Torah and mitzvos in the days of the Patriarchs before it was given, there are

## 109

two ways to explain it: 1) The *cheftza* (essence/object) of Torah and mitzvos exists (for the Torah preceded the world by two thousand years), but there was not yet a command to the *gavra* (the person), because it had not yet been given below. According to this, the fulfillment of mitzvos before the Giving of the Torah is in the category of "one who is not commanded and yet performs" (which we find after the Giving of the Torah), where the Torah and mitzvos exist, but the person is not commanded regarding them. 2) Before the Giving of the Torah, the *cheftza* of Torah and mitzvos did not exist in the world; not only was there no command to the person, but the *cheftza* itself was created (in the world) at the time of the Giving of the Torah.

It can be said that this is the innovation in Rav’s statement regarding the Mishnah:
In the Mishnah, which emphasizes "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah *before it was given*," the intention is that before the Giving of the Torah, the definition of "fulfillment of a mitzvah" did not apply to the Patriarchs' performance of Torah and mitzvos, but rather it was merely an act, since the entire *cheftza* was created at the time of the Giving of the Torah.

Whereas Rav innovates that Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah, meaning that even though the Torah was not yet given, nevertheless, the *cheftza* of Torah and mitzvos existed, only that he was not commanded to fulfill them. Thus, the term "fulfillment" of a mitzvah can properly apply to Avraham’s act [similar to women in the fulfillment of positive time-bound mitzvos, where even though they are not commanded regarding them, they can nevertheless perform them, and it is considered the fulfillment of a mitzvah, and we hold according to the opinions that women can recite the blessing on the fulfillment of positive time-bound mitzvos: "Who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us"].

d. Yet, it still requires explanation: What is the relevance to the content of the Mishnah’s words of the emphasis that "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given," that there was not even the *cheftza* of Torah, whereas in the passage in Yoma, the emphasis (in Rav’s statement) that there was also the *cheftza* of Torah for Avraham is relevant?

This will be understood by first explaining the addition in the proof from Avraham, "And so it says regarding Avraham Avinu," over the first proof from the verse, "They shall still bring forth fruit in old age":
The proof from the verse, "They shall still bring forth fruit in old age," can be interpreted as meaning that "Torah is not like this (other crafts), but rather it guards him from all evil in his youth and gives him an end and hope in his old age." As Rashi explains, "But Torah is not like this, for even one who comes to old age or suffering and cannot engage in it, he eats of the reward... for it is written, 'They shall still bring forth fruit in old age,'" which is in the category of reward for Torah study (and fulfillment of mitzvos) in his youth,

## 110

or at the very least, the merit of the Torah itself, the very *cheftza* of Torah that protects and saves even in his old age. Regarding this, the second proof comes to teach us that this is also from the side of the (merit of the) act of the person, as we find with Avraham, "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given," even though the entire definition of Torah did not exist then, and yet it is said of him, "And Avraham was old, and Hashem blessed Avraham with everything."

It can be said that likewise in Rav’s statement in Yoma there, the precision "Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah" is relevant, because there was already a *cheftza* of mitzvah, and consequently, there is the definition of "fulfillment of a mitzvah" in Avraham’s act.

Earlier in the Gemara there, it says: "The prayer of Avraham—from when the walls darken. Rav Yosef said: We should arise and learn from Avraham (and the Aruch explains: How can we learn from Avraham who fulfilled the Torah before it was given? We should only learn from the custom of the prophets, because they practiced it as a law given to Moshe at Sinai). Rava said: The Tanna learned from Avraham, and we do not learn from him? For it is taught: 'On the eighth day he shall circumcise the flesh of his foreskin'—this teaches that the entire day is valid for circumcision, but the zealous ones hasten to perform mitzvos, as it is stated: 'And Avraham arose early in the morning and saddled, etc.' Rather, Rava said: Rav Yosef, this is what is difficult for him, etc. Or perhaps Avraham is different because he had great astrological wisdom. Or perhaps because he was old and sat in the Yeshiva, etc."

Based on the aforementioned interpretation of the Aruch, it can be said that this is why the Shas brings Rav’s statement, "Avraham Avinu fulfilled the entire Torah," immediately afterward. This is not just incidental to the passage regarding Torah study and Avraham’s fulfillment of mitzvos, but rather, since there was the definition of "fulfillment of a mitzvah" in Avraham’s act, because the *cheftza* of Torah existed, therefore it is possible to learn (also) from Avraham, even though it was before the command to fulfill them [and according to this, it is explained why it is possible to learn from Avraham not only the matter of zeal in the fulfillment of mitzvos, but also regarding the fulfillment of mitzvos itself].

e. Now, the aforementioned explanation of the Mishnah’s language, "Avraham Avinu performed the entire Torah before it was given," also fits with the inner meaning of "performed" (asah)—that he acted and innovated within it (similar to the language of the verse, "And the souls that they made in Charan," and according to the simple interpretation of our Sages that it refers to the "making" of converts, that "Avraham converted the men and Sarah converted the women").

This means that Avraham, through his performance and his *avodah*, "performed"—he acted and drew down the Torah for the sake of the Children of Israel. As our Sages say regarding several things, "The acts of the fathers are a sign for the children," and the meaning is that it is a source of strength for the children. So it is regarding the entirety of the matter of Torah, that through Avraham’s *avodah* with *mesiras nefesh* for Torah and mitzvos, he "performed" and drew down the Torah to his children, all the Children of Israel after him.

[And as it is in connection to the souls of Israel, since "the Patriarchs are the Chariot ('that all their limbs were entirely holy and separated from the matters of this world, and they were made a chariot only for the Supreme Will alone all their days'), and therefore they merited to draw down *nefesh, ruach, neshamah* to their children after them forever from the ten *sefirot* of holiness"].

f. It can be said that in our Torah study as well, there are levels similar to these: Torah as it is from the perspective of the command; where there is no command but the *cheftza* exists; and a mode that does not relate to a command at all, as if the *cheftza* is also not there:

1) Study from the perspective of the general obligation that applies to everyone, that each person "fulfills his obligation by setting times for Torah study... by day and by night, in order to study and remember well the study that leads to action only, which is an obligation upon all—that is, the Shulchan Aruch, from the laws that every person needs to know, etc.—and the rest of the day he shall engage in business so that he can support the hands of Torah scholars";

2) The fulfillment of "this mitzvah (of Torah study) properly (by) working a little every day only for his needs and removing the desires and pleasures of the time from his heart, as the Sages said: 'Such is the way of Torah: eat bread with salt, etc.,'" meaning that he makes his "work temporary." This obligation is not incumbent upon every Jew, for "he is not obligated to live a life of suffering and make his work temporary in order to increase study that he does not understand thoroughly, etc., unless he acts with the measure of *chassidus* and love of Torah", but if he does so, he merits the "crown of Torah";

3) Torah study in a manner where the person trusts in Hashem completely and does not work at all, similar to those who ate the *man*, and as Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai said: "Is it possible for a person to plow, etc.? What will become of Torah? Rather, at the time that Israel does the will of the Omnipresent, their work is done by others, etc."

It can be said that the latter two modes, for which there is no command for everyone, are similar to the two aforementioned modes regarding the definition of Avraham’s Torah—if the *cheftza* of Torah exists but he was not commanded, similar to the second mode, where there is a definition of such study where his Torah is permanent and his work is entirely temporary ("'Every day a little for his needs'... temporary work will suffice for him through the salvation of Hashem and His success, without mentioning miraculous acts"), but the person is not obligated in this except from the measure of *chassidus* and love of Torah. Above this is the mode of trusting in Hashem completely that He will provide all his needs, and he engages only in Torah. The definition of such study is similar to Torah before it was given, where not only is there no obligation, but according to *halacha*, we do not find such a reality to rely on a miracle.

This is what Rabbi Nehorai emphasizes: "I set aside every craft in the world and teach my son only Torah." He does not come to disagree with Rabbi Meir before him, "A person should always teach his son a craft," but rather he comes to teach us from his own *maaseh rav* (great act): "I set aside every craft in the world and teach my son only Torah" (as explained in the commentaries), which is specifically because of the greatness of his trust in Hashem, he teaches his son only Torah. But this is not the path demanded of every person; for every person, his Torah must be permanent and his work temporary, as mentioned above, according to the words of Rabbi Meir.

It is also precise in the words of Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai, "At the time that Israel *does* (oseh) the will of the Omnipresent, their work is done by others"—meaning that the Children of Israel "do" and innovate His will, for from the perspective of Hashem’s command, there is no place for this conduct. Rather, individuals of distinction, because of the greatness of their trust in Hashem, do and innovate the will of the Omnipresent, and then, in a natural way, their work is done by others, as the Holy One, Blessed be He, fulfills all their needs in a miraculous way, entirely above nature.

(From the Sicha of 6 Tishrei 5745)

## Sicha 3

### 113

A. At the conclusion of our Parsha, it is stated: "And Esau saw that the daughters of Canaan were evil in the eyes of Isaac... and Esau went to Ishmael and took Mahalath the daughter of Ishmael... in addition to his wives, to be his wife."

In Midrash Rabbah, two opinions are brought regarding the interpretation of this verse: "Rabbi Joshua ben Levi said: He set his mind to convert (to do teshuvah); 'Mahalath' [is so named] because the Holy One, Blessed be He, forgave (machal) him for all his sins, etc. Rabbi Elazar said: If he had put away the first ones, it would have been well, but 'in addition to his wives'—pain upon pain (the first ones were evil and the second ones likewise. Rashi to Bereishis Rabbah). Another explanation: thorn upon thorn, an addition to a house that was already full (the house was full of foreign (accursed) wives, and he added to them. Rashi)."

Based on this, one must examine the position of Rashi in his commentary on the Torah. Here, he explains "in addition to his wives" as: "He added wickedness to his wickedness, in that he did not divorce the first ones," which follows the view of Rabbi Elazar in the Midrash. Yet, later in Parshas Vayishlach, regarding the verse "Basemath the daughter of Ishmael," Rashi comments: "And earlier it calls her Mahalath... there are three whose sins are forgiven... and one who marries a wife... therefore she is called Mahalath, because his sins were forgiven"—which indicates that he adopted the view of Rabbi Joshua ben Levi (that his sins were forgiven)!

Now, regarding the conduct of Esau, one might attempt to reconcile this by saying that even in his commentary in Parshas Vayishlach, Rashi does not mean (as Rabbi Joshua ben Levi says in the Midrash) that "his sins were forgiven" because "he set his mind to convert." Rather, the very act of "marrying a wife" possesses a virtue that leads to the forgiveness of sins (similar to "one who ascends to greatness," as cited in Rashi there). Thus, Esau’s sins were forgiven even though he did not set his mind to convert (and on the contrary, "he added wickedness to his wickedness").

However, we must clarify the matter of "Mahalath" herself:
From the plain meaning of the Midrash, it is understood that the two opinions regarding Esau's intent (whether he "set his mind to convert" or the opposite) also disagree regarding the nature and essence of "Mahalath the daughter of Ishmael"—specifically, whether she was a righteous woman. According to Rabbi Joshua ben Levi, she was a righteous woman (and therefore she is called "Mahalath"—a name hinting at the forgiveness (mechilah) of sins). According to Rabbi Elazar, she was a wicked woman, like Esau’s first wives (and this is the meaning of "in addition to his wives," that she was similar to his first wives).

If so, Rashi’s statements contradict one another: here (at the end of our Parsha) he maintains that she was wicked, whereas later he explains the name "Mahalath" as being on account of the forgiveness of sins, which implies that she was a righteous woman.
[For even if you say that the act of "marrying a wife" itself brings about the forgiveness of sins (even without a change in deeds for the better)—nevertheless, from the very fact

## 114

that only she is called by the name "Mahalath" (which hints at the forgiveness of sins), and not his first wives, it implies that she was worthy of this (also) on account of her own deeds, etc.
Especially according to the version in Rashi found in several manuscripts—"that her sins were forgiven" (and not "his sins"), which implies that only her sins were forgiven and not Esau’s, and this is because she was righteous, unlike Esau, who remained wicked.]

B. But upon closer inspection, it appears that the difficulty does not exist from the outset, because Rashi’s intent in his commentary here is not like the interpretation of Rabbi Elazar in the Midrash, but rather a new interpretation of the words of the text:

The language of the Midrash is: "If he had put away the first ones, it would have been well, but 'in addition to his wives'—pain upon pain, etc." However, Rashi changed this and wrote: "He added wickedness to his wickedness, in that he did not divorce the first ones." There are two (primary) changes here:

(a) "Pain upon pain" (thorn upon thorn) means that he added a wicked wife to the wicked wives he already had. But Rashi’s language, "He added wickedness to his wickedness," means that the very act of marrying Mahalath is an act of wickedness on Esau’s part.
[In the common printings of Rashi, there is an added gloss in parentheses: "meaning, a wicked woman upon the wicked women he already had."
However, it must be that the glossator does not mean that "wickedness (rish'ah) upon his wickedness" means "a wicked woman (rish'ah) upon the wicked women he already had" (where "rish'ah" is vocalized with a kamatz, meaning the wife is wicked, like "the wicked kingdom of Greece")—for if so, Rashi should have written "a wicked woman upon his wicked women". Since Rashi wrote "upon his wickedness (rish'ato)," it implies that the resh in the word "rish'ah" is vocalized with a chirik (as in "and all wickedness shall vanish like smoke"), and it refers to Esau’s own wickedness.
It is more plausible to say that the glossator’s intent is only to explain and clarify that Esau’s wickedness (which he added to his previous wickedness) is that he married a wicked woman in addition to the wicked women he already had.
But even according to this, it is difficult to see how one can interpret Rashi this way, for Rashi did not mention at all that Mahalath was wicked. If that were Rashi’s intent, he would have written it in clear language: "He added a wicked woman" (or similar).]

(b) The phrase "If he had put away the first ones, it would have been well" in the Midrash appears at the beginning of the matter, whereas Rashi reverses the order and writes this phrase ("in that he did not divorce the first ones") at the end of the matter.

C. It can be said that these two changes are interdependent:
According to the Midrash, Esau’s wickedness lay in the fact that he married another wicked woman, "pain upon pain," and the statement "If he had put away the first ones, it would have been well" is merely a preface to refute the view of Rabbi Joshua ben Levi (mentioned earlier) that "he set his mind to convert," which cannot be said, because if so, he should have divorced the first ones.
In contrast, according to Rashi, Mahalath was not wicked, and this is why Rashi wrote "He added wickedness to his wickedness, in that he did not divorce the first ones,"

## 115

meaning that this itself is the wickedness that Esau added now —that he did not divorce the first ones, as will be explained in section D.
It is compelled by the context of the Parsha that Mahalath was not wicked, for the entire reason Esau married the daughter of Ishmael is because he saw "that the daughters of Canaan were evil in the eyes of Isaac his father," and he wanted to bring satisfaction to his father and marry a woman who would be upright in his eyes. Therefore, he went to Ishmael to marry a woman of noble lineage specifically [this is the precision of the verse's language: "(And Esau went to Ishmael) and he took Mahalath the daughter of Ishmael son of Abraham"—for seemingly "son of Abraham" is superfluous, and what does it come to teach us ? Rather, by this the verse emphasizes that Esau married a woman of noble lineage specifically, the daughter of "the son of Abraham"]—if so, it is logical to say that he took a righteous woman, and not a woman like his first wives who were "a bitterness of spirit to Isaac and to Rebecca".

D. Yet the matter still requires explanation—how can it be said that his wickedness is that "he did not divorce the first ones"? How does that relate to "and he took Mahalath, etc."? The wickedness of "not divorcing the first ones" had been with him for a long time (ever since he married them). What is the matter that then "he added wickedness to his wickedness (by this) that he did not divorce the first ones"?

The explanation for this can be stated simply:
The wickedness of Esau in taking his first wives was not only that he married women who were "a bitterness of spirit to Isaac and to Rebecca," but also because of the hypocrisy and deceit in these marriages. As Rashi comments on the verse "And Esau was forty years old"—"Esau was compared to a pig... just as this pig, when it lies down, extends its hooves to say 'See that I am pure,' so too these [descendants of Esau] rob and plunder and yet show themselves as righteous. For forty years Esau hunted women... when he was forty, he said, 'My father was forty when he married a wife, I too shall do so.'"

This is also what (the verse and) Rashi are saying at the end of our Parsha—"And he took Mahalath, etc., in addition to his wives," "He added wickedness to his wickedness in that he did not divorce the first ones"—for just as his first marriage was an act of hypocrisy (so that they would say he conducts himself like Isaac his father), so too in the marriage of Mahalath he added wickedness similar to his previous wickedness: he took a righteous woman only so that Isaac would say he is doing teshuvah, at the very time that it was nothing but hypocrisy, for "he did not divorce the first ones". In other words: marrying a righteous woman while his other wives are not righteous indicates that this marriage is hypocrisy and wickedness.

[According to this, the words "in addition to his wives" in the verse do not come to teach the common denominator of Esau’s wives (similar to the above in the Midrash—"pain upon pain"), but rather to describe Esau’s wickedness: that even though he now took a righteous woman, it was nevertheless "in addition to his wives"—"in that he did not divorce the first ones."]

E. The "inner dimension of Torah" in Rashi’s commentary:
The stories of the Torah in general are not merely stories, G-d forbid, but are there because of the instructions in the service of Hashem that are hidden within them (Torah comes from the word *hora'ah*—instruction). All the more so, the stories of the generations of Esau, which "were not written and considered worthy to be explained" in the Torah unless every detail in them contained an instruction in the service of the Children of Israel.

From this it is understood regarding our subject as well, that the story at the end of our Parsha about Esau’s marriage to Mahalath the daughter of Ishmael relates to the service of the Children of Israel. And in this itself there is a special lesson according to Rashi’s commentary that Esau’s "wickedness" is his "hypocrisy." That is to say: the flaw in hypocrisy is not only because it misleads and deceives, but that "hypocrisy" itself is the (essence of the) "wickedness" of Esau.

This will be understood by prefacing the general difference between the generations of Esau in Parshas Toldos and those in Parshas Vayishlach—in our Parsha, it is told (primarily) about the matters of Esau that relate to Isaac ("And these are the generations of Isaac"), whereas in Parshas Vayishlach, the history of Esau is presented in relation to Jacob.

The main difference between them: regarding Isaac, Esau strove to deceive him into thinking that he was a tzaddik, as Rashi comments on the verse "for hunting was in his mouth," whereas toward Jacob his brother, he went in open warfare.

These two aspects of Esau are an instruction in the service of every Jew, that there are two types of evil—open evil, and hidden evil that is covered and concealed within good. And just as one must stand with all one's strength against open evil (which one cannot be mistaken about)—so too, and even more so, one must be wary of the mixture of good and evil, hidden evil; on the contrary, the war against this evil is more difficult.

This is similar to what we find with Jacob himself, who stayed in the house of Laban for twenty years, whereas his time with Esau lasted only one day (as it is said "And Esau returned on that day to his way to Seir"). It is explained that "Laban the Aramean" represents deception and fraud, and in order to overcome fraud and nullify it, a long time is required. In contrast, Esau, who went in open warfare against Jacob, represents open evil, which Jacob overcame quickly.

It can be said that the fraud and hypocrisy of Esau toward Isaac is lesser and worse than the "fraud" of Laban toward Jacob. For in the concealment of evil within good, there are two modes: a) by concealing the evil as much as possible, b) by emphasizing and publicizing the good. This is the difference between Laban and Esau: Laban only deceived Jacob so that he would not recognize his evil intent, but Esau acted like that pig, which "extends its hooves to say 'See that I am pure,'" not only hiding the impure sign within him but performing an act to publicize his "righteousness"—and the service to clarify and nullify this fraud is much harder than the nullification of mere fraud.

It can be said that this is also the reason the Torah tells of Esau’s marriage to Mahalath the daughter of Ishmael, for in this the verse reveals to us the extent of Esau’s hypocrisy, that he went in a manner of "adding wickedness to his wickedness," that his "extending of his hooves" was to such an extent that because of this he took a truly righteous woman, while remaining Esau the wicked, whose entire previous wickedness did not depart from him at all, for "he did not divorce the first ones."

From this there is an instruction for every Jew, how much one must be wary of the matter of hypocrisy, for it is possible that the *yetzer* will agree that a person should perform good deeds, while his wickedness has not departed from him at all!

F. According to this explanation regarding the matter of Esau the wicked, one can also better understand the words of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe regarding the reason for the length of this final exile. He writes :

"The matter of the length of this exile compared to the earlier exiles must be understood through the wars of Jacob with Esau, which were for a short time, as he fought with him and defeated him, whereas with Laban he stayed for twenty-two years... until it is said 'And Jacob came whole'. [The explanation is] because Esau... what his heart desired, he would quickly do, for he went in the stubbornness of his heart, only evil all the day, and everything was in the open; he said what he wanted and was not in a state of fraud, and it is easy to fight with him so that he does not do his will in all the things he desires and craves... And so in the earlier generations, it was in the open; whoever was wicked was not ashamed to do evil... And therefore the Sages said that the earlier generations, whose sin was revealed, their end was revealed, but the later generations, whose sin was not revealed, etc., it means that they cover their sin and the deception of others is not recognizable, that he can commit a sin... but in secret, and he is ashamed to transgress in the open... The evil is hidden and concealed and covers itself in the service of Hashem... And this was the war of Jacob with Laban, who was a great deceiver, and it was most difficult because he also had to conduct himself with him with fraud, for it is impossible otherwise, and therefore their war lasted a long time, and this is the length of this exile, which is the category of Laban." End quote.

Seemingly, it is not understood: it is known that this final exile is the exile of Edom, for "Esau is Edom", and according to the above, it is explained that this exile relates to Laban specifically, and not to Esau who went in the open "and is not in a state of fraud"?

According to the above, it is resolved: for in Esau himself there are two modes: toward Jacob his brother, Esau went in open warfare, and therefore Jacob overcame him without a long duration (and Jacob’s war against the attribute of "fraud" was while he was with Laban the Aramean, and therefore he had to stay there twenty years); in contrast, toward Isaac his father, Esau conducted himself with fraud, and even more so than the fraud of Laban as mentioned above, and this "clarification" of Esau is even harder than the clarification of Laban.

This is the reason that this final exile (whose service is to nullify this fraud as mentioned above) is the exile of Edom specifically, which is compared to a pig (as it is in the Midrash "And the pig, this is Edom"), which "extends its hooves to say 'See that I am pure'"—this is the reason for the length of this exile, because the primary clarification in it is to nullify such hidden evil, which in addition to being mixed with good (like with Laban), furthermore, they "extend" (emphasize) the good outwardly to say "See that I am pure."

G. It can be said that the reason this hypocrisy of Esau was only toward Isaac is based on what is known regarding the blessings of Isaac, that the fact that Isaac wanted to bless Esau specifically is because Isaac "looked" at Esau, saw him as he is from his root, and saw the good hidden within him (like the souls of the converts who came from him, "such as Rabbi Meir, and Onkelos, and Shemaiah and Avtalyon, and Obadiah"), and he said that through the blessings, this good would come out from concealment into revelation (even in Esau himself), in a way that would completely nullify the evil within him.

So too in our case: the "good" that is in Esau—which is what gives place to his fraud, to mix good with evil and even to extend his hooves and say I am pure, etc.—is from the good hidden in his root, and therefore the clarification of this evil that is in Esau relates to Isaac specifically.

This is also the content of the service of the Jewish people in this final exile, the exile of Edom, to clarify even this evil of Esau (the father of Edom), until the time of the end, when the good hidden within him will be revealed (as it was seen by Isaac),

And through this, they also transform "Edom" to good—as the Sages say "The pig is destined to become pure" (which also hints at Edom, "the pig, this is Edom")—in the fulfillment of the prophecy "And saviors shall ascend Mount Zion to judge the Mount of Esau, and the kingdom shall be Hashem’s," speedily in our days, truly.

(From the Sichos of Shabbos Parshas Toldos and Shabbos Parshas Vayishlach, 5727; Shabbos Parshas Vayetzei, 5740)


## ויצא

## Sicha 1

### 119

A. At the beginning of our Parsha, at the start of the narrative of Yaakov’s journey to Charan, it is stated, "And Yaakov departed from Be’er Sheva, etc." Regarding the phrasing "departed" (*Vayetzei*—rather than "and he went," *Vayelech*, as was used twice in the previous section), Rashi explains: "(It should have only been necessary to write 'And Yaakov went to Charan'; why then does it mention his departure? Rather,) it tells us that the departure of a tzaddik from a place leaves an impression. For while the tzaddik is in the city, he is its splendor, he is its radiance, he is its majesty. Once he departs from there, its splendor has departed, its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed. And similarly, 'And she departed from the place' is stated regarding Naomi and Ruth."

The source for this explanation is in Bereishis Rabbah on the verse, which states: "When the tzaddik is in the city, he is its radiance, he is its majesty. Once he departs from there, its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed. And similarly, 'And she departed from the place where she had been, etc.'—'And she departed'—did only she depart from the place?... When the tzaddik is in the city, he is its radiance, he is its majesty. Once he departs from there, its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed. This is understandable there, for only that righteous woman was there. But here, where Yitzchak and Rivkah were present? Rabbi Azaryah said in the name of Rabbi Simon: The merit of one tzaddik is not comparable to the merit of two tzaddikim."

It requires study: Why did Rashi not address the question and answer found in the Midrash—that although the cases are not comparable, because here Yitzchak and Rivkah remained in the place from which Yaakov departed, whereas in the case of Naomi, not a single tzaddik remained—the merit of three tzaddikim is not comparable to the merit of two? After all, the question and answer are relevant even according to the plain meaning (*peshat*).

[It is very forced to explain that Rashi holds that the place of Yitzchak and Rivkah’s residence at that time was in the city of Chevron (similar to what is written in the Midrash there earlier: "It is already written 'And [Yaakov] listened to his father and mother,' so why does the text say 'And Yaakov departed from Be’er Sheva'? Rather, he said: 'My father, when he sought to go abroad, where did he receive permission? Was it not from Be’er Sheva? I, too, will go to Be’er Sheva; if he gives me permission, I will depart, and if not, I will not.' Therefore, the text needed to say 'And Yaakov departed from Be’er Sheva'"). Thus, Rashi did not need to explain that the merit of one tzaddik is not comparable, etc., because since Yitzchak and Rivkah were living in Chevron at the time, while Yaakov’s departure was "from Be’er Sheva," not a single tzaddik remained in the city (exactly as in the case of Naomi and Ruth), and the aforementioned Midrash (the source for Rashi’s commentary) holds that Yitzchak was living in Be’er Sheva at that time, which is why it needed to answer that the merit of one is not comparable, etc.—

For in the plain meaning of the text, it is only explicitly stated

## 120

that "[Yitzchak] went up from there to Be’er Sheva," and it is not mentioned that Yitzchak went from Be’er Sheva to Chevron].

B. Seemingly, one could say that according to Rashi’s explanation, there is no room for this question. For Rashi adds to the language of the Midrash at the beginning of his commentary, writing, "it tells us that the departure of a tzaddik from a place leaves an impression." One could explain his intent here as follows: Even though generally the departure of a tzaddik from a city causes the removal of the city’s majesty, radiance, and splendor (which is not merely a matter of making an "impression"), nevertheless, in this specific case, Yaakov’s departure from Be’er Sheva made (only) an "impression," because Yitzchak and Rivkah were there.

However, it is not *glatt* (smooth) to explain Rashi’s intent this way, for he writes, "it tells us that the departure of a tzaddik from a place leaves an impression (and continues in the same breath) for while the tzaddik is in the city, etc." According to the above, it would have been more appropriate to say, "it tells us that Yaakov’s departure made an impression," and then continue with a general statement (regarding tzaddikim in general), "for while the tzaddik is in the city, etc."

This implies that what Rashi wrote, "the departure of a tzaddik from a place leaves an impression," was not said only regarding Yaakov, but also applies when no other tzaddik remains in the city (as with Naomi). [The reason he uses the phrase "leaves an impression" is that the majesty, radiance, and splendor are only a matter of an "impression" relative to the actual existence of the city.]

C. Now, Rashi brought the content of this explanation also in his commentary on Ruth, where he writes: "It tells us that the departure of a tzaddik from a place is noticeable and leaves an impression; its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed, its praise has departed, and similarly, 'And Yaakov departed from Be’er Sheva.'"

It requires study: Why, here in his commentary on the Torah, did Rashi need to repeat, "for while the tzaddik is in the city, he is its majesty, he is its radiance, he is its splendor; once he departs from there, its majesty has departed, etc.," and not write it concisely (as in the commentary on Ruth) that through the departure of a tzaddik from a place, its radiance has departed, etc.?

It seems that there is a substantive difference between Rashi’s commentary in our Parsha and his commentary on Ruth. Here (in our Parsha), Rashi begins with, "for while the tzaddik is in the city, he is its majesty, he is its radiance, he is its splendor" (and then continues, "once he departs from there, its majesty has departed, etc."). This implies that the novelty is in the positive: the tzaddik is the entire majesty, radiance, and splendor of the city (and consequently, through his departure, "its majesty has departed, etc."). In contrast, in Ruth, he wrote only the negative: that through "the departure of a tzaddik... its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed." There, it is not emphasized that the presence of the tzaddik effects a fundamental change in the city such that he *is* the majesty, etc., of the city, but only that his departure causes a lack in the city.

For this reason, Rashi writes there, "is noticeable and leaves an impression," whereas in our Parsha he wrote only "leaves an impression"—because there, the emphasis is not that the tzaddik is the radiance and splendor of the city, but that his departure is merely noticeable and leaves an impression. In our Parsha, however, his intent is to emphasize that the tzaddik’s presence in the city *is* the majesty of the place, etc. According to this, one could say that his intent in the phrase "leaves an impression" is that it is more than "noticeable"; rather, the face of the city has changed entirely (unlike the phrase "leaves an impression" in Rashi on Ruth, which, coming after the word "noticeable," serves only to qualify the strength of the matter).

This requires explanation: What is the reason for the change between his commentary here and his commentary on Ruth?

## 121

D. All of this can be understood by first explaining the definition of the tzaddik’s influence on the city according to Rashi. Here, too, he changed the language of the Midrash. In the Midrash, it says, "for while the tzaddik is in the city, he is its radiance, he is its majesty; once he departs from there, its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed." Rashi adds, "he is its majesty," and furthermore, he places "majesty" (*hod*) before "radiance" (*ziv*) and "splendor" (*hadar*) (and similarly, in Rashi on Ruth before us, the words "its majesty has departed" are absent).

There are many interpretations of these terms. However, since Rashi, who explains for a five-year-old student of Scripture, did not explain and detail these terms, it must be that he holds it is understood from the language of the verses in the Torah itself—in order, from the later to the earlier:

*Hadar* (Splendor): The term *hadar* is explicitly defined in the verse, "And you shall honor (*ve-hadarta*) the face of an elder," which means, simply, a term for honor. Similarly, in our case, "while the tzaddik is in the city... he is its *hadar*," because the honor of a city is that it contains important and precious things. When a tzaddik is in the city, it becomes an honored city, a city where a tzaddik dwells.

*Ziv* (Radiance): *Ziv* is like "the radiance of the sun," which means a term for light, yet it is not the light itself, but rather a radiance and illumination that spreads from it to illuminate the world. Similarly, in our case, "while the tzaddik is in the city... he is its *ziv*," because through his good conduct and good deeds, he radiates light and *ziv* upon all the people of the city, and they learn from his deeds, etc.

*Hod* (Majesty): *Hod* is also a term for light (like *ziv*), as we find regarding "the rays of Moshe’s *hod* [majesty/glory]," and in the language of the verse, "And you shall place some of your *hod* upon him," which is "the radiance of the skin of his face" ("The face of Moshe was like the sun, the face of Yehoshua like the moon"). However, *hod* contains an additional element compared to *ziv*—that "the skin of his face radiated" caused that "they were afraid to approach him." From this, it is understood that *hod* is not merely *ziv*, but a light that causes awe and fear.

[And as is also understood simply: The radiance of the sun and the radiance of the moon do not evoke a feeling of awe and fear; on the contrary, one enjoys their light. Whereas the rays of Moshe’s *hod* caused them to be afraid to approach him.]

Similarly, in our case, "while the tzaddik is in the city, he is its *hod*"—that in addition to the *ziv* he radiates upon the people of the place to influence their conduct, there is also the element of *hod*, radiating such a light that it causes the people of the city to feel *bitul* (self-nullification) and awe.

Admittedly, all this is according to Rashi, who explains according to the simple sense of the matter, that "he is its *hod*... *ziv*...

## 122

*hadar*" of the city refers to his influence and effect on the inhabitants of the city (and therefore Rashi begins with *hod*, as mentioned).

However, according to the Midrash, the fact that the tzaddik affects the city with "radiance and splendor" ("he is its radiance, he is its splendor") does not refer to the effect and change in the conduct of the city’s inhabitants, but to the merit of the tzaddik—as it concludes there, "the merit of one tzaddik is not comparable, etc."—meaning that the tzaddik protects the city, and that through the tzaddik’s merit, there are good influences, blessing, and the like.

E. Based on this, the difference between the words of the Midrash and the words of Rashi is resolved: According to Rashi, the discussion concerns the tzaddik’s influence and effect on the conduct and deeds of the city’s inhabitants. Therefore, there is no need to resolve the fact that Yitzchak and Rivkah remained—for it is explicitly stated in Rashi in the continuation of the verses here regarding Yitzchak, "his eyes had dimmed and he was confined to the house, and he was like a dead person." Since Yitzchak was "confined to the house," he did not come into contact with the people of the city to effect a change in their conduct, to radiate his light and *ziv* upon them, etc. (even though it is understood that his merit protects the city).

[Regarding Rivkah, in addition to the general concept that "all the glory of a princess is within," it can be said that she needed to be in Yitzchak’s presence throughout the entire time, because "his eyes had dimmed," in order to serve and assist him, etc. (which, simply put, Rivkah did).]

This is not the case when discussing the protection of the city or influences of good and blessing. When Yitzchak is in the city, even in a state of being "confined to the house," he influences the city. On the contrary, because he is in the state of "his eyes had dimmed and he was confined to the house, and he was like a dead person, and the *yetzer hara* ceased from him"—"the Holy One, blessed be He, attached His Name to Yitzchak" ("even though we do not find in Scripture that He attached His Name to tzaddikim during their lifetimes to write 'the G-d of so-and-so'")—it is obvious that Yitzchak’s merit is even greater to bring protection and all good influence to the city in which he dwells.

F. Based on all the above, we can explain the difference between Rashi’s commentary in our Parsha and Rashi’s commentary on Ruth:

In his commentary on Ruth, "It tells us that the departure of a tzaddik from a place is noticeable and leaves an impression; its radiance has departed, its majesty has departed, its praise has departed," the intent is primarily on the protection and influence *in* the city (similar to the interpretation of the Midrash here). Therefore, Rashi did not place "its majesty has departed" first there. Conversely, he adds, "(its) praise has departed," emphasizing that the discussion is not (so much) about the effect and influence on the conduct of the city’s inhabitants, but about the "praise" of the city, which departs through the tzaddik’s departure.

In contrast, in our Parsha, Rashi’s intent (as mentioned) is the tzaddik’s influence on the conduct and deeds of the city’s inhabitants. This is why he concludes here, "And similarly, 'And she departed from the place' is stated regarding Naomi and Ruth," and his intent here is that the departure of Naomi together with

## 123

Ruth caused that "its majesty... radiance... splendor departed," for Ruth cleaved to her mother-in-law (even though she was a king’s daughter) and went to convert, and this matter influenced the people of the place. Therefore, through the departure of Naomi with Ruth, "its majesty, radiance, and splendor departed."

[However, in his commentary on Ruth, where the plain meaning of the verse "And she departed from the place" refers to Naomi, one cannot say regarding her "he is its majesty, etc." (in a way that effects awe and *bitul* upon the people of the place), for her departure from Eretz Yisrael together with her husband was not a desirable thing, and it was conduct contrary to the fear of Hashem. Rather, it is still appropriate to say that the merit of her good deeds protected and caused an influence of blessing in the place, in such a way that upon her departure, the radiance, splendor, and praise of the city departed.]

G. The inner dimension of Torah in Rashi’s commentary:
It is known what is written in the *Ohr HaChayim* that the verse "And Yaakov departed from Be’er Sheva and went to Charan" is a hint to the descent of the *neshamah* into the body. "Be’er Sheva" refers to the root of the *neshamah* above, and "Charan" refers to this world ("Charan" from the words "*charon af*—the burning anger of the Place (in the world)"). It is precisely through the descent of the *neshamah* into the body, "and he went to Charan," that it is elevated higher than it was before its descent, according to the rule, "a descent is for the sake of an ascent."

Rashi hints in his commentary on "And Yaakov departed from Be’er Sheva" an additional matter: "It should have only been necessary to write 'And Yaakov went to Charan'; why then does it mention his departure (from Be’er Sheva)?" That is to say: The main novelty in the descent of the *neshamah* is that "he went to Charan," the *neshamah*’s journey to a place of the "burning anger of the Place," where there are concealments and hiddenness regarding the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos*. Yet, despite this, it overcomes all these concealments and hiddenness and engages in Torah and *mitzvos*. Furthermore, it effects the refinement of the *nefesh habahamit* (animal soul) and the body and its portion in the world, making a dwelling place for Him, may He be blessed, in the lower realms. If so, there is room to ask why the text mentions here also his departure from Be’er Sheva (which is not the main trial of the *neshamah*, and consequently it is understood that this is not the reason for its ascent).

To this, Rashi answers: "It tells us that the departure of a tzaddik from a place leaves an impression." One can say the hint here is to the matter of the *neshamah*’s descent below, that the very "departure" from a state of *dveikus* (cleaving) to Hashem (in which it was before its descent below) "leaves an impression" for the good and is a great action.

## 124

For since when the tzaddik is in the city—"the city of our G-d"—"he is its majesty, he is its radiance, he is its splendor," for the entire existence of the *neshamah* is that it enjoys the radiance of the *Shechinah* and possesses love and fear of *Elokus* (Divinity). And through its departure from the "city of our G-d," "its majesty has departed, its radiance has departed, its splendor has departed"—the very departure of the *neshamah* from its root and source ("Be’er Sheva") is *mesiras nefesh* (self-sacrifice), and therefore it "leaves an impression."

Since this is so, it is clear that by virtue of the departure itself, every Jew is worthy that Hashem should return him to his true place, "And I return in peace to my father’s house, and Hashem will be my G-d, etc.," in the true and complete Redemption through Mashiach Tzidkeinu, immediately, literally.

(From the *Sichos* of *Shabbos Parshas Vayetzei* 5725, 5748)


## וישב

## Vayeshev

### 1

"And he told it to his father and to his brothers, and his father rebuked him and said to him, 'What is this dream that you have dreamed? Shall I and your mother and your brothers indeed come to bow down to you to the ground?'" Rashi quotes the verse, "Shall I and your mother," and explains: "But your mother had already died! Yet he did not know that the matter referred to Bilhah, who had raised him as her own mother. Our Sages learned from here that there is no dream without nonsensical elements. Yaakov intended to remove the matter from his sons' hearts so they would not envy him; therefore, he said to him, 'Shall I and your mother...'—just as it is impossible for your mother [to bow down], so too the rest is nonsensical."

According to several commentators on Rashi, Rashi is providing two explanations here: 1) That Yaakov "did not know that the matter referred to Bilhah" (based on *Bereishis Rabbah* on the verse), and 2) The explanation of our Sages that "there is no dream without nonsensical elements" (based on the Gemara).

Seemingly, according to the first explanation, Yaakov thought there was no substance to the dream and it was a false dream, since "your mother" would also be bowing down (and he was mistaken in this, because "he did not know that the matter referred to Bilhah"). Conversely, according to the explanation of our Sages, Yaakov knew the dream was a true dream, and the detail of "your mother" bowing down does not contradict the truth of the dream (even though, according to our Sages, one cannot say the matter refers to Bilhah), because "there is no dream without nonsensical elements," and this detail is one of those "nonsensical elements." As for Rashi’s conclusion, "Yaakov intended to remove the matter from his sons' hearts so they would not envy him; therefore, he said to him, 'Shall I and your mother...'—just as it is impossible for your mother, so too the rest is nonsensical," this is the conclusion of the second explanation: even though he knew the dream was true, he nevertheless said this "to remove it from his sons' hearts."

However, this interpretation of Rashi seems forced. The verse continues, "And his father kept the matter in mind," and Rashi explains, "He was waiting and expecting when it would come." This explicitly indicates that it was clear to Yaakov that it was a true dream and that it would certainly be fulfilled; he was merely waiting and expecting when it would be fulfilled.

### 2

Perhaps it can be said that there are not two separate explanations in Rashi, but rather that all his words are a single, continuous explanation. This is indicated by the precision of his language: "Our Sages learned from here" (he does not write "Another explanation" or "Our Sages expounded," as is his custom when introducing two distinct interpretations). This phrasing does not imply a new interpretation, but rather an additional point. The intent of Rashi’s words is as follows: Since Yaakov did not know that the matter referred to Bilhah, and yet it was clear to him that the dream would be fulfilled (as the verse continues, "And his father kept the matter in mind"), from this our Sages learned that there is no dream without nonsensical elements. This was Yaakov’s opinion: although the general content of the dream would be fulfilled, this specific detail (that his mother would come to bow down before him) was one of the nonsensical elements within it. In truth, however, it was not a nonsensical element, because the matter did indeed refer to Bilhah. Regarding this, Rashi concludes that even though Yaakov knew the general dream would be fulfilled, he nevertheless said, "Shall I and your mother..." in order "to remove the matter from his sons' hearts," as they did not know that "there is no dream without nonsensical elements." Consequently, it was possible to tell them, "Just as it is impossible for your mother, so too the rest is nonsensical."

But according to this, an explanation is needed: since in truth "the matter referred to Bilhah," what then are the "nonsensical elements" in the dream?

### 3

This can be understood by first addressing a question regarding the entire account in the verse: "And his father kept the matter in mind," meaning "he was waiting and expecting when it would come." This implies something dear to him, something he wanted to come and be fulfilled. What is the greatness in his son ruling and his father bowing down to him to the ground? Although the rule that "a person is not jealous of his son or his student" is a simple and understood matter, this only means that a person is not jealous when they attain a higher level than him. There is no place for a father to expect that his son will rule over him, in such a way that all his older brothers and even the father will be his servants and bow down to him to the ground.

Some commentators explain Rashi’s intent based on the Midrash here: "Thus our father Yaakov thought that the resurrection of the dead would occur in his days." That is, his waiting and expecting for the time when his dream would be fulfilled was not because he wanted Yosef to rule and everyone to bow to him—which was actually a great sorrow for Yaakov—but because he thought the resurrection of the dead would occur in his days, and therefore he was waiting for the time when his mother, Rachel, would arise.

However, in addition to the inherent difficulty of interpreting Rashi this way—as there is no hint to the aforementioned matter (from the Midrash) that Yaakov thought the resurrection of the dead would occur in his days—the simple meaning of "kept the matter in mind" refers to the general content of the dream, not to a peripheral matter that would be fulfilled when the time of the dream’s fulfillment arrived (especially when the matter itself has nothing special about it, and only a peripheral detail is what he is waiting for). Furthermore, according to the above (Section 1), Rashi’s intent is the exact opposite: that Yaakov thought this detail of the dream (that his mother would bow to him) was one of the nonsensical elements of the dream.

Based on this, what is the great significance of this dream that Yaakov was waiting and expecting for it to come?

### 4

It can be said that this is resolved by the precision of our Sages' language, who learned from here that "there is no dream without nonsensical elements." Seemingly, according to the above, there was only one thing in the dream that was not true (that his mother would bow to him); they should have said, "There is no dream without a nonsensical element."

It seems that they intentionally said "nonsensical elements" in the plural because Yaakov thought there were several "nonsensical elements" in the dream. Just as the detail that his mother would bow to him was a nonsensical element (since he did not know the matter referred to Bilhah), he also thought that the fact that he himself would come to bow down to him to the ground was one of the nonsensical elements of the dream. The truth of the dream was only that Yosef would rise to greatness until he ruled over all his brothers, but not that he would also rule over his father to the extent that Yaakov would bow down to him to the ground.

It can be said that this was indeed the case: this detail, that Yaakov would bow down to Yosef, was one of the nonsensical elements of the dream, for Yaakov did not bow down to Yosef "to the ground," but only "Yisrael bowed upon the head of the bed."

Based on this, we can also explain the precision of Rashi’s language: "Just as it is impossible for your mother, so too the rest is nonsensical." He did not use the phrasing "it is all nonsensical," because he used language that also hinted at Yaakov’s true opinion. Yaakov held that although the essence of the dream had to be fulfilled, there were several details that were not necessarily going to be fulfilled. Moreover, according to the order of things, there were necessarily details that would not be fulfilled because "there is no dream without nonsensical elements." Therefore, Yaakov used language that would not contain a lie (for it is simple that even though Yaakov wanted "to remove the matter from his sons' hearts," it is impossible that he would utter a lie). This is what he meant by "so too the rest is nonsensical"—that not only this detail of "your mother" is a nonsensical element, but there are other "nonsensical" details in it as well. The brothers, however, understood from his words that the entire dream was nonsensical.

### 5

But we still need to understand (in the inner dimension of the matters) regarding the essence of the dream: that Yosef would rule over the rest of the brothers and they would come to bow down to him to the ground. Seemingly, even though Yaakov loved Yosef more than all his sons and wanted him to rise to greatness—even more than his other sons—what is the greatness in his ruling over his brothers to the point that they would bow to him, and to the extent that Yaakov was waiting and expecting when it would come?

This can be explained based on what Rashi says later regarding the verse, "And he sent him from the valley of Chevron"—"From the deep counsel of that righteous man buried in Chevron, to fulfill what was said to Avraham at the Covenant Between the Parts, that your seed would be strangers." This means that the mission upon which Yaakov sent Yosef was "to fulfill what was said to Avraham at the Covenant Between the Parts." Accordingly, it can be said that the entire narrative of our parsha regarding Yosef and his brothers also contained a "deep counsel" that the Covenant Between the Parts would be fulfilled.

Based on this, it can be said that the same applies to our Rashi: Yaakov’s waiting and expecting for when it would come does not, in its inner dimension, refer to Yosef’s kingship in and of itself (for there is no greatness in his brothers bowing to him), but rather that the decree of the Covenant Between the Parts would be fulfilled through Yosef’s kingship. The decree that "your seed will be strangers in a land not their own" could have been fulfilled in several ways (and as our Sages said, Yaakov our father was worthy of descending to Egypt in iron chains). Rather, Yaakov was waiting and expecting for the fulfillment of Yosef’s dream—that Yosef would rule—so that Yaakov’s descent to Egypt would be in a manner of honor, and that the stay of Yisrael in the land of Egypt for many years would be in a manner of rest, honor, and tranquility, since Yosef was the viceroy, without whom no man would lift his hand or foot, as indeed happened, with the Children of Yisrael dwelling in the land of Goshen, which is the best of the land.

However, this interpretation is also not entirely clear, for the simple meaning of the dream was not about the general kingship of Yosef (over the nations of the world), but only that the sun, the moon, and the eleven stars were bowing down to him—meaning only the bowing of Yaakov and his brothers. Furthermore, the simple meaning of the phrase "he was waiting and expecting when it would come" does not fit well with this, as this language indicates that he was expecting the matter to come soon and quickly. According to the above interpretation, he certainly would not have been expecting it to come soon (why would Yaakov expect the beginning of exile, even if it were in a manner of honor?).
