It is only appropriate to say that he knew it would be fulfilled, but not that "he was waiting and expecting when it would come."

### 6

The explanation for this:
The inner meaning is well known as to why all of Yisrael are called by the name of Yosef, "He leads like the flock of Yosef." This is not only "because he provided for them and sustained them during the days of famine," as the simple meaning suggests, but because he drew down and influenced his own spiritual qualities and traits to each and every one of Yisrael.

Yosef’s uniqueness compared to the other tribes was that the other tribes were shepherds, separated and distinguished from the affairs of the world so that they would not distract them from the service of Hashem. Yosef the Tzaddik, however, was in exile, "captured among the nations." Moreover, he ruled over the entire land of Egypt. This was not merely on the outside or in a superficial sense; he was occupied with all of this—first in the house of Potiphar, "and he appointed him over his house and all that he had he gave into his hand," and afterwards, "and by your word shall all my people be governed," managing in practice all the affairs of Egypt. This is a "great and immense preoccupation." And yet, all these preoccupations did not disconnect him, nor even distract him, from the service of Hashem. Even while occupied with them, he remained in a state of absolute dveikus in Elokus, to the point that he was "a chariot for Elokus."

Furthermore, not only did the preoccupation with worldly matters not cause him any weakening in his dveikus in Elokus, but he acted upon them and upon the affairs of Egypt. As our Sages said regarding the verse, "And Yosef was brought down to Egypt," "He ruled over them... he conquered them," meaning he influenced the affairs of the exile so that they would be in accordance with Yosef’s service of Hashem.

This is why all of Yisrael are called by the name of Yosef, because the power that all of Yisrael have to pass through the exile is drawn and comes from Yosef the Tzaddik.

This is why Yaakov "was waiting and expecting when it would come"—the fulfillment of Yosef’s dream, that Yosef’s brothers would bow down to him to the ground. The act of bowing indicates bitul toward the king to whom one bows. Through this bitul to the king, the king’s influences are drawn down to the people, both material and spiritual influences.

Therefore, Yaakov was waiting and expecting the fulfillment of Yosef’s dream, because it is precisely in this manner that Yisrael have the power to pass through the concealment and hiddenness of the exile of Egypt (and exile in general, as all the kingdoms are called by the name of Egypt), when they are under the kingship of Yosef. This refers to the conduct where, even while in exile—which is a state of darkness, concealment, and hiddenness—they are not distracted or confused from the service of Hashem, remaining whole in their state, in the study of Torah and the performance of mitzvos. On the contrary, they rule and govern over the affairs of the exile.

### 7

But according to this, what Yaakov was expecting is only an elevation and refinement in the state and service of the Children of Yisrael in exile compared to their state otherwise during the time of exile. But ultimately, this is still a state and time of exile, and it is not so plausible to say that Yaakov would be waiting for this, wondering "when it would come."

Therefore, it can be said that Yaakov’s "waiting and expecting when it would come" refers to what every individual of Yisrael is always waiting and expecting—the Geulah, the redemption of the Children of Yisrael from exile. This is emphasized in the continuation of Rashi’s language, "And he also keeps faith," which refers to the fact that the Children of Yisrael wait and expect "the faithfulness of the Holy One, Blessed be He... to redeem them" from exile.

The explanation for this:
It has been explained many times that the general matters explained in this parsha refer not only to Yaakov our father, the tribes, and Yosef as private individuals, but point to the entirety of the Children of Yisrael in all generations. This is not only as all the deeds of the fathers explained in the Book of Righteousness (the Book of Bereishis) are a sign and a source of strength for the children, but the things themselves hint at the entirety of the people of Yisrael.

As hinted in the Midrash in our parsha: "The tribes were occupied with the sale of Yosef, and Yosef was occupied with... Reuven was occupied with... Yaakov was occupied with... and Yehudah was occupied with... and the Holy One, Blessed be He, was occupied with creating the light of King Moshiach." The inner meaning of the Midrash is not that the tribes, Yosef, Reuven, Yaakov, and Yehudah were each occupied with their own private affairs while the Holy One, Blessed be He, was occupied with something else—"creating the light of King Moshiach"—but rather that this is the true content of the occupation of each one of them in particular.

Just as in the continuation of the parsha, in the story of Yehudah and Tamar, the true content is that the Holy One, Blessed be He, was occupied with creating the light of Moshiach, so too it is at the beginning of the parsha, in Yosef’s dream, which was a preface and preparation for all this. Yaakov was "waiting and expecting when it would come"—the kingship of Yosef, meaning Moshiach ben Yosef, who comes as a preface and preparation for Moshiach ben Dovid, as is brought in many places in the words of our Sages.

The content of "waiting and expecting when it would come" indicates the closeness and connection to the thing for which one is waiting. From this itself comes the strength for each and every one of Yisrael throughout the entire duration of the exile to be waiting and expecting when Moshiach Tzidkeinu will come to redeem the Children of Yisrael.

All the more so in the time of the footsteps of Moshiach, when we stand on the threshold of the Geulah. Then is the true state of "waiting and expecting when it would come," for "I will wait for him every day that he should come." And not only every day, waiting for him to come on that day, but every day itself—every hour and every moment, as we say three times a day (on weekdays), "For Your salvation we hope all day."

And immediately and instantly, the "light of Moshiach" is revealed, in such a way that everyone sees it and brings every one of the Children of Yisrael—men, women, and children—to our Holy Land, immediately and instantly.

## Sicha 2

1. "And it came to pass after these things, that the butler of the king of Egypt and his baker offended..." Rashi comments: "After these things — Because that cursed woman [Potiphar’s wife] had accustomed everyone to speak of the Tzaddik, Hashem brought upon them their own transgression so that they would turn their attention to them and not to him [meaning: since all their talk was about what happened to Yosef with his master’s wife, what did Hashem do? He orchestrated the transgression of these men so that they would turn away from discussing him and would no longer recall the matter of Yosef], and furthermore, that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them."

It is implied that Rashi’s intent in his commentary is to explain the connection between this passage (the dreams of the butler and the baker) and the preceding passage (Yosef’s encounter with Potiphar’s wife). As the verse states, "After these things," which indicates a continuation of what was written previously. Rashi provides two reasons for this: a) that it served to nullify the slander Potiphar’s wife had spread about Yosef, and b) that through the butler and the baker, relief would come to the Tzaddik (as recounted in Parshas Mikeitz).

Seemingly, this is puzzling. The sequence of events in these passages is clear and explicit in the simple meaning of the text (at the beginning of Parshas Mikeitz), that it was through the butler that relief came to Yosef the Tzaddik. What compelled Rashi to innovate, within the simple meaning of the text, that the story with the butler and the baker was intended to nullify the slander spread by Potiphar’s wife (especially since this is not mentioned at all in the verse)?!

Some commentators suggest that because of the aforementioned reason, "that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them," it would have been appropriate for this event to occur immediately following the dream; however, since they had already been in prison for twelve months, one must also provide the first reason [to nullify the slander immediately. It can be said that this also relates to their twelve-month stay in prison, because in order to nullify and cause the slander that she had spread "to everyone" to be forgotten, they needed "to turn their attention to them" for a long time—twelve months].

But on the face of it, this explanation does not resolve the main difficulty in Rashi here [in addition to the fact that it is questionable whether this difficulty (why they were in prison for twelve months) is a sufficient reason according to the simple meaning to force a point not mentioned in the text, that the sin of the baker and the butler was intended to nullify the slander, etc.]: Rashi brought the reason "that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them" only as a second reason, as a secondary point to the reason "Because that cursed woman had accustomed, etc." ("and furthermore, that relief would come, etc."). This is highly puzzling, for even if we were to say that there is a necessity in the verses to assume there was an additional intent in the sin of the baker and the butler (to nullify the slander, etc.), why did Rashi set aside the reason explained in the simple meaning of the text ("that relief would come to the Tzaddik, etc.") and bring as the first (and primary) reason, "Because that cursed woman had accustomed, etc."?

2. The explanation for this can be given, by way of introduction:

Generally, "after these things" (*achar hadevarim ha'eleh*) is interpreted as "after these events," with *davar* meaning "matter." But in Rashi, it is evident that in his view, the primary interpretation of "after the words" (*achar hadevarim*) is "after these utterances," with *davar* meaning "speech."

At the beginning of the Parshah of the Akeidah, regarding the verse "And it came to pass after these words," Rashi explains: "Some of our Sages say: after the words of the Satan who was accusing, etc.; and some say: after the words of Yishmael who was boasting, etc." From the precision of his language, "after the words of the Satan... the words of Yishmael," it is understood that he interprets "after the words" as referring to speech [meaning that the act of the Akeidah was a continuation of specific utterances that were not explicitly detailed in the text]. Similarly, after the Akeidah, regarding the verse "And it came to pass after these words, and it was told to Avraham, etc.," Rashi explains: "Upon his return from Mount Moriah, Avraham was contemplating and saying, 'If my son had been slaughtered, etc.' And this is 'after these words'—the ruminations of words that occurred due to the Akeidah." It is explicit in his words that he interprets *devarim* as "speech" [which is why he specifies, "Avraham was contemplating and saying," and it is not enough to say "contemplating" alone].

Based on this, the precision of Rashi’s language in Parshas Lech Lecha (the first time the phrase "after these words" appears in the text) is also explained: "After these words, the word of Hashem came to Avram in a vision, etc., 'Do not fear, etc.'" He writes in his commentary: "After these words — after this miracle was performed for him, that he killed the kings and was worried, saying, 'Perhaps I have received my reward for all my righteousness,' therefore the Almighty said to him, 'Do not fear, Avram, etc.'" Seemingly, it would have been sufficient to say "he was worried," so why does Rashi add "(worried) and saying"? It is because he holds that since it says "after these words," there must have been utterances that preceded this event.

Similarly, in our Parshah above, after Yosef was appointed over Potiphar’s house, "And he left all that he had in Yosef’s hand, etc., and Yosef was of beautiful form and beautiful appearance", it is said : "And it came to pass after these words, that his master’s wife lifted her eyes to Yosef, etc." Rashi comments: "And Yosef was of beautiful form — Since he saw himself as a ruler, he began to eat and drink and curl his hair. The Holy One, Blessed be He, said, 'Your father is mourning, and you are curling your hair? I will incite the bear against you.' Immediately, 'his master’s wife lifted her eyes, etc.' Wherever it says 'after,' it means 'immediately following.'" Here, too, Rashi specifies, "The Holy One, Blessed be He, said, 'Your father is mourning, etc.'"

Simply put, his intent is to explain two matters regarding the reason the text added "after these words" [even though the sequence of events is understood simply, that since "Yosef was of beautiful form and beautiful appearance," therefore "his master’s wife lifted her eyes to Yosef, etc."]: a) The word "after" means "immediately following," teaching us that the story with Potiphar’s wife occurred immediately after he began to eat, drink, and curl his hair—"immediately, 'his master’s wife lifted her eyes, etc.'"; b) From the words "(after) these words," we learn that the fact that "his master’s wife lifted her eyes, etc." came as a continuation of specific utterances: "The Holy One, Blessed be He, said, 'Your father is mourning, etc.'".

3. Based on all this, the difficulty in Rashi here is also simply resolved. Rashi is bothered by the phrase here, "And it came to pass after these words, they offended, etc."—for we do not find any preceding utterances that have any connection to the sin of the butler and the baker? To this, he explains that the intent of the verse in adding "after these words" [even though the connection between the dreams of the butler and the baker and what was recounted previously is understood anyway] is to inform us that there were utterances here that were not explicitly stated in the text: "Because that cursed woman had accustomed everyone to speak of the Tzaddik," and as a continuation of these utterances, "Hashem brought upon them their own transgression so that they would turn their attention to them and not to him."

However, Rashi cannot be satisfied with this alone, for if so, it would imply that this is the *only* reason Hashem orchestrated the story of the butler and the baker. Yet, the verses later explicitly state that the sin of the butler and the baker led to the relief of Yosef. Therefore, Rashi adds, "and furthermore, that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them." Thus, in addition to the connection between these two stories implied by the phrase "after these words" (to which Rashi’s commentary here refers), there was also a (primary) intent "that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them."

Based on this, it is also understood why Rashi brought this as a second reason. It is not because it is secondary (in general) to the first reason, but because Rashi is addressing the words "after these words." In interpreting these specific words, the primary focus is the utterances that preceded them ("these words"), even though regarding the overall sequence of the two stories, the primary connection between them is indeed the second reason—that relief would come, etc.

It can be said that Rashi’s use of "and furthermore" does not mean that this is a secondary matter, but rather that he is explaining the reasons according to their chronological order. First, the story of the sin of the butler and the baker served to nullify the slander, which happened immediately, and only afterward—"and furthermore"—did it lead to the relief of that Tzaddik, after two years, etc.

4. The "inner dimension of Torah" in Rashi’s commentary:

It is known that Yosef’s descent to Egypt was the beginning of the exile of Egypt, which is the root of all exiles, as our Sages say : "All the kingdoms (exiles) are named after Egypt, because they distress (*metzirin*) Yisrael." The redemption of Yisrael is bound up with the nullification of the "distressors" (*metzirin*) and the exile.

Within this itself, there are, in general, two aspects: a) The nullification of the "distressors" so that they cannot distress or harm Yisrael. b) *Atahpacha* (transforming) darkness into light, so that not only do they not distress, but on the contrary, they themselves assist in the redemption of Yisrael.

It can be said that this is the inner content of the two reasons in Rashi’s commentary:

In the first stage, the intent behind the sin of the butler and the baker was to nullify the slander that the cursed woman had spread about Yosef the Tzaddik—the nullification of the "distressor," etc. But the primary intent is in the second stage that follows—"and furthermore"—so that "relief would come to the Tzaddik through them," that through the ministers of Pharaoh themselves, relief and redemption would come to Yosef the Tzaddik, until he became "viceroy."

(From the Sichos of Shabbos Parshas Vayeishev and Shabbos Parshas Mikeitz, 5740)

***

5. Based on the above, one can explain the teaching of R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba in Maseches Chullin regarding the verse "And in the vine were three branches" (in the butler’s dream): "'The vine'—this is Yisrael... 'three branches'—these are the three festivals upon which Yisrael ascend each and every year. 'And as it was budding'—the time has arrived for Yisrael to be fruitful and multiply... 'its blossoms shot forth'—the time has arrived for Yisrael to be redeemed. 'Its clusters ripened into grapes'—the time has arrived for Egypt to drink the cup of reeling... R’ Abba said to R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba: 'When Rav expounds these verses in Aggadah, he expounds them like you'" (and not like the expositions of the Tannaim on this verse brought in the Gemara there previously).

One must note the order of events in R’ Yirmiyah’s exposition. According to his exposition, it turns out that first "the time has arrived for Yisrael to be redeemed," and only afterward "the time has arrived for Egypt to drink the cup of reeling." Seemingly, it should be in the reverse order: first, the power of Egypt must be nullified, and only then can the redemption from Egypt occur?

Also, an explanation is needed: Why would Rav expound according to the teaching of R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba, and not like the expositions of the Tannaim that preceded it?

And even more puzzling: R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba was a student of Rav. If so, what is the meaning of R’ Abba’s statement that Rav "expounds them like you"? Seemingly, he should have said the opposite: that he (R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba) aimed for the opinion of Rav (his teacher) and expounded these verses like him.

6. The explanation for this:

The commentators explain the reason why our Sages expounded the details of the butler’s dream regarding the "status, matters, and events" of Yisrael: "Because it was truly orchestrated through his dream that Yosef was elevated, and this was the beginning of the chain of events leading to the descent of Yisrael to Egypt afterward, and it was all preparation for the redemption that followed. Therefore, the dream hinted at the matter of Yisrael."

The difference between the expositions of the Tannaim and the exposition of R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba is that they expounded the verse regarding various levels of Yisrael themselves [the three Patriarchs, the Matriarchs and the tribes; Torah, Moshe, Aharon, and Miriam, the Sanhedrin and the Tzaddikim of every generation; Yerushalayim, the King’s Sanctuary and the High Priest, the priestly flowers and the libations; Torah, the well, the pillar of cloud and the manna, the first fruits and the libations], whereas R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba "explains the entire matter as the primary subject of the exile of Egypt and its redemption."

Based on this, it can be said that in his exposition, R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba also comes to hint at the elevation achieved through the avodah of Bnei Yisrael in exile, which is not only to nullify the distress of exile, but to cause the distressors themselves to turn into helpers and assistants to Yisrael and their redemption (as mentioned in section 4).

It can be said that these are two stages in the time of the redemption itself, as explained in the Rambam regarding the future redemption. The beginning of the redemption is connected with war, that King Moshiach "will fight the war of Hashem," and initially he will defeat "all the nations surrounding him" —the nullification of the opponent. Afterward, a higher stage comes: "to repair the entire world to serve Hashem together, as it is said, 'For then I will turn to the nations, etc., to call all of them by the name of Hashem, etc.,'" until "there will be no famine, etc., and no war, etc.".

Based on this, it can be said that this is the inner content of "the time has arrived for Egypt to drink the cup of reeling." That it is likened to drinking wine specifically is because it is not merely a matter of punishment; rather, it hints at the *atahpacha* in their very existence, similar to "the intoxicating wine... give strong drink to the lost and wine to the bitter of soul; he shall drink and forget his poverty." Through this "drinking," his poverty is forgotten, and he can be transformed from one extreme to the other. So too in our case, the drinking of the "cup of reeling" hints at the *atahpacha* of Egypt.

This is why he places "the time has arrived for Yisrael to be redeemed" before "the time has arrived for Egypt to drink the cup of reeling"—because first comes the redemption of Yisrael from the exile of the kingdoms (through the nullification of the distressor, etc.); and afterward, they reach the second stage, where the kingdoms themselves are transformed (through "drinking the cup of reeling"). This is similar to the two reasons in Rashi (mentioned above) regarding the act of the butler and the baker: a) nullification of the slander against Yosef, b) that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them—which includes not only release from prison but that he became viceroy.

7. Based on this, one can explain the reason why this exposition was said specifically by R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba (and not by the Tannaim):

It is known that for the Tannaim, the darkness of exile was not in full force, as our Sages say, "And it remained—in the days of Rabbi." Until the days of Rabbi, there were still "remnants" of the revelation of Elokus from the time of the Beis Hamikdash [and it can be said that this is why the Tannaim expounded primarily on the levels of Yisrael, while they were in a state of light and redemption, as mentioned above].

However, in the days of the Amoraim, the primary darkness of exile began. Therefore, specifically R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba expounds the verse regarding the matter of the exile and redemption of Egypt, and on the other hand—specifically in his exposition, the elevation achieved through the descent into exile is emphasized.

It can be said that this is also hinted at in his name—R’ Yirmiyah, from the word *merirus* (bitterness), referring to the bitterness of exile.

This is what the Gemara concludes: "R’ Abba said to R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba: 'When Rav expounds these verses in Aggadah, he expounds them like you.'"

Although "Rav is a Tanna and disagrees", nevertheless, the essence of Rav was that he disseminated Torah specifically in Bavel [which is why he is called "Rav" specifically (and not "Rabbi"), as the saying goes, "Every 'Rav' is from Bavel and every 'Rabbi' is from Eretz Yisrael," because in Bavel, the place of exile, there is a lack in the revelation of Elokus (hinted at by the letter *yud*)]—and therefore, Rav also expounds specifically like R’ Yirmiyah bar Abba.

8. One can connect all this with the Festival of Redemption of the 19th of Kislev (which falls in most years during the week of Parshas Vayeishev):

It is known that the redemption of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe occurred while he was saying the verse in Tehillim, "He has redeemed my soul in peace." It is explained in many places that there are two ways of redemption: (a) through war, where one defeats the enemy; (b) redemption in peace, and in a way of tranquility, because "even his enemies make peace with him", as our Sages say (in explaining the continuation of the verse, "for there were many with me") that even the men of Avshalom prayed for David’s victory.

These are the two matters mentioned above—the nullification of the opponent, and afterward—"that relief would come to the Tzaddik through them."

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Vayeishev, 5726)


## וישב — חנוכה

## 169

### Vayeshev — Chanukah

1. "And the man said, 'They have traveled from here, for I heard them saying, "Let us go to Dothan"'". Rashi explains [following his previous commentary that "a man found him — this is Gavriel"]: "'They have traveled from here' — they distanced themselves from brotherhood; 'Let us go to Dothan' — to seek for you pretexts of laws (*niclei datas*) by which to kill you. And according to its plain meaning, it is a place name, and a verse does not depart from its plain meaning."

Ramban quotes Rashi’s language and writes: "It is not the intention of our Sages that the man explained to him that 'they have traveled from here' means from brotherhood and went to stir up judgments and grievances against you, for if so, he would have been prevented from going and would not have endangered himself. Rather, their intention is that the man, Gavriel, who told him, told the truth, and used language that serves two meanings, both of which are true, and he [Yosef] did not understand the hidden meaning within it and followed its revealed meaning."

However, from Rashi’s commentary, it is implied that according to the *drash*—"they distanced themselves... to seek for you pretexts of laws"—this is the language of Gavriel to Yosef. This is also explicitly stated in Rashi’s commentary on the Gemara : "'They sold him'—as it is written, 'And he sent him from the valley of Chevron, and he came to Shechem, and they were grazing there,' and Dothan is not a place name. And that which is written, 'And the man said, "They have traveled from here,"'—this is what he said to him: 'You say that you are seeking your brothers; travel from this brotherhood, for they do not consider themselves your brothers, for I heard them saying, "Let us go and seek laws and judgments as to how to kill him if he comes to us."'"

And even according to the precision of Rashi’s language in his commentary on the Torah here, where he concludes, "(And according to its plain meaning, it is a place name, and a verse does not depart from its plain meaning)"—from which it is implied that in the study of the *peshat*, the interpretation "they distanced themselves from brotherhood... to seek for you pretexts of laws" does not come to negate that "Dothan" is a place name, but rather that there are two interpretations here—

Nevertheless, it seems to me that, on the face of it, Rashi’s intention here is not as the Ramban says [that the angel said only "They have traveled from here... Let us go to Dothan," but in these words he hinted that "they distanced themselves... to seek for you pretexts of laws," and Yosef "did not understand the hidden meaning within it"]—for from the simple phrasing of Rashi, "They have traveled from here — they distanced themselves...; Let us go to Dothan — to seek for you..." (and he did not write "he hinted to him that they distanced themselves... to seek for you..." or the like), it is implied that these words are part of the plain meaning of the things said to Yosef. And what he adds, "And according to its plain meaning, it is a place name, and a verse does not depart from its plain meaning," means that even though the angel’s intention was "to seek for you pretexts of laws," nevertheless, Yosef’s brothers went to another place (which is named Dothan), but from the precision of the words "they have traveled from here" and the length of the phrasing "for I heard them saying, 'Let us go to Dothan'", it is understood that he also told him about the manner of their going, which is "to seek for you pretexts of laws to kill you..."

And according to this, according to Rashi, Yosef went to seek his brothers even though he knew they were seeking to kill him.

[It should be added that even according to the Ramban, the intention of the verses here is to emphasize that Yosef exerted himself in honoring his father and suffered much for this, as he explained on the verse, "And he sent him from the valley of Chevron": "The verse mentions the place from which he sent him to say that there was a great distance between them, and therefore they did evil to him because they were far from their father, and to tell that Yosef, for the sake of his father’s honor, exerted himself to go after them to a distant place and did not say, 'How shall I go when they hate me?'" And on the verse, "And a man found him, and behold, he was wandering in the field," he wrote: "The verse elaborates on this to tell that many reasons came to him that it would have been appropriate to return, but he suffered everything for the sake of his father’s honor."

But according to Rashi, there is something even more than this: the verse comes to inform us that not only did Yosef "suffer for the sake of his father’s honor" (as the Ramban says), but he went to a place of actual danger in order to fulfill the will of Yaakov.]

2. It may be said that the dispute between Rashi and the Ramban depends on the dispute among the *Rishonim* regarding the law of "let him transgress and not be killed" (*ya'avor v'al yeihareig*):

It is a widespread *halacha* that in all the prohibitions of the Torah, except for *avodah zarah*, *gilui arayos*, and *shefichas damim*, if a person is told to transgress them or be killed, if it is in private, he should transgress and not be killed. And if he wishes to be stringent with himself and be killed, the *Rishonim* differ on the matter: according to the Rambam, "Whoever is told 'transgress and do not be killed' and is killed and does not transgress, he is liable for his own life." But others say that what is meant by "transgress and do not be killed" is that "he has the permission to transgress so as not to be killed," and "many others hold that if he is killed and does not transgress, it is considered a merit for him".

And according to this, it can be said that this is what Rashi and the Ramban differ on in the interpretation of these verses: according to the Ramban’s view, whoever is subject to the law of "transgress and do not be killed," if he is killed and does not transgress, he is liable for his own life ; and Rashi holds that he has the permission to transgress so as not to be killed, but if he is killed and does not transgress, "it is considered a merit for him."

Therefore, in our case, since the mitzvah of honoring parents is not one of the three prohibitions of "be killed and do not transgress" [and furthermore, even during a time of decree where the law is "be killed and do not transgress" even for other mitzvos, some say that "they only said this regarding transgressing negative commandments, but not if they decreed to nullify a positive commandment"], it is obvious that there was no obligation upon Yosef to endanger himself. Therefore, the Ramban explains that the words of the angel were not said explicitly to Yosef, for if it were known to him that there was danger in the matter, it would have been forbidden for him to endanger himself. Whereas according to Rashi, even though he heard explicitly from Gavriel that they were seeking pretexts of laws to kill him, he went and endangered himself to fulfill his father’s mission, because even though from the perspective of the law he could "transgress and not be killed," it is only a permission. And since if he is killed and does not transgress it is considered a merit for him, he went even though there was danger in the matter.

3. And there is still room to discuss this: according to Rashi in the Gemara that the brothers did not travel from Shechem (because Dothan is not a place name), but that when he arrived there he did not find them, the reason is understood why even when Gavriel told him that they had distanced themselves from brotherhood to seek for him pretexts of laws, he continued in the fulfillment of his father’s command: "Are not your brothers grazing in Shechem? Go, I will send you to them... Go now, see the welfare of your brothers...".

But according to Rashi on the Torah that "a verse does not depart from its plain meaning," that "Dothan is a place name" and his brothers had already traveled from Shechem and gone to another place, searching for his brothers in another place would not have been the fulfillment of his father’s mission and command (since Yaakov told him, "Are not your brothers grazing in Shechem?").

Simply, it can be said that the fact that Yaakov mentioned Shechem is only to provide him with a location, and the command was, "Go now, see the welfare of your brothers and the welfare of the flock" (as written in *Ohr HaChaim*), and therefore the fulfillment of Yaakov’s words is when he finds them wherever they may be.

And it requires study regarding this entire journey: after Yosef heard from Gavriel that they had distanced themselves from brotherhood to seek for him pretexts of laws to kill him, by going, he not only placed himself in danger to fulfill his father’s mission, but furthermore, he placed in doubt the very fulfillment of the command, for Yaakov’s mission was not about the journey itself—"Go now, see the welfare of your brothers and the welfare of the flock"—but the main thing is "and bring me back word." And if so, what room is there to go when the command "and bring me back word" will not be fulfilled ?

4. This can be explained according to what the *poskim* write, that even according to the Rambam, who holds that whoever is subject to "transgress and do not be killed" means that it is forbidden to be killed so as not to transgress, nevertheless, "if he is a great man and a pious, G-d-fearing person, and he sees that the generation is lax in this, he is permitted to sanctify the Name and sacrifice himself even for a minor mitzvah so that the people will see and learn, etc.".

And it can be said that the obligation of *mesiras nefesh* in this situation is not for the sake of the fulfillment of the specific mitzvah for which he is sacrificing himself, but to sanctify His Name, may He be blessed, to nullify the laxity of the generation.

And according to this, it can be said (according to Rashi) that Yosef saw in the conduct of his brothers that they were lacking in honoring their father, and not only from the events that had passed [such as Shimon and Levi killing all the men of Shechem, which was against Yaakov’s will, as Yaakov said, "You have troubled me to make me odious to the inhabitants of the land... and I am few in number," and also Reuven, who disturbed his father’s bed], but in his brothers’ hatred for him. For since their hatred for him came because of Yaakov their father’s love for him, as it is written, "And Yisrael loved Yosef more than all his sons... and his brothers saw that their father loved him more than all his brothers, and they hated him," this was an affront to his father’s honor. Therefore, Yosef reasoned that the law applies to this: when the generation is lax in this, he is permitted to sacrifice his life for it, so that they will see that he is prepared to sacrifice his life without any calculation, etc., "and they will learn, etc.".

And therefore, he did not care if the command might not be fulfilled, because the *mesiras nefesh* in this situation is for the sake of the sanctification of the Name, as mentioned above.

5. The words of the *Shelah* are well known (in *Parshas Noach*), that all the festivals of the year have a connection to the *parshios* in which they fall. It is explained there at length the connection of Chanukah to this story in our *parshah*, "the matter of Yosef’s business with his brothers, that he went to see the welfare of his brothers, etc.," and "the matter of Yosef will also be in the kingdom of the Chashmonaim in the days of the Greeks... for then we were considered as sheep for the slaughter to sacrifice our lives to sanctify His Great Name, and these are the sheep of Yosef and Chana and her seven sons, faithful witnesses to the holiness of the Unique Name, etc."

And according to this, it should be added that the manner of Yosef’s *mesiras nefesh* mentioned above (which he was not obligated to do by law, and furthermore, it was beyond all calculation) we also find in Chanukah.

The Rambam writes at the beginning of the Laws of Chanukah: "In the Second Temple, when the Greek kingdom reigned, they issued decrees against Yisrael and nullified their religion and did not allow them to engage in Torah and mitzvos, and they laid their hands on their property and their daughters, and they entered the Sanctuary and breached it and defiled the pure things, and it was very distressing to Yisrael because of them, and they oppressed them with great oppression." And from the plain meaning of his language, it appears that the law of the situation of the days of Chanukah is like a time of decree, as the Rambam writes in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*, that the distinctions between other mitzvos and the three prohibitions, in public or in private, etc., are only when it is not a time of *shemad* (religious persecution). "But in a time of *shemad*, which is when a wicked king like Nevuchadnetzar and his peers stands and issues a decree of *shemad* against Yisrael to nullify their religion or any of the mitzvos, one should be killed and not transgress even for one of the other mitzvos." And according to this, it turns out that at that time, Yisrael were obligated to sacrifice their lives, and especially since, in addition to the decrees on circumcision, Shabbos, and the new month (as brought in many places), they said, "Write on the horn of the ox that you have no share in the G-d of Yisrael."

But there is room for reflection on this. For even though in a time of *shemad* one should be "killed and not transgress" in any case—this is only if they force him to transgress the mitzvos of the Torah, he is obligated to sacrifice his life and not transgress; but on the face of it, one cannot say that there is an obligation to go out to war against the wicked kingdom that issued these decrees.

And furthermore: in our case, the sons of the Chashmonaim were few against many and weak against strong (where according to nature there is no room for them to win), and it is not appropriate to say that they were obligated to do this by law. For even if you say that going to war is not in the category of *mesiras nefesh*, because this is the content of every war, that they place themselves in danger (and if so, in this itself, that the Torah permitted going to war (even a discretionary war), the law of war was removed from the laws of *mesiras nefesh*), nevertheless, in this manner, where there is no room at all in nature for them to win, it is a certain danger, and on the face of it, there is no permission to go out to such a war.

Rather, with the sons of the Chashmonaim, there was *mesiras nefesh* without measure or limit, and consequently, they went with *mesiras nefesh* even in matters where, according to the obligations of the Torah, there is no room for it, as in our case, that they went out to war (to certain danger) against the Greeks [in addition to the actual fulfillment of the positive commandment of circumcision and the new month, according to the opinions that they only said "be killed and do not transgress" regarding transgressing negative commandments, and not if they decreed to nullify a positive commandment, as mentioned above].

6. And according to this, one can explain what we say in the text of "HaNeiros Halalu," that the salvations, miracles, and wonders were "by the hands of Your holy priests," and so the Rambam emphasizes, "Until the G-d of our fathers had mercy on them and saved them from their hand and rescued them, and the sons of the Chashmonaim, the High Priests, grew strong and killed them and saved Yisrael from their hand."

For on the face of it, one should emphasize this: is there a difference in the fact that the salvation was "by the hands of Your holy priests," "the sons of the Chashmonaim, the High Priests"?

And it can be said that this emphasizes that the permission for such *mesiras nefesh* at that time, and even to the extent of going out to open war against the Greeks (not just *mesiras nefesh* to fulfill the mitzvos), is because they were "Your holy priests," "the sons of the Chashmonaim, the High Priests," and their status was like a "great man and pious, G-d-fearing person," who sacrifices his life at any time and in any manner, even when it is not according to the obligations of *mesiras nefesh* according to the Torah.

And for this very reason, salvations, miracles, and wonders were performed for our ancestors, because through the power of the holy and great priests, they reached, through their *mesiras nefesh*, above the definition of nature.

7. And here, this matter fits with what is explained in other places regarding the precision of the language of the text of "Al HaNissim," that the wicked Greek kingdom stood "to make them forget Your Torah and to force them to depart from the laws of Your will," that their main war was against the spirituality (and the *Elokus*) within the Torah and mitzvos. For this is specifically "to make them forget Your Torah," that the Greeks did not care (so much) that Yisrael would learn Torah, but they wanted the study of Torah to be from the perspective of the intellect within the Torah, "for it is your wisdom and your understanding in the eyes of the nations", and thereby "to make them forget Your Torah," to make Yisrael forget, G-d forbid, that the Torah is the Torah of Hashem.

And similarly regarding the mitzvos, that the war of the Greeks was against the *Elokus* within the mitzvos, that the mitzvos are His will, may He be blessed, and this is "to force them to depart from the laws of Your will" (*chukei retzoncha*—specifically *chukim* and specifically *retzoncha*). For there are two matters in this: (a) That regarding the mitzvos of *eidus* (testimonies) and *mishpatim* (judgments), because they are intellectual mitzvos, the Greeks agreed, and their main war was against *chukim* (statutes) that are above reason. (b) In the *chukim* themselves, they would agree if their fulfillment was from the perspective of some intellectual explanation [such as: that there is certainly a reason for the mitzvah as explained in the Rambam, for it is a command of the Holy One, blessed be He, but the reason is very deep, but they rely on the *mitzvah* of the mitzvos that there is a reason and intellect in this, etc.], and their main war was against "(the laws of) Your will," that the fulfillment of the laws should be solely because it is the will of Hashem, "A statute I have enacted, a decree I have issued", and "you have no permission to contemplate it".

And with this, one can understand the language of the Gemara, "When the Greeks entered the Sanctuary, they defiled all the oils that were in the Sanctuary," for the implication of the language is that when they entered the Sanctuary, they strove to defile the oils—for on the face of it, they should have destroyed the oil, and why did they only defile it ? And according to the above, it can be explained that "oil" indicates wisdom, and the Greeks agreed that there should be the matter of wisdom ("oil") among Yisrael, but they wanted this oil to be "defiled oil", that the *Elokus* within the Torah should not be felt in it, that the Torah is "Your Torah," the Torah of Hashem, and that the mitzvos are "the laws of Your will."

And this is the greatness of the miracle of Chanukah, that they found pure oil, and furthermore, that the oil was from a cruse that was sealed "with the seal of the High Priest". For, according to the above (regarding the intention of the Greeks), it is understood that the war against this, and all the more so the victory of this war, came through the *avodah* of *mesiras nefesh* specifically. And in *mesiras nefesh* itself, such a *mesiras nefesh* that is completely above reason and intellect (from one extreme to the other from the opinion of the Greeks that everything must be only according to intellect specifically). And this is hinted at in the "cruse of oil" sealed with the seal of the High Priest—for the High Priest, who is called "Holy of Holies", his matter is *bitul* to the Holy One, blessed be He, to the point of *mesiras nefesh*, completely above reason and intellect. And this is the victory of the days of Chanukah, that they found a cruse of pure oil sealed with the seal of the High Priest, that this *mesiras nefesh* was in the Children of Yisrael (through the Chashmonaim, "Your holy priests"), and through this power they defeated the Greeks.

And the point mentioned above regarding the war of the Chashmonaim against the Greek kingdom—that it was *mesiras nefesh* that was completely above reason and intellect (even more than what is required according to the intellect of the Torah)—aligns with the content of the miracle of the lights, that they found a cruse of pure oil sealed with the seal of the High Priest and lit from it for eight days, and they established these eight days of Chanukah to thank and praise Your Great Name.

(From *Sichos* of *Shabbos Parshas Mikeitz* 5724, *Shabbos Parshas Vayeshev* 5743)


## מקץ

## Sicha 1

**176**

### Sicha 1

A. "Pharaoh sent and called for Yosef, and they rushed him from the dungeon; he shaved and changed his clothes and came to Pharaoh," and Rashi comments: "He shaved — out of respect for the monarchy."

The commentators raise a difficulty: Why does Rashi explain "out of respect for the monarchy" only regarding "he shaved," when "he changed his clothes" was also out of respect for the monarchy? [As is also explicit in the commentary (attributed to) Rashi in Midrash Rabbah (on the Chazal teaching "Pharaoh sent and called for Yosef... to show respect to the monarchy") — "He shaved and changed his clothes to show respect to the monarchy (which is a reflection of the Above, where He shared some of His honor with flesh and blood)."]

It cannot be argued that it is self-evident that changing clothes is out of respect for the monarchy, and therefore Rashi did not need to explain it, as the verse states, "For one may not enter the king’s gate in sackcloth," because, on the face of it, the lack of respect in entering the king’s presence while unkempt and unshaven is also self-evident.

Seemingly, one could answer based on what the commentators say regarding why Rashi is needed at all — for on the face of it, it is simple that before coming to a king, one shaves and changes clothes. However, Rashi is troubled by the following: The verse states, "Pharaoh sent and called for Yosef," which occurred after "his spirit was agitated" by his dreams and there was no one to interpret them for Pharaoh. This means that Yosef’s arrival before Pharaoh was extremely urgent, which is why "they rushed him from the dungeon," with haste and speed. Based on this, it is difficult: How could Yosef delay to shave himself, etc., when it is not proper conduct or etiquette for the king and his servants to sit and wait for him? Furthermore, this would be like rebelling against the monarchy; how did he not fear for his life that the king might kill him for delaying his arrival? Therefore, Rashi explains that he did this "out of respect for the monarchy," and thus he acted correctly, and Pharaoh would not blame him for this.

According to this, one could say that the need to justify Yosef’s conduct applies primarily to "he shaved," which requires time and effort, whereas changing clothes does not take much time.

**178**

Shaving the head—it is obvious that he would trim his hair when necessary, but here it was a special shaving—not out of necessity (the regular shaving of all prisoners from time to time), but a special shaving "out of respect for the monarchy."

In other words: The fact that "one may not enter the king’s gate in sackcloth," when one is wearing clothing unsuitable for entering the king, is a negative matter—that one should not enter the king in a way that is degrading. Similarly, in our case, since he did not want to come to the king in the clothes of a prisoner, he changed his clothes so as not to degrade the king. In contrast, this special shaving before coming to the king was not intended to avoid entering the king in a degrading manner (for he would have trimmed his hair from time to time anyway), but rather he performed a special act "out of respect for the monarchy."

C. By way of *remez*, it can be said that Rashi’s commentary here hints at a matter of *halacha*:

Yosef’s departure from the dungeon was in order to become king over Egypt, as it is written, "From the dungeon he came forth to reign." Regarding the obligations and laws of a king, there are these two matters: (a) Shaving, for "the king gets a haircut every day... Your eyes shall see the king in his beauty," (b) wearing royal garments (as it is brought in the Gemara, "One who shaves his head in a dream, it is a good sign for him," and Rashi comments: "One who shaves—it is a sign of greatness and splendor for him, like 'he shaved and changed, etc.'").

This is the hint in Rashi’s commentary: that what Yosef did "out of respect for the monarchy" was primarily "he shaved," and not that he "changed his clothes"—because his royal garments were not given to him then, but later, when Pharaoh appointed him over his house and over all the land of Egypt. In contrast, the shaving (of royalty) was immediately upon his exit from the dungeon.

D. According to this, we can explain (in the *pnimiyus* of the matters) the "wine of Torah" in Rashi’s commentary, based on the teaching of Chazal that Yosef left the dungeon on Rosh Hashanah:

It is known that Rosh Hashanah is the time of the "building of *Malchus*," meaning that the vitality of all the worlds is from the *middah* of His *Malchus*. For from the perspective of the Holy One, Blessed be He, Himself, there is not even a place for the existence of worlds. In order for the worlds to be brought into being from nothingness, there must be a *Tzimtzum* and concealment of the light and *shefa* of the *Ein Sof, Baruch Hu*, so that there will be no revelation except from the perspective of the *middah* of His *Malchus*, as it is written, "Hashem is King, He has donned majesty,"

**179**

that in order to be King over the world, the Holy One, Blessed be He, donned a garment of majesty so that His Name would be called King, and the light of the *shefa* of the *Ein Sof, Baruch Hu* was concealed and hidden in this garment.

This matter of "Hashem is King, He has donned majesty" occurred for the first time on the sixth day of Creation, the day Adam HaRishon was created, and it is the same every year on Rosh Hashanah, because the general vitality of the previous year is in a state of withdrawal at the beginning of the night of Rosh Hashanah, and through the *avodah* of the Jewish people on Rosh Hashanah, the building of *Malchus* is renewed.

It can be said that this matter is hinted at in the words "he shaved and changed his clothes," and by way of introduction:

It is known that there are two aspects regarding "hair." Sometimes we find in the Torah that there is a great virtue in hair, such as with a *Nazir*, of whom it is said, "He shall be holy, he shall let the hair of his head grow long." At other times, we find that the Torah degrades hair, and it is called secondary to the flesh. Note the teaching of Chazal that "hair in a woman is *ervah* (a source of immodesty)." It is the same regarding the aspect of "hair" Above: the hair of *Atika Kadisha* is the source of vitality for all the worlds, while from the hair of the *Nukvah* (of the *sefirah* of *Malchus*) there is potential for the *chitzonim* to draw sustenance.

It is explained therein that hair is the "excess of the *mochin*," and its virtue is according to the place from which it is drawn. In the aspect of *Atika Kadisha*, which is *Ein Sof*, the excess from there contains great vitality, and the hair is great and wondrous holiness, as it is written, "And the Ancient of Days sat; His garment was white as snow, and the hair of His head like clean wool." Similarly, with a *Nazir* during the days of his *nezirus*, a great illumination shines and is enclothed in his hair; thus, in his hair there is great holiness, and he was warned not to shave and is commanded to let it grow long. Also, for every person, in the hair of the beard and the *pe'os*, an illumination from the holiness of the upper hair shines, and they were warned not to shave them.

In contrast, in the aspect of *Nukvah*, *Malchus*, which is in the aspect of *Tzimtzum*, from the excess of its *mochin* there can be sustenance for the *chitzonim*, because the vitality is in great *Tzimtzum*, "hair in a woman is *ervah*," etc.

Now, even though the source of the vitality of all the worlds is from the aspect of the hair of *Atika Kadisha*, the creation does not occur when this matter shines in revelation. In order for creation to exist, there must be a distinct "he shaved"—the concealment of the revelation of vitality and the drawing down of the *Ein Sof, Baruch Hu* into the worlds in the manner of hair (since in this hair a revelation of great light shines). Through this, it is possible for "he changed his clothes" to follow—"Hashem is King, He has donned majesty," the garment of *Malchus* from which the existence of the worlds was brought about, as mentioned above.

This is hinted at in Rashi’s language, "He shaved—out of respect for the monarchy," that in order for there to be "respect for the monarchy," there must first be "he shaved."

The intention of this is that the Jewish people should effect in the world—which is an aspect of *yesh*—that there be a nullification of the *yesh* to *ayin*, which is through the *avodah* of the Jewish people in *Torah* and *mitzvos*, which draws down the revelation of *Elokus* into the world.

## Sicha 2

## 184

Even regarding the decrees of the Rabbis, and likewise regarding Yaakov, it is written, "And I have kept the six hundred and thirteen mitzvos" — yet concerning the Tribes, it is not mentioned anywhere in Rashi’s commentary on the Torah that they were careful to observe the six hundred and thirteen mitzvos.

On the contrary: Rashi brings in his commentary on the Torah (the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda) that "twin sisters were born with each and every Tribe, and they married them." According to this opinion, they were not careful to observe all six hundred and thirteen mitzvos, for they married their sisters. And even according to the opinion of Rabbi Nechemiah (which Rashi brings as a second interpretation) that the wives of the sons of Yaakov "were Canaanites," and it has been explained elsewhere that one of the reasons Rabbi Nechemiah disagrees with Rabbi Yehuda is because he is concerned for the honor of Yisrael and does not find it appropriate to say that the Tribes were not careful regarding the prohibition of a sister — nevertheless, it is not explicitly mentioned in Rashi that they were careful to observe the entire Torah.

And although logic dictates that since Yaakov observed all six hundred and thirteen mitzvos, as did Avraham, surely he also "commanded his children and his household after him" to keep "the way of Hashem", and the Tribes observed the six hundred and thirteen mitzvos — perhaps it can be said that this was only while they were in the house of Yaakov (in close proximity to him).

In other words: according to the simple meaning, the definition of the fact that the Patriarchs observed the entire Torah before it was given is a stringency that the Patriarchs took upon themselves (as mentioned above), and this does not obligate the Tribes (and their descendants after them) to conduct themselves like them. Therefore, Rashi did not mention this at all (regarding the Tribes).

Based on this, it is resolved simply how Yosef prepared for them this slaughter that was not slaughtered according to law, for Yosef thought that they were not careful regarding meat that was not slaughtered properly. And especially according to Rashi above that Yosef suspected his brothers of eating a limb from a living animal; if so, if he suspected them regarding that which they were commanded, all the more so that they were not careful regarding that which they were not commanded.

6. Now, all of this only answers the question of how the Tribes ate of this slaughter (and how Yosef prepared it for them) — but the phrase "and slaughter a slaughter and prepare" remains difficult.

For we find many times in the Torah regarding the arranging of feasts, and the details of the preparations for the feast are not specified, just as with Lot, "and he made them a feast."


## חנוכה

## 187

### Chanukah

A. In a letter, the Rebbe Rayatz expresses uncertainty regarding the Haftorah of Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh: whether one should recite the verses of Rosh Chodesh (after the Haftorah of Shabbos Chanukah), "due to the concern that one should not skip from one prophet to another."

On the face of it, one must examine the reason for this doubt. Regarding the Haftorah for the first day of Rosh Chodesh that falls on Shabbos, there is a dispute between the Beis Yosef and the Rema. The Beis Yosef writes (in Shulchan Aruch) that "if Rosh Chodesh is two days, Shabbos and Sunday, we recite the Haftorah 'Hashamayim Kisei,' and it is customary to recite afterwards the first and last verse of the Haftorah 'Vayomer Lo Yehonasan,' as a remembrance that tomorrow is also Rosh Chodesh." The Rema writes there that "some say one should not interrupt from one prophet to another, and one should only recite the Haftorah of Rosh Chodesh (and he concludes), and this is the custom."

However, in the annotations of the Rebbe Rashab on the Siddur Torah Or, it is written that his father, the Rebbe Maharash, "would recite after the Haftorah of Shabbos Rosh Chodesh the first and last verse of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh'." This means he followed the opinion of the Beis Yosef, and not the opinion of the Rema, who holds that one should not add these verses because "one should not interrupt from one prophet to another."

Based on this, it is difficult to understand why the Rebbe Rayatz was uncertain regarding the addition of the Rosh Chodesh verses on Shabbos Chanukah, "due to the concern that one should not skip from one prophet to another."

It may be suggested that the doubt is rooted in the words of the Beis Yosef himself, who cited this custom (to add the verses of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh') only regarding Rosh Chodesh that falls on Shabbos and Sunday, and not in other cases where this possibility exists—such as the case at hand, Shabbos Chanukah falling on Rosh Chodesh, or Shabbos Parshas Shekalim and Parshas Hachodesh falling on Rosh Chodesh (or Erev Rosh Chodesh), and so on. From this it is implied that only regarding Rosh Chodesh falling on Shabbos and Sunday do we say that we add the first and last verse of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh' "as a remembrance that tomorrow is also Rosh Chodesh," whereas in the aforementioned examples, we do not add anything to remind [the congregation] that it is Rosh Chodesh (or that tomorrow is Rosh Chodesh).

[For it is seemingly forced to say that the Beis Yosef did not specify regarding all the aforementioned examples that one should add [verses] because he relied on what he wrote regarding Shabbos that is the first day of Rosh Chodesh—that one adds the first and last verse of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh' "as a remembrance that tomorrow is also Rosh Chodesh."]

Rather, the reason requires clarification: what is the distinction between Shabbos that falls on the first day of Rosh Chodesh and the other aforementioned examples?

## 188

B. Some have sought to explain that we only add verses from another Haftorah when there is an opinion that one should recite a different Haftorah, in which case we add the first and last verse of that Haftorah in order to satisfy the other opinion, even though the Halacha does not follow it.

Just as regarding Shabbos that falls on the first day of Rosh Chodesh, there are those who say that one should recite the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh' and not 'Hashamayim Kisei' (the Haftorah of Rosh Chodesh), because the second day of Rosh Chodesh is the primary one. To satisfy this opinion, we add the verses of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh' at the end of 'Hashamayim Kisei.' Similarly, the Beis Yosef writes (in his commentary to the Tur, though he did not bring it in the Shulchan Aruch) that for Rosh Chodesh Elul falling on Shabbos (we recite 'Ani So'ara' and) we add the first and last verse of 'Hashamayim Kisei.' This is because there is an opinion that on Rosh Chodesh Elul falling on Shabbos, one should not recite 'Ani So'ara' but rather 'Hashamayim Kisei' ; therefore, even according to the opinion that one recites 'Ani So'ara,' we add the verses of 'Hashamayim Kisei.'

However, in the aforementioned examples—Shabbos Chanukah or Shabbos Parshas Shekalim and Parshas Hachodesh—where according to all opinions the Haftorah of Rosh Chodesh (or 'Machar Chodesh') is pushed aside by the Haftorah of the day (Chanukah, Shekalim, or Hachodesh), there is no place to add the verses of 'Hashamayim Kisei' (or 'Machar Chodesh').

[They proved this from what is written in the Darkei Moshe regarding Rosh Chodesh Menachem Av falling on Shabbos, where there is a dispute whether one recites 'Hashamayim Kisei' or 'Shim'u.' The Darkei Moshe writes that one should recite 'Shim'u' and add the first and last verse of 'Hashamayim Kisei', "and with this we satisfy everyone." Thus, we add verses from another Haftorah to satisfy another opinion, etc.]

But on the face of it, it is a great stretch to interpret the words of the Beis Yosef this way, as he explained the reason for the custom to add the first and last verse of 'Machar Chodesh' on Shabbos that falls on the first day of Rosh Chodesh as being "as a remembrance that tomorrow is also Rosh Chodesh," and he did not mention at all that this is to be concerned for the opinion that one recites the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh' in such a configuration [especially since in the Shulchan Aruch he did not mention this opinion at all].

C. It may be suggested—prefaced by further clarification of the view of the Beis Yosef (mentioned above) that "it is customary to recite afterwards the first and last verse of the Haftorah 'Vayomer Lo Yehonasan,'" and he was not concerned with the rule that "one should not skip from one prophet to another":

In the Beis Yosef (on the Tur), he brings that the source of these words is the Terumas Hadeshen, who resolves the custom (which was also brought as Halacha in the Shulchan Aruch) that on a Shabbos where there is a chassan, one recites after the Haftorah of the week (two or three verses from) the Haftorah 'Sos Asis,' and we are not concerned about skipping from one prophet to another. The Terumas Hadeshen explains several reasons why one can say that in the present time we are not concerned with the rule that one should not skip from one prophet to another. But

## 190

on Erev Rosh Chodesh one recites after the Haftorah of the month the first and last verse of the Haftorah of Shabbos Rosh Chodesh or 'Machar Chodesh'."

However, on the other hand, even in this list, he did not explicitly instruct how to conduct oneself on Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh, nor regarding Parshas Shekalim that falls on Rosh Chodesh or Erev Rosh Chodesh.

Now, from this instruction, it is explicit that it is not like the aforementioned opinion (Section B)—that the reason for adding verses is to satisfy the other opinion, etc.—for in Parshas Hachodesh falling on Rosh Chodesh (or Erev Rosh Chodesh) there is no opinion to push aside the Haftorah of Parshas Hachodesh, and yet we add [verses] as mentioned.

But according to the aforementioned logic (Section C), that one might say we only add verses when both Haftorahs are of the same theme, then it is impossible to prove from the instruction of the Rebbe Rayatz regarding Parshas Hachodesh to the Haftorah of Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh, or to Parshas Shekalim—because even the addition of the verses of 'Hashamayim Kisei' or 'Machar Chodesh' in the Haftorah of Parshas Hachodesh is considered the same theme, since the Haftorah of Parshas Hachodesh is also about the subject of Rosh Chodesh. This is not the case in the matter of Shabbos Chanukah or Parshas Shekalim, where the addition of the verses of 'Hashamayim Kisei' (or 'Machar Chodesh') is a new subject relative to the Haftorah of the day; here there is room to say that we are concerned about "skipping from one prophet to another."

But on the face of it, one can learn from the aforementioned list that we add verses from another prophet even if it is not the same theme, and this is from what is written there regarding Shabbos Parshas Re'eh that falls on Erev Rosh Chodesh Elul, "we recite 'Ani So'ara' and add the first and last verse of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh'." Here it is explicit that we add the verses of 'Machar Chodesh' even when it is not the same theme as the Haftorah of the day.

And since this is so, it is understood that it is the same for Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh, and likewise for Shabbos Parshas Shekalim that falls on Rosh Chodesh (or Erev Rosh Chodesh), that we add the verses of the Haftorah of Rosh Chodesh (or 'Machar Chodesh'). And similarly for us, who recite 'Shim'u' on Rosh Chodesh Menachem Av—we should add the verses of 'Hashamayim Kisei'.

We must say that even though initially the Rebbe Rayatz was uncertain whether to add verses from another prophet when it is not the same theme, later, in the year 5701, he decided that one should add even in such a manner.

E. Now, in the aforementioned letter of the Rebbe Rayatz, only the addition of the Rosh Chodesh verses is mentioned, and there is still room to examine regarding the addition of the verses of the Haftorah 'Machar Chodesh,' for when Rosh Chodesh Teves falls on Shabbos Chanukah, it is the first day of Rosh Chodesh, and if so, just as one should add the verses of Rosh Chodesh, so too one should add the verses of 'Machar Chodesh'.

[And in the aforementioned list of Haftorahs, it is impossible to clarify, for it is not mentioned there how to conduct oneself on Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh, and the question can only exist on Shabbos Chanukah that falls on Rosh Chodesh, for Parshas Hachodesh—Rosh Chodesh Nissan is always one day, and regarding Rosh Chodesh Elul, for us, we recite the Haftorah 'Hashamayim Kisei' and there is only one addition (of 'Machar Chodesh')].

And on the face of it, it seems to me that there is room to distinguish between adding two verses or four verses, that only two verses should be added, and not four verses

## 191

(and especially—from two different prophets). There is some support for this from what the Beis Yosef wrote (on the Tur) regarding Shabbos Rosh Chodesh Elul (as mentioned in Section B), that one recites 'Ani So'ara' "and after they finish the Haftorah, they recite the first verse of the Haftorah 'Hashamayim Kisei' and the verse 'And it shall be from month to month' as a remembrance that today is Rosh Chodesh." Now, in the Beis Yosef there itself, in the continuation of his words, he brings that whenever Rosh Chodesh Elul falls on Shabbos it is the first day of Rosh Chodesh, and if so, seemingly one should add also the verses of 'Machar Chodesh' (like every Shabbos that falls on the first day of Rosh Chodesh), yet the Beis Yosef did not mention the addition of the verses of 'Machar Chodesh.' It is implied somewhat that he holds we do not add them.

Regarding the reason for this, it may be suggested—prefaced by the logic of the Rav Manoach (mentioned in Section C)—that since "reciting by heart is not considered skipping," this was brought in the Beis Yosef (Laws of Rosh Chodesh) also regarding the addition of the verses of 'Machar Chodesh' (on Shabbos Rosh Chodesh). And on the face of it, this requires explanation, for according to this, one must say that this addition (of the verses of 'Machar Chodesh') is recited by heart specifically.

It may be suggested that the intention is not that one must recite the verses of 'Machar Chodesh' by heart specifically, but rather that the matter of adding the verses of 'Machar Chodesh' is akin to the recitation of the verses of 'Sos Asis' by heart. The matter of reciting verses by heart indicates that it is not a category of a fixed reading and the law of reciting a Haftorah does not apply to it. Based on this, it may be suggested that any addition of only two verses is not in the category of a fixed reading to which the prohibition of skipping would apply, because a reading is only significant when it contains three verses, as we find regarding the reading of the Torah that every Aliyah must have at least a reading of three verses (and this number also applies to the Haftorah, for the reason that a Haftorah must be twenty-one verses is because it corresponds to the reading of the Torah which has seven Aliyos, each with at least three verses).

And if this is correct, it is difficult to understand in our case whether it is possible to add both the verses of 'Hashamayim Kisei' and the verses of 'Machar Chodesh'—four verses.

But it seems that even though there is room for the aforementioned doubt, in practice one should add also the verses of 'Machar Chodesh,' and this is based on what the Rebbe Rayatz wrote in the aforementioned letter, that even though he was uncertain about adding the verses of Rosh Chodesh on Shabbos Chanukah, nevertheless he would be accustomed to mention the verses of Rosh Chodesh, "and to consider this an interruption between the recitation of the blessings and the recitation of the verses that are not of the theme of the Haftorah—there is no concern in this, for when one wishes to recite certain verses by heart, this does not invalidate the blessings after the Haftorah." And if so, similarly it may be said in our case, that even if you say there is room for doubt in the matter, one should add also the verses of 'Machar Chodesh.'

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Mikeitz 5729)


## ויגש

## Sicha 1

1. Regarding the law of praying in a whisper, the Zohar in our Parsha states: "A person should not need to make his voice heard in his prayer, but rather pray in a whisper, in that voice which is not heard. This is the prayer that is always accepted. Your sign is 'And the voice is heard' — *kol* (voice) without a *vav* is *nishma* (heard). This is the prayer that is in secret, as it is written regarding Chana, 'And her voice was not heard.' This is the prayer that the Holy One, Blessed be He, accepts, when it is performed with desire, intent, and proper preparation, and to unify the unity of its Master, as is fitting, every day."

Commentators have noted that the words of the Zohar imply that the law of "her voice was not heard" means not only that he should not make his voice heard to others, but "even he himself should not hear his own voice." This is also implied in the Zohar, Parshas Vayakhel: "And if that prayer is heard by the ears of a person, there is no one above who listens to it... Furthermore, the matter of prayer is attached to the supernal world, and the matter of the supernal world does not need to be heard."

However, the Halacha is ruled in the Rambam (and similarly in the Shulchan Aruch): "He makes it heard to his ears in a whisper, and he does not make his voice heard." The Tur writes: "Some say that the statement that he should not make his voice heard in his prayer means that it must be in a whisper, to the point that it is not heard even to his own ears, and they bring proof from the Tosefta: 'Might one think he should make it heard to his ears? It is already explained regarding Chana, "And her voice was not heard."' However, in our Gemara, it excludes only making his voice heard, which implies that he makes his voice heard to others, but to his own ears he may make it heard. This is explicitly stated in the Yerushalmi: 'Might one think he should raise his voice in his prayer? It is already explained regarding Chana, "And her voice was not heard."'"

2. Now, although everyone agrees that it is forbidden to make one's voice heard to others, the Yerushalmi states: "When Rabbi Yonah would pray in the synagogue, he would pray in a whisper, and when he would pray in his house, he would pray with a voice, until the members of his household learned his prayer from him."

The Tur learns from this Yerushalmi that "if he makes his voice heard... so that the members of his household will learn from him, it is permitted." Commentators explain that according to the Tur, "until" is used in the sense of "in order that" (so that his household members will learn the prayer). However, the Beis Yosef explains that "he would raise his voice until it happened by chance that his household members learned from him, even though it is taught that one who raises his voice in his prayer is among the prophets of falsehood; it was stated regarding this that if he cannot concentrate in a whisper, it is permitted, etc."

Thus, there is a dispute regarding the reason for the permission to make one's voice heard to others. According to the Tur, it is permitted to raise one's voice even just so that his household members will learn to pray, whereas according to the Beis Yosef, the permission is only when, by raising his voice, he is able to concentrate as required.

3. It seems this can be explained by first noting that the source for this law is learned in the Gemara from what is written regarding Chana — (as mentioned above) "And her voice was not heard" — "Might one think he should make his voice heard in his prayer? It is already explained by Chana, as it is said, 'And her voice was not heard.'" And further on in the Gemara: "How many great laws are there to be learned from these verses of Chana... 'And her voice was not heard' — from here that it is forbidden to raise one's voice in his prayer."

We find several versions of this: In the Gemara before us, the version is (as above) "And her voice was not heard — from here that it is forbidden to raise one's voice in his prayer." In the Chiddushei HaRashba, the version is "From here for one who prays that he should not make his voice heard in his prayer" (and similarly in the Rosh and the Tur). In the Rif, it is "From here for one who prays that his prayer needs to be in a whisper." It requires understanding the reason for these differences in versions.

It may be suggested:
Regarding the definition of making a voice heard in prayer, there are two general laws: the law prohibiting the making of a voice heard in one's prayer (similar to what is said in the Baraisa above, "One who makes his voice heard in his prayer is among the small of faith," "One who raises his voice in his prayer is among the prophets of falsehood"), and the law of the mitzvah of prayer in a whisper, which stems from the very definition of prayer. Regarding this latter law itself, one could suggest two ways: a) that the law is due to the definition of the speech of prayer, that prayer must be in a whisper; b) that it is a law regarding the concentration of prayer, that because the essence of prayer is "service of the heart," the prayer must be in a whisper.

It can be said that the three aforementioned ways are highlighted by the differences in the versions of the learning from Chana's prayer: According to the version before us, "from here that it is forbidden to raise one's voice in his prayer," the learning is (as the simple meaning of the words) the prohibition against raising one's voice, which is like a secondary prohibition. According to the version of the Rashba and the Rosh, "from here for one who prays that he should not make his voice heard in his prayer," this touches upon the very essence of the prayer, but in connection to the speech of prayer, that the manner of speech must be such that he does not make his voice heard. Whereas according to the version of the Rif, "that his prayer needs to be in a whisper," which is written in the positive, this is because prayer in a whisper is of the very essence of the *cheftza* (object/entity) of prayer, which is service of the heart.

It may be said that the Rambam and the Shulchan Aruch also differ on this. The Rambam writes: "He should not raise his voice in his prayer, and he should not pray in his heart, but rather he articulates the words with his lips and makes them heard to his ears in a whisper, and he does not make his voice heard, unless he is ill or cannot concentrate his heart until he makes his voice heard, then it is permitted." In the Shulchan Aruch, the Mechaber writes: "And he should not pray in his heart alone, but rather he articulates the words with his lips and makes them heard to his ears in a whisper, and he does not make his voice heard, and if he cannot concentrate in a whisper, it is permitted to raise his voice, etc."

Although in general the language of the Shulchan Aruch is like that of the Rambam, there is a fundamental change in the order of things. The Rambam begins with "He should not raise his voice in his prayer" and afterwards continues with "and he should not pray in his heart, but rather he articulates the words, etc." Whereas the Shulchan Aruch begins with "And he should not pray in his heart alone, but rather he articulates the words with his lips and makes them heard to his ears in a whisper, and he does not make his voice heard." Since the essence of the definition of prayer is service (concentration) of the heart, there might have been room to think he should pray in his heart alone, and therefore it begins with "he should not pray in his heart alone, but rather..." and therefore, the fact that he makes it heard to his ears in a whisper is also because prayer is in the heart.

4. Explaining this:
It is known that regarding prayer there are two aspects: a) requesting one's needs, as the simple meaning suggests; b) service of the heart, as they learned from "And to serve Him with all your heart," "What is service that is in the heart? This is prayer."

It can be said that the aforementioned ways (in section 3) regarding the definition of the law of making a voice heard depend on what the essential matter of prayer is:
If you say that the essential matter of prayer is requesting one's needs, then prayer is primarily a mitzvah of speech, like a person requesting his needs from a king, whose request is through speech.
According to this, there is room to say that the speech of prayer must specifically be in a voice heard by others, because that is the definition of speech, for the sake of the other.
